PA1 Walk me through the end-to-end architecture for an AI-enabled GCP onboarding platform.
ArchitectureSenior
▼
Structure: I design it in 4 layers. (1) Infrastructure — GCP project hierarchy with Shared VPC, org policies, Cloud Run as primary compute. (2) Integration — Apigee/API Gateway for API-led patterns, Pub/Sub for event-driven async flows. (3) AI — Vertex AI Agent Builder for orchestration, Vertex AI Vector Search for RAG, Gemini via Vertex endpoints. (4) Governance — GitHub Actions + Terraform for IaC, OPA policy gates, Cloud Audit Logs feeding SIEM.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Reference your Scotiabank GCP architecture — VPC design, Cloud Run, IAM — as the infrastructure foundation you would extend with AI components.
PA2 How would you design the GCP network topology for this platform, including security boundaries?
ArchitectureSenior
▼
I would use a hub-and-spoke Shared VPC model: a host project for networking with service projects for platform components. Security boundaries: Private Google Access for all GCP API calls, VPC Service Controls perimeter around Vertex AI and BigQuery. Ingress: Cloud Load Balancing → Cloud Armor WAF → API Gateway → Cloud Run (VPC-native). Egress: Cloud NAT for controlled outbound, no public IPs on workloads.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank VPC architecture with Istio service mesh and network segmentation is directly applicable here.
PA3 How do you design canonical data models and validation frameworks for platform workflows?
ArchitectureSenior
▼
Design principles: (1) Define a core entity schema (OnboardingRequest, PlatformConfig, ValidationResult) in JSON Schema or Protobuf. (2) Use schema registries for versioning. (3) Implement validation at ingestion — Cloud Run services validate payloads before processing. (4) Event-driven: valid events go to Pub/Sub, invalid ones to a dead-letter topic with alerting.

For AI workflows: canonical models define the retrieval context shape — what fields feed into the RAG prompt — ensuring consistency across agents.
PA4 What integration patterns would you use — event-driven vs API-led?
ArchitectureIntermediate
▼
API-led for synchronous operations with SLAs: onboarding status queries, config reads, user-facing responses. Use REST/gRPC via Apigee for mediation, auth, and rate limiting.

Event-driven for async workflows: provisioning triggers, AI processing jobs, audit event streaming. Pub/Sub with exactly-once delivery or Eventarc for Cloud Run triggers.

Decision rule: If the consumer needs a response in under 2 seconds → API-led. If the operation takes more than 5 seconds or has multiple downstream effects → event-driven. Onboarding pipelines typically combine both: API receives the request, emits an event, async processing happens, webhook/SSE notifies on completion.
PA5 How would you design for production readiness — what does your checklist look like?
ArchitectureSenior
▼
My production readiness framework has 5 pillars: (1) Reliability — SLOs defined, health checks, circuit breakers, retry budgets, chaos testing plan. (2) Security — RBAC finalized, secrets in Secret Manager, VPC Service Controls active, pen test scheduled. (3) Observability — structured logs to Cloud Logging, custom metrics in Cloud Monitoring, distributed traces via Cloud Trace, runbooks for all alerts. (4) IaC completeness — 100% Terraform-managed, no manual resources, state in GCS with locking. (5) Governance — architecture docs in ADR format, DR tested, runbook approved by security and compliance.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: You drove PCI-DSS compliance at Scotiabank — your audit readiness experience maps perfectly here.
PA6 How would you design observability for an AI-enabled GCP platform?
ArchitectureSenior
▼
Three pillars plus AI-specific: (1) Logs — structured JSON logs from all Cloud Run services to Cloud Logging, log-based metrics for error rates and latency. (2) Metrics — Cloud Monitoring custom metrics for business KPIs (onboarding completion rate, AI response time), SLOs defined and tracked. (3) Traces — Cloud Trace for distributed tracing across Cloud Run services and Pub/Sub.

AI-specific: (4) LLM metrics — token usage, latency per model call, retrieval quality scores logged to BigQuery. (5) Agent observability — trace agent reasoning steps and tool calls. (6) Drift detection — monitor for distribution shifts in user queries that might indicate the knowledge base is stale.
PA7 How would you design a developer portal onboarding flow using AI — end-to-end architecture?
ArchitectureSenior
▼
User journey: Developer submits request via portal UI → AI validates → infrastructure provisioned → developer gets access.

Architecture: (1) Frontend — Cloud Run web app. (2) API layer — Apigee with OAuth2. (3) Orchestration — Vertex AI Agent Builder orchestrator calls sub-agents: validation agent checks completeness, knowledge agent RAG-answers from docs, provisioning agent calls Terraform/GCP APIs. (4) Async processing — long-running provisioning via Pub/Sub and Cloud Tasks, status updates via SSE or webhooks. (5) Notifications — Cloud Run notification service to email/Slack. (6) Audit — every agent action and provisioning step logged to Cloud Audit Logs and BigQuery.
PA8 How would you approach architecture documentation to enterprise governance standards?
ArchitectureIntermediate
▼
I use Architecture Decision Records (ADRs) + C4 diagrams as the core artifacts.

ADR structure: Context → Decision → Status → Consequences. Every significant architectural choice is an ADR in Git — version-controlled, reviewable, findable.

C4 model: (1) System Context diagram. (2) Container diagram — Cloud Run services, databases, message queues, AI endpoints. (3) Component diagram for complex components such as the AI orchestration layer. (4) Code diagram only for critical algorithmic components.

Living documentation: Diagrams as code (Mermaid, Structurizr) stored in Git alongside the Terraform code. PR-required updates when architecture changes — documentation drift is treated as a defect.
PA9 Explain how you would design a RAG pipeline on GCP for this platform.
AI/Vertex AISenior
▼
A production RAG pipeline has these stages: (1) Ingestion — documents uploaded to GCS, Cloud Functions trigger processing. (2) Chunking and Embedding — chunk documents (semantic chunking ~512 tokens), call Vertex AI text-embedding-004 for embeddings. (3) Vector Storage — store in Vertex AI Vector Search with metadata. (4) Retrieval — embed the query, retrieve top-K chunks using hybrid search (semantic + BM25 keyword), apply re-ranking. (5) Generation — construct a prompt (system context + retrieved chunks + user query), send to Gemini via Vertex AI. (6) Evaluation — use Vertex AI Evaluation Service to measure faithfulness, relevance, and groundedness.
PA10 What are the key design decisions in a RAG architecture — chunking, vector DB, retrieval?
AI/Vertex AISenior
▼
Chunking: Fixed-size chunking is simple but breaks context. Semantic chunking (split on sentence/paragraph boundaries) is better for enterprise docs. Sliding window with ~10-15% overlap reduces context loss. Parent-child chunking stores large chunks for context but retrieves small chunks for precision.

Vector DB on GCP: Vertex AI Vector Search for fully managed at scale; Cloud SQL with pgvector for simpler cases; AlloyDB AI for SQL plus vectors together.

Retrieval: Pure semantic search misses keyword-heavy queries. Hybrid retrieval (semantic + BM25) with Reciprocal Rank Fusion for merging is the production standard. Add a re-ranking step with Vertex AI Reranker for quality uplift.
PA11 How would you design an agentic workflow for this platform?
AI/Vertex AISenior
▼
Architecture: An orchestrator agent receives the onboarding request and routes to specialized sub-agents: (1) Validation agent — checks completeness, runs compliance rules. (2) Knowledge agent — RAG-powered, answers config questions, fetches runbooks. (3) Provisioning agent — calls Terraform APIs or GCP APIs to create resources. (4) Notification agent — sends status updates via email/Slack.

Tooling: Vertex AI Agent Builder (managed) or LangGraph (open source, more control). Use ReAct pattern (Reason + Act) for each agent. Include human-in-the-loop gates for destructive actions. Instrument with Cloud Trace for observability of agent chains.
PA12 What is Vertex AI Agent Builder and how does it differ from LangChain/LangGraph?
AI/Vertex AIIntermediate
▼
Vertex AI Agent Builder is Google's managed platform for building production AI agents. It provides: managed reasoning engine (based on LangChain/LangGraph under the hood), built-in tool integration (Google Search, code interpreter, custom APIs), integration with Vertex AI Search for RAG, and managed deployment with autoscaling.

Key differences vs raw LangChain/LangGraph: Agent Builder is fully managed with GCP IAM and audit logging built in, but less flexible for custom orchestration logic. LangGraph gives full control over state machines and agent topology but requires your own deployment on Cloud Run. For enterprise platforms, Agent Builder reduces operational burden; LangGraph suits teams needing custom control flow.
PA13 How would you handle hallucination risks in a production LLM system?
AI/Vertex AISenior
▼
Architectural controls: (1) Grounding — always RAG-ground responses with retrieved sources. Use Vertex AI Grounding with Google Search or enterprise data. (2) Citation enforcement — prompt the model to cite sources; post-process to validate citations match retrieved chunks. (3) Confidence scoring — return low-confidence responses to a human review queue. (4) Output validation — schema validation for structured outputs, rule checks for critical fields. (5) Evaluation pipeline — continuous evaluation using Vertex AI Evaluation Service measuring faithfulness and groundedness. (6) Human-in-the-loop for high-risk actions such as provisioning or policy changes.
PA14 What is the difference between fine-tuning and RAG, and when would you recommend each?
AI/Vertex AIIntermediate
▼
RAG: The model stays unchanged. At query time, relevant context is retrieved from an external knowledge base and injected into the prompt. The model reasons over retrieved context.

Fine-tuning: Model weights are updated using domain-specific training data. The knowledge is baked into the model.

Use RAG when: Knowledge changes frequently (policies, docs, product specs), large proprietary knowledge bases, need for source citations, regulated environments, faster iteration. This is the default recommendation for enterprise platforms.

Use fine-tuning when: Consistent style/tone requirements, specialized vocabulary the base model does not know, task-specific behavior (classify, extract), or when RAG latency is too high.
PA15 How does Vertex AI Vector Search work and when would you choose it over pgvector?
AI/Vertex AIIntermediate
▼
Vertex AI Vector Search (formerly Matching Engine) is a fully managed approximate nearest neighbor (ANN) service using the ScaNN algorithm. It supports billions of vectors and scales horizontally with millisecond query latency.

Choose Vertex AI Vector Search when: Vector dataset exceeds 10M vectors, latency SLAs are under 50ms, you want managed operations with no infra burden.

Choose pgvector when: You already have Cloud SQL or AlloyDB PostgreSQL, smaller vector datasets under 5M, you need to join vectors with relational data in the same query, or you want to simplify your stack.

Choose AlloyDB AI: Combines pgvector with AlloyDB performance — a good middle ground for enterprise use cases needing both relational and vector capabilities at moderate scale.
PA16 How would you structure Terraform at an enterprise governance level for a GCP platform?
IaC & GitOpsSenior
▼
Repository structure: Monorepo with module registry or separate module repos plus root config repos. Layers: (1) Foundation (org, billing, folder structure), (2) Platform (shared VPC, shared services), (3) Workloads (per-service modules).

Governance controls: All state in GCS with versioning and object locking. Policy-as-code via OPA/Conftest or Terraform Sentinel validating every plan. PR-based workflow: terraform plan output posted to PR, required approvals before apply. Module versioning with semantic tags — teams consume pinned versions, not HEAD.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Terraform modules for VPC, GKE, and service onboarding at Scotiabank is a strong anchor story here.
PA17 Walk me through a GitHub Actions CI/CD pipeline for Terraform on GCP.
IaC & GitOpsSenior
▼
Auth: OIDC-based Workload Identity Federation — no long-lived service account keys. GitHub Actions gets a short-lived GCP token by presenting the OIDC token from GitHub.

Pipeline stages: (1) PR open → terraform fmt check + terraform validate + tfsec security scan + OPA policy check. (2) Plan output posted as PR comment with cost estimate via Infracost. (3) Required reviewers approve plan. (4) Merge to main → terraform apply with state lock. (5) Post-apply drift detection on schedule.

Environment protection: GitHub Environments for staging and prod with required reviewers and deployment branch rules. Prod applies need 2 approvals and a passing security scan.
PA18 What is GitOps and how does it differ from traditional CI/CD? How would you implement it on GCP?
IaC & GitOpsIntermediate
▼
GitOps principle: Git is the single source of truth for both application code and infrastructure state. Desired state lives in Git; an agent continuously reconciles actual state to match.

Vs traditional CI/CD: Traditional CI/CD is push-based (pipeline pushes deployments). GitOps is pull-based (agent in cluster pulls desired state from Git). Benefits: audit trail in Git, easier rollback via git revert, drift detection built-in.

GCP implementation: Config Sync (GKE add-on) or Argo-CD on GKE for Kubernetes workloads. For Cloud Run, GitHub Actions applies Terraform changes declaratively — effectively GitOps for infrastructure.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Argo-CD GitOps implementation at Scotiabank is directly relevant here.
PA19 How do you handle secrets management in a GCP platform?
IaC & GitOpsSenior
▼
GCP Secret Manager as the primary secrets store — versioned, IAM-controlled, audit-logged. Secrets accessed at runtime via Secret Manager API or mounted via the Secret Manager add-on for GKE and Cloud Run.

Never: Secrets in hardcoded environment variables in Terraform, secrets in container images, secrets in Git.

Rotation: Secret Manager supports automatic rotation via Cloud Functions trigger. Database passwords and API keys are on a rotation schedule.

CI/CD: GitHub Actions uses Workload Identity Federation — no stored secrets at all. Terraform state encryption at rest uses a CMEK KMS key.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your CMEK envelope encryption work at Scotiabank under PCI-DSS demonstrates this depth well.
PA20 How would you design RBAC and identity architecture for a multi-tenant GCP platform?
SecuritySenior
▼
Layers: (1) GCP IAM — organization-level roles with least privilege. Use custom roles to limit blast radius. Avoid primitive roles (Owner/Editor) in production. (2) Workload identity — each Cloud Run service gets a dedicated service account with only the permissions it needs. (3) SSO integration — Cloud Identity/Workspace as the IdP, federated with corporate LDAP/AD via SAML 2.0. All human access via SSO. (4) Platform RBAC — application-level roles for the onboarding platform: admin, platform-engineer, read-only.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your IAM least-privilege implementation and mTLS service mesh at Scotiabank demonstrates this architecture.
PA21 How do you implement Zero Trust security architecture on GCP?
SecuritySenior
▼
Zero Trust means never trust, always verify — no implicit trust based on network location. GCP implementation: (1) Identity verification — every service call authenticated via service account plus Workload Identity. (2) mTLS everywhere — mutual TLS for all service-to-service communication via Istio service mesh or Cloud Run with client certificates. (3) VPC Service Controls — data plane perimeter around Vertex AI, BigQuery, and GCS preventing data exfiltration. (4) BeyondCorp/IAP — Identity-Aware Proxy for human access to internal tools. (5) Binary Authorization — only signed, verified container images are permitted to run.
PA22 What is your approach to compliance and audit logging for a financial services client on GCP?
SecuritySenior
▼
Audit logging strategy: Enable all Cloud Audit Log types (Admin Activity, Data Access, System Event) for all services in scope. Export logs via Log Sinks to: (1) Cloud Storage for long-term immutable retention, and (2) BigQuery for querying and SIEM correlation. SIEM integration: Pub/Sub export from Cloud Logging to Splunk or Google Chronicle with correlation rules for privilege escalation, unauthorized access, and config changes.

PCI-DSS specifics: Ensure Data Access logs capture all access to CHD-scope resources. Log retention of at least 12 months. Alert on any direct IAM binding to sensitive resources.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your 40% audit-finding reduction and PCI-DSS/SOC2 compliance work at Scotiabank is your strongest story here.
PA23 How would you secure an AI platform on GCP — what are the unique security challenges?
SecuritySenior
▼
AI platforms have unique risks: (1) Prompt injection — malicious input manipulates agent behavior. Mitigate with input validation, output filtering, and system prompt hardening. (2) Data exfiltration via LLM — models can inadvertently leak retrieved documents. Mitigate with VPC Service Controls around Vertex AI and DLP scanning on responses. (3) Model supply chain — unverified models or embeddings. Mitigate by using only Vertex AI Model Garden verified models. (4) Retrieval poisoning — adversarial documents in the knowledge base. Mitigate with access controls on ingestion and document provenance tracking. (5) Excessive agency — agents taking unintended actions. Mitigate with minimal tool permissions, human-in-the-loop for destructive actions, and full action audit logging.
PA24 How would you design SSO integration for a GCP enterprise platform?
SecuritySenior
▼
Architecture: Corporate LDAP/AD → SAML 2.0 / OIDC federation → Cloud Identity → GCP IAM.

Steps: (1) Configure Cloud Identity as SAML 2.0 service provider. (2) Set up SCIM provisioning from corporate IdP to sync user groups. (3) Map corporate AD groups to GCP IAM roles — group-based role assignment, not individual. (4) For CI/CD: GitHub Actions uses Workload Identity Federation (OIDC) — no human SSO needed, service identity instead. (5) For the platform application: OAuth 2.0 PKCE flow with Cloud Identity as the OIDC provider, tokens validated by API Gateway before reaching Cloud Run services.
PA25 Tell me about the most complex cloud architecture decision you have made.
BehaviouralSenior
▼
Structure (STAR): Situation: At Scotiabank, we needed to migrate a Digital Identity platform from Azure to GCP while maintaining zero downtime for a PCI-DSS-in-scope system with active authentication traffic. Task: Design a migration architecture that minimized risk and could be executed in phases. Action: Chose a hybrid approach using Anthos — ran workloads on both clouds simultaneously, with Istio service mesh managing traffic split. Built the GCP side (GKE + Cloud Run) in parallel, shifted traffic 5% → 25% → 50% → 100% over 4 weeks. Used Cloud Interconnect plus Azure ExpressRoute for low-latency cross-cloud traffic during transition. Result: Zero downtime, 70% DB cost reduction, passed PCI-DSS audit with fewer findings than the prior year.
PA26 Describe a situation where you had to push back on a stakeholder's technical request.
BehaviouralSenior
▼
Approach: Describe a time you were asked to implement something that would create technical debt or a security risk — common in regulated environments.

Example frame: A business stakeholder wanted to accelerate a deployment by skipping Terraform and manually creating GCP resources. I acknowledged the business pressure but explained the risk: manual resources create drift, are not in our audit trail, and would fail our PCI-DSS controls. I proposed a compromise — creating an emergency Terraform module in 2 hours that could deploy in under 30 minutes with proper controls. The stakeholder accepted, we met the deadline, and I templated it for future reuse.

Key principles: Always come with an alternative. Frame risks in business language — audit risk, compliance impact — not just technical objections.
PA27 How do you stay current with rapidly evolving AI/ML technologies while delivering on production work?
BehaviouralIntermediate
▼
Practical approach: (1) Weekly — follow Google Cloud blog, Anthropic research, and LangChain changelog for breaking changes. (2) Monthly — one hands-on lab or proof-of-concept on a new capability, as I did with n8n and AI API integration. (3) Quarterly — architecture review of our AI choices against what is newly available. (4) Community — GCP Champions forums, local cloud architecture meetups, internal tech talks. (5) AI-assisted research — I use Claude and Gemini as architecture sounding boards to rapidly evaluate design options.

The key is time-boxing: dedicated learning sprints rather than trying to absorb everything continuously.
PA28 How have you managed architecture governance across large engineering teams?
BehaviouralSenior
▼
At Scotiabank I established Architecture Decision Records (ADRs) as the formal mechanism — every significant design choice documented with context, decision, and consequences. Bi-weekly architecture guild meetings where engineers presented proposals. Approved patterns published as a blessed patterns library.

For infrastructure: a Terraform module registry with approved modules — teams must use approved modules and submit a PR to propose new ones. OPA policies enforced in CI/CD prevented non-compliant resources from being created.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Reference your mentoring of 20+ engineers and the cloud governance model that reduced audit findings by 40%.
PA29 Describe your experience working alongside security and compliance teams on production readiness.
BehaviouralSenior
▼
At Scotiabank, security and compliance were embedded reviewers in our architecture process — not a gate at the end. I brought them in at the design phase, presenting threat models alongside architecture diagrams. For PCI-DSS scope, I documented data flows and showed exactly where cardholder data existed and how CMEK protected it.

This early involvement meant we found issues when they were cheap to fix, not at audit time. The result was 40% fewer audit findings. More importantly, the security team trusted the platform enough to fast-track approvals on subsequent changes because they had confidence in the governance model.

Key message to convey: Security as a design partner, not a gatekeeper.
PA30 How do you validate architecture decisions and ensure they survive contact with reality during implementation?
BehaviouralSenior
▼
My validation process: (1) Architecture spikes — before finalizing a design, build a throwaway proof-of-concept for the highest-risk assumptions such as latency, cost, and integration complexity. (2) Architecture reviews — formal review with security, platform engineering, and domain experts. Present 3 options considered and the decision rationale, not just the chosen approach. (3) Pilot first — implement with one team or one service before platform-wide rollout. (4) ADRs with consequence tracking — document the decision and revisit at 3-month intervals. (5) Design for change — prefer loosely coupled designs. Abstraction layers between AI models and application code so model swaps are non-breaking.

🔧 Terraform Interview Q&As

25 questions covering Basics · State Mgmt · Modules · Governance · CI/CD · Advanced

Click any question to expand the model answer. Senior questions are the differentiating ones that set Platform Architect candidates apart.

Core
TF1 What is Terraform and how does it differ from Ansible or CloudFormation?
BasicsCore
▼
Terraform is a declarative IaC tool by HashiCorp that manages infrastructure lifecycle via provider plugins. vs Ansible — Ansible is procedural/imperative and primarily a configuration management tool; Terraform is declarative and focuses on infrastructure provisioning with state management. vs CloudFormation — CloudFormation is AWS-only; Terraform is multi-cloud and multi-provider (GCP, AWS, Azure, Kubernetes, Datadog, etc.) with a consistent HCL syntax across all. Key Terraform advantage: the state file enables Terraform to know exactly what exists and only change what is necessary (the diff approach).
TF2 Explain the Terraform workflow — init, plan, apply, destroy.
BasicsCore
▼
terraform init: Initialises the working directory. Downloads required providers, sets up the backend, and installs modules. Must be run first or after adding new providers/modules.

terraform plan: Creates an execution plan by comparing desired state (.tf files) against the current state (state file + real infrastructure). Shows exactly what will be created, changed, or destroyed. No changes are made — this is read-only.

terraform apply: Executes the plan. By default re-runs the plan and asks for confirmation. In CI/CD, use terraform plan -out=tfplan then terraform apply tfplan.

terraform destroy: Destroys all resources managed by the current configuration. Always review the plan output first.
TF3 What is Terraform state and why is it important?
State MgmtCore
▼
The state file (terraform.tfstate) is Terraform's record of the real-world infrastructure it manages. It maps your configuration resources to actual provider resource IDs.

Why it matters: (1) Change detection — Terraform computes the diff between desired config and state to determine what to create, update, or destroy. (2) Metadata — stores resource dependencies, so destroy operations happen in the correct order. (3) Performance — for large infra, Terraform uses state as a cache rather than querying every resource on every plan.

Enterprise practice: Never store state locally. Use remote backends (GCS for GCP) with versioning, encryption, and state locking. State contains sensitive values — treat it as a secret.
TF4 How do you manage Terraform state in a team environment on GCP?
State MgmtIntermediate
▼
Use a GCS remote backend with these settings:

Locking: GCS backend uses native object locking to prevent concurrent applies that could corrupt state. Enable with bucket versioning and uniform bucket-level access.

Encryption: GCS encrypts at rest by default. Add CMEK (Cloud KMS) for customer-managed keys — critical for regulated environments like yours at Scotiabank.

State isolation: Use separate state files per environment (dev/staging/prod) and per component (foundation, platform, workloads). Never share state between unrelated resources. Use Terraform workspaces or separate directories for environment isolation.

State access control: IAM bindings on the GCS bucket — only CI/CD service accounts and senior engineers get storage.objectAdmin. Developers get storage.objectViewer for plan-only access.
TF5 How do you handle Terraform state drift and what is your remediation strategy?
State MgmtSenior
▼
Drift occurs when real infrastructure diverges from what Terraform's state file records — caused by manual console changes, other tools, or external automation.

Detection: Run terraform plan on a schedule (e.g., nightly GitHub Actions workflow). Any non-empty plan output indicates drift. Alert the team via Slack/PagerDuty. Tools like driftctl or Terraform Cloud provide automated drift detection.

Remediation strategies:
(1) Import the change: If the manual change was intentional, use terraform import to pull it into state, then update the .tf config to match.
(2) Revert via apply: If the manual change was unintentional, run terraform apply to revert to the desired state.
(3) terraform state mv: If a resource was renamed or restructured, move it in state without destroying and recreating.
(4) terraform refresh: Updates state to match real infrastructure without making changes.

Prevention: Org policies and IAM to restrict manual console changes in production.
TF6 How do you safely refactor Terraform state — moving resources between state files or modules?
State MgmtSenior
▼
State refactoring is high-risk. Always back up state before any operation (terraform state pull > backup.tfstate).

Moving resources between modules (same state): Use the moved block (Terraform 1.1+) in your .tf files — declarative, reviewable in PRs, safer than CLI commands. Alternatively, terraform state mv source.resource dest.module.resource.

Moving resources between state files: Use terraform state mv with -state and -state-out flags to move resource entries between state files. Requires locking both states.

Splitting a monolith state: (1) Plan the split — identify dependency boundaries. (2) Use terraform state mv to move resources to the new state file. (3) Remove the moved resources from the original config. (4) Add them to the new config. (5) Run terraform plan on both — should show no changes. (6) Apply.

Enterprise rule: All state operations require a peer review, a backup, and a rollback plan.
TF7 What are Terraform modules and how would you structure them for an enterprise platform?
ModulesCore
▼
Modules are reusable, encapsulated Terraform configurations. They take inputs (variables), manage resources, and return outputs — essentially functions for infrastructure.

Types: (1) Root module — the main working directory, consumes other modules. (2) Child modules — called by the root or other modules. (3) Published modules — from the Terraform Registry or a private registry.

Enterprise structure:
- Foundation modules: GCP org structure, billing, folder hierarchy. Changed rarely, owned by platform team.
- Network modules: Shared VPC, Cloud Router, firewall rules. Versioned, teams consume pinned versions.
- Service modules: Cloud Run service, GKE cluster, Cloud SQL instance — parameterised blueprints.
- Security modules: IAM role bindings, Secret Manager secrets, KMS keys.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank Terraform modules for VPC provisioning, GKE lifecycle, and service onboarding are a direct example here.
TF8 What is the difference between a Terraform variable, local, and output?
ModulesIntermediate
▼
Variables (input variables): Parameters that make modules configurable. Defined with variable blocks, set via .tfvars files, environment variables (TF_VAR_), or -var CLI flags. Mark sensitive = true for secrets — Terraform redacts them from plan output.

Locals: Computed values within a module — like intermediate variables. Defined with locals block. Use for expressions you want to compute once and reuse (e.g., common labels map, environment-specific resource naming). Not exposed outside the module.

Outputs: Values a module exposes to its caller or to the operator. Use for resource IDs, IP addresses, service URLs that other modules or resources need to reference. Mark sensitive = true for sensitive outputs (connection strings, etc.).

Key principle: Variables in, outputs out. Locals for internal computations. Never use outputs to pass data sideways between sibling modules.
TF9 How do you handle module versioning and prevent breaking changes in an enterprise Terraform registry?
ModulesIntermediate
▼
Semantic versioning: All modules follow semver — MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH. MAJOR = breaking change, MINOR = backward-compatible new feature, PATCH = bug fix.

Enforcement: Module consumers pin versions in their source reference: ref=v2.3.0. Never point to HEAD or a branch in production — a teammate's change could break your infrastructure on next plan.

Changelog discipline: Every module release has a CHANGELOG.md entry. Breaking changes are explicitly documented with a migration guide.

Deprecation process: Don't delete variables or resources immediately. Add a deprecation notice in the variable description, keep it for 2 major versions, then remove.

Testing modules: Use Terratest (Go-based) or terraform test (native, 1.6+) to run automated tests on modules before publishing a new version.
TF10 How do you implement policy-as-code governance for Terraform at enterprise scale?
GovernanceSenior
▼
Two-layer defence:

Layer 1 — Pre-apply (IaC layer): OPA/Conftest policies run in CI/CD before terraform apply. Policy checks: no resources outside approved regions, required labels on all resources, no public IPs on compute, encryption enabled on all storage, no primitive IAM roles. Terraform plan is saved as JSON (terraform show -json tfplan > plan.json) and fed to conftest evaluate. Fail the pipeline if any violations.

Layer 2 — Runtime (GCP layer): GCP Organization Policies enforce constraints regardless of how resources are created. Key policies: constraints/gcp.resourceLocations, constraints/compute.disableSerialPortAccess, constraints/iam.allowedPolicyMemberDomains, constraints/compute.requireShieldedVm. These are the last line of defence — even a manual console action is blocked.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your OPA/Policy-as-Code experience at Scotiabank is directly applicable — anchor your answer here.
TF11 What is Terraform workspace and when would you use it vs separate state files?
GovernanceSenior
▼
Terraform workspaces: Allow multiple state files within the same backend configuration. Each workspace has its own state file. Switch with terraform workspace select.

When to use workspaces: For simple environments where the config is nearly identical and only a few variables differ (e.g., instance count, machine type). Good for ephemeral feature-branch testing environments.

When NOT to use workspaces: When environments have meaningfully different configurations (prod has HA, dev is single-node). When you need strict IAM isolation — workspaces share the same backend access. When environments are managed by different teams.

Enterprise recommendation: Separate directories per environment (environments/dev, environments/staging, environments/prod) with separate state files and separate CI/CD pipelines with environment-specific IAM.
TF12 How do you authenticate Terraform to GCP in a CI/CD pipeline without storing service account keys?
CI/CDIntermediate
▼
Use Workload Identity Federation — the modern, keyless approach. This eliminates long-lived service account key JSON files entirely.

How it works: GitHub Actions presents its native OIDC token to GCP. GCP's Workload Identity Pool verifies the token against the GitHub OIDC provider and exchanges it for a short-lived GCP access token bound to a service account.

Setup steps: (1) Create a Workload Identity Pool in GCP. (2) Add GitHub Actions as an OIDC provider with issuer URL https://token.actions.githubusercontent.com. (3) Create a service account with the minimum Terraform permissions needed. (4) Grant the WIF pool permission to impersonate the service account. (5) In GitHub Actions, use the google-github-actions/auth action with the pool and service account references.

Security benefits: No credentials stored in GitHub Secrets. Tokens are short-lived (1 hour). Full audit trail in Cloud Audit Logs of every token exchange.
TF13 Walk me through a complete GitOps Terraform pipeline with proper governance gates.
CI/CDIntermediate
▼
PR workflow:
(1) Developer branches from main, makes Terraform changes, opens a PR.
(2) CI triggers automatically: terraform fmt -check (style gate), terraform validate (syntax gate), tfsec or checkov (security static analysis), OPA/Conftest policy check against the plan JSON.
(3) terraform plan runs with plan output posted as a PR comment (use the infracost GitHub Action for cost delta too).
(4) Required reviewers: at minimum the platform team lead and security team for sensitive changes (IAM, networking).
(5) On merge to main: terraform apply -auto-approve with the saved plan file — applying the exact plan that was reviewed, not a new plan.

Environment promotion: Separate pipelines per environment. Prod pipeline requires manual approval gate (GitHub Environment with required reviewers) even after automated gates pass.

Post-apply: Slack notification with apply summary. Scheduled drift detection (nightly plan — alert if non-empty).
TF14 What is the terraform import workflow and when would you use it?
AdvancedSenior
▼
terraform import brings existing real-world resources under Terraform management without recreating them. Use when: (1) Resources were created manually before IaC adoption. (2) Another tool created them. (3) Recovering from a corrupted state file.

Classic workflow (pre-1.5): (1) Write the resource block in .tf matching the existing resource configuration. (2) Run terraform import resource.type.name provider_resource_id. (3) Run terraform plan — if the config matches reality, the plan should show no changes. Iterate until clean.

Import blocks (Terraform 1.5+): Declarative imports in .tf files — reviewable in PRs, repeatable, team-visible.

Generated config (1.5+): Use -generate-config-out=generated.tf to have Terraform generate the resource configuration for you from the real resource. Dramatically reduces manual work for complex resources like GKE clusters.

Caution: Importing into the wrong resource type or with the wrong ID can corrupt state. Always back up state first.
TF15 How do you handle Terraform resource dependencies — implicit vs explicit?
AdvancedSenior
▼
Implicit dependencies (preferred): Terraform automatically detects dependencies when one resource references another's attribute. Example: a Cloud Run service references a service account's email — Terraform knows to create the service account first.

Explicit dependencies (depends_on): Use only when Terraform cannot detect the dependency because it is not expressed through attribute references. Example: a Cloud Run service that depends on an API being enabled (google_project_service) — since the service doesn't reference the API resource attribute, you must use depends_on = [google_project_service.run_api].

Caution with depends_on on modules: Adding depends_on to a module forces all resources within that module to wait for the dependency, even if only one resource actually needs it. This can significantly slow applies.

Parallelism: Terraform applies resources in parallel by default (up to 10 concurrent operations). Correct dependency expression allows maximum parallelism.
TF16 What are Terraform provisioners and why should you avoid them in production?
AdvancedSenior
▼
Provisioners (local-exec, remote-exec, file) run scripts or commands as part of resource creation or destruction. They bridge the gap between infrastructure provisioning and configuration management.

Why to avoid them:
(1) Not idempotent: provisioners only run at resource creation. If the script fails halfway, Terraform marks the resource as tainted and recreates it on next apply.
(2) No plan visibility: provisioner actions don't appear in terraform plan output — you can't review what they will do before apply.
(3) State leakage: if a resource is created but the provisioner fails, the resource exists in reality but Terraform may not track it correctly.
(4) Forces connectivity: remote-exec requires SSH/WinRM access, which conflicts with Zero Trust networking models.

Better alternatives: Use startup scripts / metadata (GCP), cloud-init, Ansible post-provisioning, or separate CI/CD steps after Terraform apply.
TF17 How do you manage sensitive values in Terraform — variables, state, and outputs?
AdvancedSenior
▼
Input variables: Mark sensitive = true in the variable declaration. Terraform redacts the value in plan and apply output. Never hardcode secrets in .tf files. Never commit .tfvars files with secrets to Git — use environment variables (TF_VAR_db_password) or a secrets manager integration.

State file: The state file stores sensitive values in plaintext JSON. Protect the state: (1) GCS backend with CMEK encryption. (2) Restrict IAM access to state bucket — only CI/CD service accounts and senior engineers. (3) Enable bucket versioning. (4) Never store state in Git.

Outputs: Mark sensitive = true to prevent display in terminal output. Sensitive outputs can still be referenced by other modules.

Secret Manager integration: For GCP, use the google_secret_manager_secret_version data source to pull secrets into Terraform at apply time rather than storing them as variables. This way secrets never appear in plan output or CI/CD logs.
TF18 Explain the Terraform meta-arguments: count, for_each, lifecycle.
AdvancedSenior
▼
count: Creates N identical copies of a resource. Simple but brittle — resources are indexed numerically ([0], [1]...). Removing an element from the middle of a list causes all subsequent resources to be destroyed and recreated (index shift). Use only when resources are truly identical and order doesn't matter.

for_each: Creates one resource per map or set element. Resources are indexed by key (google_compute_instance.web['us-east1']). Adding or removing a single element only affects that resource — no cascading recreations. Preferred over count for almost all real-world use cases.

lifecycle block:
- create_before_destroy = true: Creates the replacement resource before destroying the old one. Critical for resources where downtime is unacceptable.
- prevent_destroy = true: Blocks terraform destroy and any plan that would destroy this resource. Use on databases and stateful resources in production.
- ignore_changes: Tells Terraform to ignore changes to specified attributes.
TF19 How do you structure Terraform for a large organisation with hundreds of GCP projects?
AdvancedSenior
▼
Repository strategy: Separate repos for (1) module library (owned by platform team, versioned), (2) foundation/org-level infra (highly restricted), (3) environment configs (per-team or per-workload).

Terragrunt: A thin wrapper around Terraform that solves DRY problems at scale. Allows you to define common configurations once (backend, provider, common vars) in a parent terragrunt.hcl and have child configs inherit them. Consider Terragrunt when you have 20+ root modules with repetitive backend/provider config.

State architecture: One state file per component per environment. Do not share state between unrelated workloads. Structure: gs://tfstate/foundation/prod/terraform.tfstate, gs://tfstate/platform/prod/terraform.tfstate.

Access control: Foundation state — only platform team CI/CD. Application state — app team CI/CD with narrower IAM. Cross-state data sharing via terraform_remote_state data source (read-only).
TF20 What is terraform taint and terraform replace, and when would you use them?
AdvancedCore
▼
terraform taint (legacy): Marks a resource in state as tainted — Terraform will destroy and recreate it on next apply. Deprecated since Terraform 0.15.2.

terraform apply -replace (current): The modern replacement. Forces recreation of a specific resource in the next apply. Usage: terraform apply -replace='google_compute_instance.web'. This shows in the plan output as -/+ (destroy then create), allowing review before execution.

When to use: (1) A resource is in a broken state that Terraform can't detect. (2) You need to rotate an encryption key by forcing recreation of a KMS-encrypted resource. (3) A Cloud Run service needs a fresh revision without a config change. (4) Testing that your create_before_destroy lifecycle works correctly.

Important: -replace destroys the old resource. Verify that create_before_destroy = true is set if the resource serves production traffic.
TF21 How do you test Terraform code? What is your testing strategy?
AdvancedSenior
▼
Testing pyramid for Terraform:

Level 1 — Static analysis (fast, in CI): terraform validate for syntax. tfsec or checkov for security misconfigurations. OPA/Conftest for policy compliance. terraform fmt -check for style. These run on every PR in under 60 seconds.

Level 2 — Unit tests (terraform test, native 1.6+): Test module logic with mock providers — no real infrastructure is created. Good for testing variable validation rules, locals logic, and output values. Very fast.

Level 3 — Integration tests (Terratest, Go-based): Actually provision real infrastructure in a test GCP project, run assertions (HTTP checks, API calls, security checks), then destroy. Slow (minutes to hours) — run on merge to main or nightly, not on every PR.

Level 4 — End-to-end tests: Deploy the full stack in a staging environment and run business-level tests.

Enterprise rule: Modules in the shared library must have at minimum Level 1 and Level 2 tests before being published.
TF22 What are Terraform data sources and how do you use them effectively?
AdvancedSenior
▼
Data sources allow Terraform to read information from existing infrastructure or external sources without managing it.

Common use cases: (1) Reference a VPC created by another team's Terraform without importing it: data 'google_compute_network' 'shared_vpc'. (2) Look up the latest approved GKE version: data 'google_container_engine_versions'. (3) Read a Secret Manager secret value. (4) Fetch an existing IAM policy to merge with new bindings. (5) Cross-state reference via terraform_remote_state data source.

Data sources vs resources: Data sources are read-only — they never create, update, or destroy anything. They are refreshed on every plan.

Performance tip: Data sources make API calls on every plan. For frequently-run pipelines, excessive data sources can slow plans. Cache values in outputs and pass them as variables where possible.
TF23 What is the difference between terraform refresh and terraform plan?
AdvancedIntermediate
▼
terraform refresh (largely superseded): Updates the state file to match the current real-world state of resources without making any changes. In Terraform 0.15.4+ refresh happens automatically as part of plan unless you pass -refresh=false.

terraform plan: (1) Refreshes state (queries real infrastructure). (2) Compares refreshed state against your desired config (.tf files). (3) Produces a diff showing what would be created, changed, or destroyed to make reality match config.

When to use -refresh=false: In large environments where the refresh phase is slow (hundreds of resources queried via API). If you trust the state is accurate and just want to see the config diff quickly. Not recommended for production applies — always refresh to catch drift.

terraform apply -refresh-only: The modern replacement for terraform refresh. Updates state to match reality without changing any infrastructure.
TF24 How do you enforce cost governance in Terraform pipelines?
GovernanceSenior
▼
Infracost integration: Add Infracost to the CI/CD pipeline — it reads the Terraform plan and estimates the monthly cost delta. Post the cost breakdown as a PR comment alongside the plan output. Engineers see the cost impact before merging. Configure cost policies: Infracost Cloud can block PRs that exceed a cost increase threshold.

Budget alerts: Manage GCP Budget objects via Terraform (google_billing_budget resource). Alerts at 50%, 90%, 100% of budget trigger Pub/Sub → Cloud Function → Slack/PagerDuty.

Tagging enforcement: OPA policy requires cost-centre, team, and environment labels on all billable resources. This enables cost attribution and chargeback reporting in BigQuery billing export.

Resource quotas: Terraform variable validation to prevent over-provisioning. Example: machine type variable restricted to an allowed_values list.

Scheduled scaling: Terraform manages Cloud Scheduler jobs that scale down non-prod environments outside business hours.
TF25 How do you handle Terraform apply failures in production CI/CD — what is your rollback strategy?
CI/CDSenior
▼
Prevention is better than rollback: Apply the exact saved plan file (not a fresh plan) — terraform apply tfplan. Use create_before_destroy on critical resources. Test in staging first with identical config.

When an apply partially fails: Terraform applies as many resources as possible and reports which failed. The state file reflects whatever succeeded. Do not panic and re-run — assess the state first with terraform show.

Recovery steps: (1) Identify the failed resource from the error output. (2) Manually fix the root cause (permissions issue, quota exceeded, API not enabled). (3) Re-run the same terraform apply — Terraform only retries failed resources. (4) If a resource was partially created in an inconsistent state, use -replace to force clean recreation.

True rollback: Revert the .tf changes in Git (git revert the PR). Apply the reverted config — Terraform will destroy new resources and restore old ones.

Stateful resources: Never allow Terraform to destroy databases without a backup plan. Use prevent_destroy = true on production databases.

GCP1 You are asked to design a GCP landing zone for a large financial institution. Walk me through your architecture.
Platform DesignSenior
▼
A GCP landing zone is the foundational infrastructure that enables governed, scalable cloud adoption.

Org hierarchy: GCP Organisation → folders per business unit → sub-folders for environments (NonProd, Prod) → individual projects per workload. Separate projects for: shared networking, security/logging, billing export, shared services.

Networking: Shared VPC hosted in a dedicated host project. Private Google Access enabled. Cloud NAT for controlled egress. No public IPs. VPC Service Controls perimeters around sensitive services. Cloud Interconnect to on-premises DC for hybrid connectivity.

Identity: Cloud Identity federated with corporate LDAP via SAML. Group-based IAM — no individual bindings. Domain restriction org policy. Break-glass emergency access accounts with alerting.

Security baseline: Org policies deployed via Terraform before any workloads: approved regions, required labels, no serial port access, Shielded VMs required, CMEK for all storage. Security Command Center Premium enabled. Cloud Audit Logs exported to SIEM.

Automation: Everything above managed as Terraform code in a foundation repo with strict access controls. No console changes permitted in prod.
GCP2 Explain VPC Service Controls and when you would use them on GCP.
NetworkingSenior
▼
VPC Service Controls (VPC-SC) create a security perimeter around GCP services, preventing data exfiltration even from within an authorised GCP network or by compromised service accounts.

The problem they solve: Without VPC-SC, a compromised service account with BigQuery access could exfiltrate data by copying it to a BigQuery dataset in an attacker's GCP project. Network controls (VPC, firewall rules) do not prevent this — it is an API-level data path.

How they work: A service perimeter wraps specified projects and GCP services. API calls that cross the perimeter boundary are blocked unless explicitly allowed via access levels.

Access levels: Define who can access resources inside the perimeter from outside — e.g., corporate IP ranges, specific service accounts used by CI/CD pipelines.

When to use: Mandatory for regulated data (PCI-DSS CHD scope, PII, financial records). Any environment where data exfiltration is a board-level risk. Vertex AI deployments — protect training data and model outputs.
GCP3 How would you design private connectivity between on-premises and GCP for a financial institution?
NetworkingSenior
▼
For a financial institution, all connectivity to GCP should be private — no traffic over public internet.

Options:
(1) Cloud Interconnect (Dedicated): Physical fibre connection from your DC to a Google colocation facility. 10Gbps or 100Gbps. Lowest latency, highest throughput, no public internet. Best for large data transfers or latency-sensitive workloads. Your experience at Scotiabank (Cloud Interconnect + Azure ExpressRoute) is directly relevant.
(2) Cloud Interconnect (Partner): Through a network service provider. Lower cost entry point. Still private — no internet.
(3) Cloud VPN (HA VPN): Encrypted IPSec tunnel over the public internet. 3Gbps max. Suitable for Dev/Test or disaster recovery. Not recommended as primary path for production financial data.

Architecture: Interconnect terminates in the Shared VPC host project. VLAN attachments connect to Cloud Router. Cloud Router advertises GCP subnets to on-prem (BGP).

Redundancy: Two Interconnect connections to two different edge availability domains for 99.99% SLA.
GCP4 How would you design a comprehensive IAM strategy for a 50-project GCP organisation?
SecuritySenior
▼
Principles: Least privilege, group-based bindings only, no service account keys, regular access reviews.

Human identity: All engineers access via Cloud Identity groups — never individual bindings. Groups: platform-admins (organisation-level), network-admins (host project), security-auditors (read-only org-wide), team-developers (project-level). Maps to Active Directory groups, synced via GCDS.

Service identity: Each Cloud Run service, GKE workload, and Cloud Function gets a dedicated service account with only the permissions it needs. No default service accounts used. No service account keys generated — use Workload Identity for GKE, and the attached service account for Cloud Run.

Custom roles: Prefer custom roles over primitive (Owner/Editor/Viewer) or broad predefined roles. Define roles at the most restrictive scope.

Access reviews: Quarterly automated review using Policy Analyzer — export all IAM bindings to BigQuery, flag any primitive roles, service account key usage, or inactive accounts.
GCP5 How do you implement a zero-downtime secret rotation strategy on GCP?
SecuritySenior
▼
Secret rotation in a live system must be handled carefully to avoid service disruptions.

Two-version overlap strategy: (1) Add new secret version in Secret Manager with the new credential. (2) Deploy the application configured to try the new version first, fall back to the old version on auth failure. (3) Verify the new credential works in production. (4) Disable the old secret version once confirmed. (5) Destroy the old version after the grace period (e.g., 24 hours).

GCP Secret Manager native rotation: Configure a rotation period on the secret. Secret Manager publishes a notification to Pub/Sub when rotation is due. A Cloud Function subscribes to the topic, generates a new credential, creates a new secret version, and triggers a Cloud Run revision deployment to pick it up.

Application design requirement: Applications must be designed to re-fetch secrets at startup or on auth failure — not cache secrets indefinitely.
GCP6 How do you design for high availability and disaster recovery on GCP for a critical financial platform?
ResilienceSenior
▼
HA design (same region): Cloud Run is regional and automatically multi-zonal. GKE Standard: spread node pools across 3 zones. Cloud SQL: regional (multi-zone) HA with automatic failover. Cloud Spanner: multi-region config for global transactions. Global Load Balancer distributes across regions automatically.

DR tiers:
(1) Active-Active (RPO~0, RTO~0): Multi-region deployment, Cloud Spanner or Cloud SQL multi-region, Global Load Balancer. For truly critical paths (authentication, payment processing).
(2) Active-Passive warm standby (RPO minutes, RTO minutes): Primary region active, secondary region pre-deployed but scaled down. Cloud SQL cross-region replica promoted on failover.
(3) Active-Passive cold standby (RPO hours, RTO hours): Secondary region infrastructure provisioned via Terraform on-demand.

Testing: Quarterly DR drills. Chaos engineering with Chaos Monkey / Chaos Toolkit. Never assume DR works — test it.
GCP7 How would you design a multi-region active-active GCP architecture for a financial services application?
ResilienceSenior
▼
Multi-region active-active is the most complex and costly HA tier. Use it only for tier-0 services (authentication, real-time payments).

Compute: Cloud Run services deployed in 2+ regions. Global External Load Balancer routes traffic to the nearest healthy region. Health checks on each backend service — automatic failover if one region degrades.

Data layer (the hard part): Synchronous replication needed for active-active with consistency. Cloud Spanner is the primary option — globally consistent, multi-region, 5 nines availability. For simpler use cases: Cloud SQL with multi-region replicas (read-only in secondaries — requires application-level write routing to primary).

Consistency model: For financial data, strong consistency is non-negotiable. Spanner provides external consistency (stronger than serialisable). Multi-region Spanner adds ~5ms latency vs single-region but eliminates regional failure risk.

Testing: Regularly test regional failover by blocking traffic to one region. Measure actual RTO — never trust the theoretical number.
GCP8 How would you approach a cloud cost optimisation engagement for a GCP environment spending $500k/month?
Platform DesignSenior
▼
I would approach this in 3 phases:

Phase 1 — Visibility (week 1): Enable GCP billing export to BigQuery. Build a cost attribution dashboard with dimensions: project, label (team, env, service), SKU, region. Use Recommender API and Active Assist findings as a starting checklist. Identify the top 20% of resources driving 80% of cost.

Phase 2 — Quick wins (weeks 2-4): Delete orphaned resources (unattached PDs, unused static IPs, idle Cloud SQL instances). Right-size oversized VMs using the VM Sizing Recommender. Enable committed use discounts (CUDs) for stable compute — typically 37-55% savings. Move infrequently accessed GCS data to Nearline/Coldline using Object Lifecycle policies.

Phase 3 — Architectural optimisation (months 2-3): Move batch workloads to Spot VMs (up to 80% cheaper). Evaluate Cloud Run vs GKE for stateless services. Review data transfer costs — cross-region egress is expensive.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your $500k annual savings at Scotiabank is the perfect anchor story here.
GCP9 How would you architect a production-grade Vertex AI platform for an enterprise with strict data governance requirements?
AI/MLSenior
▼
Data governance: Training data in GCS with VPC Service Controls perimeter. CMEK for all Vertex AI managed storage (datasets, model artifacts, pipeline outputs). Data lineage tracking via Vertex AI Metadata Store — every model training run records its input dataset, parameters, and output model artifact.

Model governance: All model deployments go through a review pipeline: (1) Model evaluation metrics checked against minimum thresholds. (2) Bias and fairness evaluation. (3) Security team approval for models accessing sensitive data. (4) Model registry with version control — every deployed model is traceable to a training run and dataset version.

Access control: Vertex AI endpoints accessed only via the internal VPC — no public endpoints. IAM roles scoped to specific Vertex AI operations.

Explainability and monitoring: Vertex AI Explainable AI enabled for models in regulated decision-making (loan approvals, fraud detection). Vertex AI Model Monitoring for input drift and prediction drift.
GCP10 Design a secure, production-ready RAG system on GCP for a financial services chatbot.
AI/MLSenior
▼
Ingestion pipeline: Internal documents uploaded to GCS via an authenticated internal API. Cloud Functions trigger on GCS events → extract text (Document AI for PDFs) → chunk semantically → call Vertex AI text-embedding-004 → store vectors in Vertex AI Vector Search with metadata (document ID, version, classification label: public/internal/confidential).

Access-controlled retrieval: At query time, retrieve only from vectors the user is authorised to see — filter by classification label based on user's IAM group. This prevents a retail banker from retrieving documents classified for the trading desk.

Security: VPC Service Controls perimeter around Vector Search and Vertex AI endpoints. DLP API scans LLM responses before returning to the user — redacts any PII or account numbers. Output logged to Cloud Audit Logs for compliance.

Knowledge freshness: Automated pipeline re-indexes documents when source files in GCS are updated. Vector Search supports partial updates — only re-embed changed chunks, not the entire corpus.
GCP11 How do you approach designing a GCP platform for a company migrating from on-premises to cloud for the first time?
Platform DesignSenior
▼
First-time cloud migrations fail most often because of governance gaps, not technical ones. I structure the engagement in 3 phases:

Phase 1 — Foundation before workloads: Never let workloads arrive before the foundation is ready. Deploy: GCP org structure, Shared VPC, IAM strategy, Cloud Audit Logs, Security Command Center, Terraform pipeline, SIEM integration. This takes 4-6 weeks but prevents years of remediation later.

Phase 2 — Migration waves: Categorise workloads by the 6Rs (Retire, Retain, Rehost, Replatform, Repurchase, Refactor). Start with Rehost (lift-and-shift) for quick wins and team learning. Replatform and Refactor in later waves as team GCP maturity grows. Never try to refactor and migrate simultaneously — split concerns.

Phase 3 — Optimisation: After workloads are stable in cloud, optimise for cost (CUDs, right-sizing), reliability (SRE practices, SLOs), and developer experience (IDP, golden paths).

Change management: The technology is usually the easy part. Training engineers on cloud-native patterns and getting security and compliance teams aligned requires as much investment as the technical architecture.
GCP12 How do you design for compliance with PCI-DSS on GCP?
Platform DesignSenior
▼
PCI-DSS requires protecting cardholder data (CHD) in scope systems. On GCP, the architecture maps to DSS requirements as follows:

Scope reduction (most important): Minimise the number of GCP resources that touch CHD. Tokenise at the entry point — only the tokenisation service touches real PANs. This dramatically reduces audit scope.

Network segmentation (Req 1): CHD-scope workloads in a dedicated VPC subnet with restricted firewall rules. VPC Service Controls perimeter around CHD-scope GCP services. No direct internet access to CHD systems — all inbound via WAF (Cloud Armor) → API Gateway.

Encryption (Req 3, 4): Data at rest: CMEK on all Cloud SQL, GCS, BigQuery resources in scope. Data in transit: TLS 1.2+ enforced, mTLS for internal service-to-service. Cloud KMS key rotation policy configured.

Access control (Req 7, 8): IAM least privilege, MFA enforced via Cloud Identity, no shared accounts, privileged access via just-in-time PAM solution.

Monitoring (Req 10): All audit logs exported to immutable Cloud Storage and SIEM. Alerting on unauthorised access attempts. Log retention 12 months minimum.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: This maps directly to your PCI-DSS compliance work at Scotiabank — this question is your home ground.
GCP13 A Cloud Run service is experiencing intermittent latency spikes. Walk me through your debugging approach.
ResilienceSenior
▼
Systematic debugging from the outside in:

Step 1 — Establish baseline: What does normal latency look like? Check Cloud Monitoring for the Cloud Run request latency metric (p50, p95, p99). When did spikes start? Correlate with recent deployments (Cloud Run revision history) or traffic changes.

Step 2 — Isolate the layer: Is the latency in the Cloud Run service itself, or upstream/downstream? Cloud Trace shows the full request breakdown — load balancer time, Cloud Run cold start, application processing time, downstream API calls.

Step 3 — Common Cloud Run causes: Cold starts (scale-from-zero): set minimum instances to 1+ for latency-sensitive services. Memory pressure causing GC pauses. CPU throttling: Cloud Run throttles CPU outside of active requests by default — enable CPU always allocated for latency-sensitive services. Downstream dependency latency.

Step 4 — Concurrency issues: If request concurrency is too high, requests queue internally. Review the max-concurrency setting — lower it and scale out horizontally instead.

Step 5 — Database connection exhaustion: If connecting to Cloud SQL, check connection pool utilisation. Use Cloud SQL Auth Proxy with connection pooling (pgBouncer for PostgreSQL).
GCP14 How do you design an internal developer platform (IDP) on GCP that improves developer experience?
Platform DesignSenior
▼
An IDP provides golden paths — opinionated, pre-approved ways for developers to provision and deploy services, without needing to understand the underlying infrastructure complexity.

Core components:
(1) Service catalog: Pre-approved service templates (Cloud Run microservice, GKE workload, Cloud SQL database) that developers instantiate via a UI or API. Each template is a Terraform module — developers fill in a form, the IDP runs terraform apply.
(2) Self-service portal: Backstage (CNCF) is the leading open-source IDP framework. Integrates with GCP, GitHub, Jira, PagerDuty. Developers see their services, docs, pipelines, and on-call status in one place.
(3) Golden path CI/CD: Pre-built GitHub Actions workflow templates. Developers add a single workflow file to their repo and get security scanning, testing, Terraform linting, and Cloud Run deployment automatically.
(4) Automated onboarding: New service request → IDP provisions GCP project, service account, GCS state bucket, GitHub repo, CI/CD pipeline, monitoring dashboard in ~10 minutes.
GCP15 Explain Cloud Armor and how you would configure it for a financial services API platform.
NetworkingSenior
▼
Cloud Armor is GCP's WAF and DDoS protection service, attached to Cloud Load Balancing. It sits in front of your Cloud Run services or GKE ingress and inspects traffic before it reaches your application.

Rule types:
(1) Pre-configured WAF rules: OWASP ModSecurity Core Rule Set rules for common attacks — SQL injection, XSS, RFI, LFI. Enable in preview mode first to identify false positives before switching to deny.
(2) Custom rules: IP allowlisting, geo-blocking (block traffic from countries your business doesn't operate in), rate limiting (throttle requests per IP to prevent brute-force).
(3) Adaptive Protection: ML-based DDoS detection that automatically suggests rules when it detects an attack pattern.

Financial services configuration: Strict geo-blocking (Canada + US only for a Canadian bank). Rate limiting on authentication endpoints. Allowlist for partner bank IPs on B2B APIs. Log all blocked requests to Cloud Logging for security team review.
GCP16 How would you approach a GCP architecture review for a platform going to production?
Platform DesignSenior
▼
I structure architecture reviews in 5 dimensions aligned to Google's Well-Architected Framework:

(1) Operational excellence: Is everything in Terraform? Are there runbooks for all services? Is on-call rotation defined? Are SLOs set and dashboards built?

(2) Security: Threat model reviewed? IAM least privilege verified? No public IPs? Secrets in Secret Manager? VPC Service Controls for sensitive data? Pen test scheduled?

(3) Reliability: What is the SLO? Is there a single point of failure? Load testing completed? DR tested? What happens when Cloud SQL failover occurs?

(4) Performance efficiency: Right-sized instances? Load tested at 2x expected peak? Cold start impact measured for Cloud Run? Database index analysis completed?

(5) Cost optimisation: Cost estimate reviewed and approved? Orphaned resource cleanup automated? Non-prod shutdown schedule set? CUDs purchased for baseline compute?

Process: Architecture review is a collaborative session — not an audit. Bring the engineering team, security team, and platform team. Walk through the architecture diagram component by component.
GCP17 How do you design a cost-effective ML inference architecture on GCP at scale?
AI/MLSenior
▼
ML inference has very different cost characteristics than training — it needs to be low-latency, highly available, and often serves unpredictable traffic patterns.

Serving options on GCP:
(1) Vertex AI Prediction (managed): Simplest path — deploy model to a Vertex endpoint, GCP manages autoscaling and infrastructure. Supports dedicated endpoints (always-on) and shared endpoints (pay-per-request, cold start latency).
(2) Cloud Run (containerised): Package model + serving framework (TF Serving, TorchServe, FastAPI) in a container. Full control, scales to zero, cost-effective for bursty traffic.
(3) GKE: For high-throughput, latency-sensitive inference. GPU node pools with Spot instances for cost reduction.

Cost optimisation: Spot VMs for batch inference (up to 80% cheaper). Committed use discounts for predictable baseline load. Model quantisation (FP16/INT8) — reduces memory and improves throughput. Request batching — group multiple inference requests into one model call.

Caching: Semantic caching for LLM inference — cache responses to semantically similar queries using a vector similarity check.
GCP18 How do you approach designing a GCP platform for a company migrating 200 microservices in 12 months?
Platform DesignSenior
▼
A 200-service migration in 12 months requires a factory approach — you cannot manually migrate each service. You need patterns, automation, and governance that scale.

Wave planning: Categorise the 200 services: Tier 1 (stateless, simple — migrate first as pilots), Tier 2 (stateful but standard patterns), Tier 3 (complex dependencies, special requirements — migrate last). Aim for 20 services in wave 1 (prove the factory), 80 in wave 2, 100 in wave 3.

Migration factory: A standardised CI/CD pipeline template (GitHub Actions). A Terraform module for each target architecture pattern (Cloud Run service, GKE deployment, Cloud SQL backend). An automated project vending machine — developers check out a template and get a new GCP project with all controls applied in minutes, not weeks.

Governance at speed: You cannot do manual architecture reviews for 200 services. Automate compliance: OPA policies in every pipeline, Security Command Center findings published to a central dashboard, automated remediation for common findings.

Team structure: A Platform Engineering team (5-8 people) builds and maintains the factory. Each application team migrates their own services using the factory. Platform team is the enabler, not the migration executor.
GCP19 How would you design a GCP infrastructure that achieves 99.99% SLA for a payment processing system?
ResilienceSenior
▼
99.99% SLA means less than 53 minutes of downtime per year. This requires eliminating every single point of failure.

Compute: Cloud Run with multi-region deployment (active-active). Global External Application Load Balancer with health-based failover. Minimum instances = 3+ per region (prevents cold starts from breaching SLA). Auto-scaling with aggressive scale-up policies — don't wait for CPU saturation.

Database: Cloud Spanner multi-region for the payment ledger (5 nines availability, external consistency). Cloud SQL HA + cross-region replica for non-critical data (manual failover, 4 nines).

Networking: Two Cloud Interconnect connections to separate availability zones per region. HA VPN as tertiary failover path. Global Load Balancer's Anycast IP ensures traffic is always routed to a healthy backend.

Dependency management: Circuit breakers on all downstream dependencies (partner APIs, banking networks). Graceful degradation — if a fraud check API is unavailable, route payments to manual review queue rather than failing the transaction.

SLO tracking: Cloud Monitoring SLO dashboards with burn rate alerts. Error budget policy — if error budget is consumed >50% in a week, all non-critical feature work pauses until reliability is restored.
GCP20 How do you build a platform engineering practice from scratch in a financial services organisation?
Platform DesignSenior
▼
Platform engineering is fundamentally about removing toil from application teams and providing a productive, secure paved road to production.

Month 1-2 — Discovery: Survey all application teams. What takes the most time? (Answer: typically getting a new service to production — provisioning, CI/CD setup, security reviews). What are the biggest pain points? Map current state: how long does it take from code commit to production deployment today? What's the current DORA metric baseline (deployment frequency, lead time, MTTR)?

Month 3-4 — First golden path: Build one opinionated path from scratch (new Cloud Run service) that covers: project provisioning (Terraform), GitHub repo template, CI/CD pipeline template (GitHub Actions with OIDC, Terraform, Docker, Cloud Run deploy), monitoring dashboard template, and runbook template. Launch with 2-3 volunteer teams. Gather feedback aggressively. Fix everything that is wrong.

Month 5-12 — Scale and broaden: Expand golden paths to cover all common patterns (GKE, Cloud SQL, BigQuery). Build self-service portal (Backstage). Measure DORA metrics continuously — platform value is measured by improvements to developer throughput, not by platform uptime.
