FO1 What is FinOps and what is the FinOps lifecycle on GCP?
OverviewCore
▼
FinOps (Financial Operations for cloud) is a set of practices that bring financial accountability to the variable spending model of cloud. It is not just cost cutting — it is about enabling engineering, finance, and business teams to make informed tradeoff decisions between speed, cost, and quality.

FinOps lifecycle (3 phases, iterative):
(1) Inform: Create visibility into cloud spending. Who is spending what, on which services, for which applications? Enable: billing export to BigQuery, tagging/labelling, cost dashboards per team/application, anomaly alerts.
(2) Optimise: Identify and implement savings opportunities. Right-size underutilised resources. Purchase Committed Use Discounts for stable workloads. Eliminate waste (orphaned resources, idle environments). Optimise architecture for cost (e.g., Cloud Run vs GKE for spiky workloads).
(3) Operate: Establish processes and culture for continuous cost management. Cost reviews in sprint retrospectives. Budget alerts per team. Cost SLOs (cost per transaction, cost per API call). Incentivise teams to optimise (chargeback, showback models).

GCP-specific FinOps capabilities: Billing export to BigQuery (granular cost data), Recommender API (automated savings recommendations), Active Assist (proactive cost optimization suggestions), Cost Table reports, Cost breakdown reports in Cloud Console, Quota management.
FO2 How do you implement a comprehensive GCP cost attribution model using labels and billing export?
Cost AttributionIntermediate
▼
Cost attribution maps every GCP cost to the team, application, and environment that incurred it — enabling chargeback (teams pay for their usage) or showback (teams see their costs without direct payment).

Labelling strategy: Define mandatory labels enforced by OPA/Conftest in Terraform CI/CD: team (engineering team responsible), application (service or product name), environment (dev/staging/prod), cost-centre (finance mapping for chargeback), data-classification (for compliance). Apply at both the project level (default labels) and resource level.

Billing export to BigQuery: Enable billing export: detailed usage and cost (includes resource labels, SKU, region, project). Enable pricing export (list and contracted prices). Schema: billing_account_id, project.id, resource.labels, service.description, sku.description, usage.amount, cost. Refresh: 24-hour delay for standard, near-real-time for detailed export.

Attribution queries: Cost per team: SELECT resource.labels.team, SUM(cost) FROM billing_export GROUP BY team. Cost per environment: filter WHERE resource.labels.environment = 'prod'. Cost trend: compare SUM(cost) by week over 13 weeks to identify anomalies. Unattributed spend: WHERE resource.labels.team IS NULL — identify labelling gaps.

Dashboard: Looker Studio or Grafana connected to BigQuery billing export. Per-team dashboard with: monthly spend, spend vs budget, top 5 cost SKUs, spend trend, anomaly highlights.
FO3 What are GCP Committed Use Discounts and how do you plan CUD purchases?
CUD PlanningIntermediate
▼
Committed Use Discounts (CUDs) offer discounted prices in exchange for a 1-year or 3-year commitment to a minimum usage level. They do not reserve capacity — they are a billing commitment.

Types of CUDs:
(1) Resource-based CUDs: Commit to a specific amount of vCPU and memory in a region. Apply to GCE VMs, GKE nodes, Cloud SQL, AlloyDB. Discount: 37% for 1-year, 55% for 3-year on on-demand price.
(2) Spend-based CUDs: Commit to spending a minimum dollar amount per month on specific services (Cloud Run, Cloud SQL, Vertex AI). Discount: 17% for 1-year, 22% for 3-year. Simpler — no need to specify resource configuration. Good for Cloud Run where resource allocation varies.

CUD planning process:
(1) Analyse 90 days of billing data to identify the stable baseline usage (the minimum usage that is consistently exceeded — not the peak). Commit only to the baseline, not the peak. If you commit to peak and usage drops, you pay for unused commitment.
(2) Build a CUD recommendation using the GCP Recommender API — it analyzes your historical usage and suggests the optimal CUD commitment amount.
(3) Model the savings: current on-demand spend - (committed spend + savings from CUD).
(4) Purchase: Console → Billing → Commitments. Commitments are per-billing-account, per-region.

CUD + Spot VMs combination: CUDs for baseline compute (predictable, stable). Spot VMs for burst and batch (unpredictable, interruptible). Maximises savings across both steady-state and variable workloads.
FO4 How do you build a FinOps practice in a large engineering organisation that has no existing cost culture?
FinOps CultureSenior
▼
Building a FinOps practice in an organisation without cost culture is fundamentally a change management challenge, not a technical one. Technical tools are necessary but not sufficient.

Phase 1 — Visibility (month 1-2): Without visibility, teams cannot be held accountable. Enable billing export, implement labelling (use OPA in CI/CD to enforce labels — silent at first, just report). Build a cost dashboard visible to all teams. Publish the first 'Cost League Table' — which team is spending what. Visibility alone motivates behaviour change.

Phase 2 — Accountability (month 3-4): Introduce showback — teams receive a monthly cost report for their workloads. Not chargeback yet (no actual payment transfer) but teams see 'you spent $45,000 on Cloud SQL this month.' Appoint a FinOps champion in each team (usually a tech lead). Include cost review in sprint retrospectives.

Phase 3 — Optimisation (month 5-6): Run a 'Cloud Efficiency Sprint' — dedicated 2-week sprint to implement Recommender suggestions. Set team cost efficiency targets (cost per transaction or cost per API call, not absolute spend — teams should not be punished for growing). Quick wins: delete orphaned disks, implement auto-shutdown for dev environments, rightsize over-provisioned Cloud SQL.

Phase 4 — Culture (ongoing): FinOps KPIs in team OKRs. Engineer who achieves the largest cost savings recognised publicly. New service requires cost projection before approval.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your $500,000 annual savings at Scotiabank is proof this approach works — use it as your case study.
FO5 How do you identify and eliminate cloud waste on GCP?
Waste EliminationIntermediate
▼
Cloud waste falls into several categories, each requiring different tools to identify.

Idle/orphaned resources (immediate wins): Unattached persistent disks (PDs): Recommender → VM Disk Idleness Recommendations. Common after VM deletion — disks remain and accrue charges. Static IP addresses not attached to a VM: billing report filtering on 'Static IP' SKU. Old GCS buckets with no recent access: lifecycle policies + Object Lifecycle Analysis. Unused Cloud SQL instances: Recommender → Idle Custom Instance Recommendations. Cost: orphaned resources typically represent 5-15% of total spend.

Oversized resources (right-sizing): VM Rightsizing Recommender: suggests smaller machine types for underutilised VMs (CPU or memory underutilisation). Cloud SQL Recommender: smaller instance class for idle database instances. GKE: VPA (Vertical Pod Autoscaler) in Off mode shows recommended vs actual resource requests — over-provisioned requests waste node capacity. Cloud Run: memory utilisation metrics — reduce memory limit if P99 memory usage is <50% of allocated.

Non-prod environments running unnecessarily: Dev and staging environments should not run 24/7. Cloud Scheduler + Cloud Run Jobs or Terraform to scale down non-prod workloads outside business hours. GKE: scale Deployments to 0 replicas. Cloud SQL: stop instances on a schedule. Savings: 50-65% reduction on non-prod compute costs.

Tiered storage not applied: GCS: ensure lifecycle policies move objects to Nearline (30+ day access) → Coldline (90+ day) → Archive (365+ day). BigQuery: long-term storage pricing applies automatically after 90 days without modification.
FO6 How do you implement budget alerts and cost anomaly detection on GCP?
Budget AlertsSenior
▼
Budget alerts and anomaly detection are the early warning system for FinOps — catching cost surprises before they become billing shocks.

GCP Budget Alerts (native): Create budgets at billing account or project level. Set thresholds: 50%, 90%, 100%, 120% of budget → email notification to billing team and department head. Also configure Pub/Sub notification at 100% → Cloud Function → Slack message to the engineering team → PagerDuty alert if the team has a cost SLA.

Manage via Terraform: google_billing_budget Terraform resource. All budgets in code — prevents ad-hoc budget creation, ensures naming convention. Budget per team (project-scoped) + budget per environment (label-filtered). Budget amounts updated quarterly based on capacity planning.

Cost anomaly detection: GCP Active Assist cost anomaly detection: automatically detects unusual spending patterns and sends alerts to Billing Admins. For custom anomaly detection: BigQuery time-series query on daily costs by SKU and project. Calculate rolling average and standard deviation over the past 30 days. Alert if today's cost exceeds mean + 2 standard deviations. Implemented as a scheduled BigQuery query + Cloud Function → Slack.

Incident response for cost anomalies: Have a runbook for cost incidents: (1) Identify the resource (billing export query by SKU, project, resource label). (2) Identify the cause (deployment of a new service? traffic spike? runaway loop creating resources?). (3) Immediately stop or scale down the offending resource. (4) Post-mortem with the owning team. (5) Implement a guardrail to prevent recurrence.

FO7 What is GCP Spot (Preemptible) VM and how do you use it cost-effectively?
Spot VMsCore
▼
Spot VMs (formerly Preemptible VMs) are GCE instances at up to 91% discount compared to on-demand pricing. In exchange, Google may reclaim (preempt) the VM with 30 seconds notice when capacity is needed.

Pricing: Spot price varies dynamically based on supply and demand by machine type and region — unlike AWS Spot, GCP Spot VMs do not have a bidding mechanism. You pay the current spot price and Google may preempt at any time. Maximum 24-hour runtime (preempted after 24 hours even if not reclaimed earlier).

Suitable workloads: Batch jobs (data processing, ETL, ML training — if the job is checkpointed and can resume). Stateless workers (no local state — Cloud Run jobs or GKE batch workers). Dev/test environments (interruption is acceptable during off-peak hours). Fault-tolerant distributed computing (the job continues with remaining nodes if one is preempted).

NOT suitable for: Production web servers, databases, stateful services requiring continuous availability.

Implementation patterns:
(1) GKE Spot node pool: Create a separate node pool with spot VMs. Use taints and tolerations to pin batch workloads to the spot pool. Production workloads taint-tolerated only on on-demand pool.
(2) Checkpointing for ML training: Vertex AI Training automatically checkpoints model weights to GCS. If the spot VM is preempted, training resumes from the last checkpoint on a new instance.
(3) Mixed pools: On-demand base pool for critical pods + spot pool for batch. Pod affinity prefers spot, falls back to on-demand if no spot capacity.
FO8 How do you implement GCP resource quotas to prevent runaway costs and accidental over-provisioning?
Resource QuotasIntermediate
▼
GCP quotas limit how much of a resource you can use — they are both a safety mechanism (prevent accidental over-provisioning) and a capacity management tool.

Types of quotas:
(1) Regional quotas: Per-project, per-region limits on resource counts (e.g., 64 vCPUs per region). Prevents a single runaway Terraform apply from provisioning 500 GCE instances.
(2) Service-specific quotas: API call rate limits, BigQuery slot quotas, Vertex AI concurrent requests.
(3) Organisation quotas: Set in Org Policies as constraints — max machine types, max persistent disk size, max disk count per VM.

FinOps use of quotas: Set project-level CPU quotas for each environment: dev (16 vCPUs), staging (64 vCPUs), production (256 vCPUs). Engineering teams cannot exceed these without a quota increase request — forces intentional scaling decisions. Prevents the most common cost accident: a developer accidentally loops terraform apply creating hundreds of VMs.

Quota increase workflow: Team submits a quota increase request. Platform team reviews: is the increase justified by capacity plan? Is it in the right environment (prod, not dev)? Approves or rejects. Approved increases are applied via Terraform (gcp_service_quota Terraform resource).

Monitoring quota utilisation: Cloud Monitoring: serviceruntime.googleapis.com/quota/usage vs serviceruntime.googleapis.com/quota/limit. Alert at 80% quota utilisation — prevents hitting quota limits in production and causing service disruption.
FO9 How do you design a GCP cost allocation model for a multi-tenant SaaS platform?
Multi-Tenant CostSenior
▼
A SaaS platform serving multiple customers (tenants) needs per-tenant cost visibility to: (1) price the product correctly, (2) identify unprofitable customers, (3) attribute engineering effort to revenue.

Tenant isolation models and their cost attribution implications:
(1) Project-per-tenant: Each customer gets a dedicated GCP project. Cost attribution is exact — billing export per project = cost per tenant. Simple and clean. Works at up to ~1,000 tenants (GCP supports up to 3,000 projects per organisation by default).
(2) Namespace-per-tenant (GKE): All tenants in one GKE cluster, namespace-per-tenant. GKE usage metering attributes compute costs to namespaces. Export to BigQuery — query cost by namespace (= tenant).
(3) Label-per-tenant (most common for large scale): Resources labelled with tenant_id. Billing export + BigQuery query aggregates cost by tenant_id label. Works for all resource types. Requires consistent labelling — missing labels create unattributed costs.

Profitability analysis: BigQuery view: JOIN billing_export (cost) with CRM data (revenue per tenant). Calculate gross margin per tenant. Identify: high-usage, low-revenue tenants (repricing opportunity), low-usage, high-revenue tenants (upsell opportunity), unexpectedly expensive tenants (architectural issue).

Showback to customers: Enterprise customers often want a cost breakdown of their usage. Build a tenant-facing dashboard (Looker Embedded) showing their consumption by service.
FO10 How do you evaluate the cost efficiency of serverless (Cloud Run) vs container (GKE) for a given workload?
Cloud Run vs GKE CostSenior
▼
The Cloud Run vs GKE cost decision depends on traffic pattern, resource utilisation, and operational overhead amortisation.

Cloud Run pricing model: Pay per request: CPU allocated only during request processing (default). Cost: CPU (vCPU-seconds) + memory (GiB-seconds) + requests. If traffic drops to zero, cost drops to zero (scale to zero). CPU always-on option available (same cost model as GKE for always-running services).

GKE pricing model: Pay for nodes continuously (GKE Autopilot pays per pod, but still continuous). Node cost is fixed regardless of whether requests are being processed. Overhead: cluster management fee ($0.10/hour for Standard, included in Autopilot). More efficient when pods are densely packed (high utilisation of node CPU/memory).

When Cloud Run wins on cost: Spiky or unpredictable traffic patterns. Low baseline traffic with occasional spikes. Dev/staging environments (scale to zero overnight). Workloads where actual CPU usage is <30% of wall-clock time.

When GKE wins on cost: High sustained traffic (Cloud Run request-based billing becomes expensive). Workloads with consistently high CPU utilisation across 24 hours. Multiple services that can be bin-packed on the same nodes. GPU workloads.

Break-even analysis: Model both: Cloud Run cost = estimated monthly requests × avg CPU-seconds × per vCPU-second price. GKE cost = nodes × hours × node price + cluster fee. Run the calculation for your actual traffic profile. At ~100M requests/month of medium CPU workload, GKE typically becomes cheaper.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank $500k savings work — if any of that involved Cloud Run vs GKE decisions, describe the analysis.

Core
IAP1What is Identity-Aware Proxy (IAP) and what problem does it solve?
OverviewCore
▼
Identity-Aware Proxy (IAP) is a Google Cloud service that enforces access control based on user identity and context, not network location. It acts as a smart gatekeeper in front of your applications — replacing the traditional VPN model where 'inside the network = trusted'.

Problem it solves: Traditionally, internal applications were protected by VPN. Anyone on the corporate VPN had access to all internal tools — a compromised device or stolen VPN credentials gave broad access. IAP replaces this with per-application, per-user access control enforced at the application layer.

How it works: (1) User requests access to an IAP-protected resource. (2) IAP intercepts the request, redirects unauthenticated users to Google sign-in. (3) After authentication, IAP checks whether the user's identity and context (device state, IP, time) meet the configured access policy. (4) Only if all conditions pass does IAP forward the request to the backend application — otherwise 403.

Supported backends: App Engine, Cloud Run, GCE VMs (via IAP-secured tunnelling), GKE, and on-premises apps (via IAP connector).

Key benefit: Zero-trust access to internal tools without VPN. Engineers access dashboards, internal portals, and admin UIs from any device, anywhere — access is granted based on who they are and the state of their device, not where they are.
IAP2How do you architect IAP to protect a Cloud Run internal admin portal?
ArchitectureIntermediate
▼
Architecture flow: Browser → Global External Load Balancer → IAP → Cloud Run service. IAP sits between the LB and Cloud Run — the Cloud Run service itself is not directly exposed.

Setup steps:
(1) Configure Cloud Run to require authentication (not allow unauthenticated invocations). Set ingress to 'Allow internal traffic and traffic from Cloud Load Balancing' — this prevents direct internet access to the Cloud Run URL, routing everything through IAP.
(2) Create a backend service in Cloud Load Balancing pointing to a serverless NEG (Network Endpoint Group) targeting the Cloud Run service.
(3) Enable IAP on the backend service. Configure the OAuth consent screen and IAP client credentials.
(4) Add IAM bindings: grant roles/iap.httpsResourceAccessor to the groups of users who should have access (e.g., platform-engineers@company.com). Access can also be granted per-user.

Bonus — context-aware access: Integrate IAP with Access Context Manager to require a specific device policy (managed device, OS up to date) before granting access — this is BeyondCorp in practice.

Request headers: IAP injects the verified user identity into request headers (X-Goog-IAP-JWT-Assertion). The backend application can read this to know who is making the request without implementing its own auth.
IAP3How do IAP access policies work — what are the components?
Access ControlIntermediate
▼
IAP access control has three components that must all be satisfied:

(1) IAM binding (who can access): roles/iap.httpsResourceAccessor grants access to an IAP resource. Bind this role to users, groups, or service accounts at the resource level (a specific Cloud Run service), project level, or organisation level. Remove the binding to instantly revoke access for a user — far faster and simpler than VPN certificate revocation.

(2) Access levels (context of the request): Defined in Access Context Manager. Conditions include: device policy (managed device, screen lock, OS version, disk encryption), IP range (office IPs, corporate VPN range), and geographic restrictions. IAP checks access levels in addition to IAM — the user must pass both.

(3) Authentication: IAP uses Google Identity for authentication. Users authenticate with their Cloud Identity account (via corporate SSO if federated). Service-to-service access uses signed JWT tokens with service account identity.

Per-resource granularity: IAP access policies are configured per backend service. Different teams can have access to different internal apps using different access policies — all enforced by IAP without any application code changes.
IAP4What is IAP TCP tunnelling and how does it replace VPN for SSH/RDP access?
TCP TunnellingSenior
▼
IAP TCP tunnelling creates an encrypted tunnel from a developer's machine to a VM's port (typically SSH port 22 or RDP port 3389) via Google's infrastructure — without the VM needing a public IP or VPN.

How it works: The developer runs gcloud compute ssh instance-name --tunnel-through-iap. gcloud opens an HTTPS connection to the IAP endpoint. IAP authenticates the user (checks IAM + access level). If authorised, IAP proxies the TCP connection to the VM's SSH port. The VM never needs to be internet-accessible.

Security benefits vs traditional bastion host:
(1) No bastion to manage: The IAP service is the bastion. No extra VM to patch, monitor, or secure.
(2) No public IP on the target VM: The VM only has a private IP. Attack surface is dramatically reduced.
(3) Identity-verified access: SSH access is gated on Google Identity + context-aware access. Org Admins can audit and revoke access centrally.
(4) Full audit trail: Every IAP tunnel session is logged in Cloud Audit Logs with the user's identity, timestamp, source IP, and target instance.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: This is directly relevant to your Zero Trust work at Scotiabank — IAP TCP tunnelling is a concrete implementation of the 'never trust the network' principle.
IAP5How do you prevent IAP bypass — what are the common pitfalls and how do you harden IAP?
SecuritySenior
▼
IAP is only as strong as the configuration around it. These are the common bypass vectors and how to prevent each:

Bypass 1 — Direct access to the backend URL: Cloud Run services have their own *.run.app URL. If this is publicly accessible, an attacker bypasses IAP entirely. Fix: Set Cloud Run ingress to 'internal' or 'internal and load balancing only'. Deny unauthenticated invocations. The direct URL then returns 403.

Bypass 2 — Missing X-Forwarded-For validation: An attacker behind the LB could forge the X-Goog-IAP-JWT-Assertion header. Fix: The backend application must validate the IAP JWT signature (using Google's public keys) — don't blindly trust the header. Use the IAP client libraries which handle this.

Bypass 3 — Over-broad IAM binding: Granting roles/iap.httpsResourceAccessor to allAuthenticatedUsers or the entire organisation effectively disables access control. Fix: Grant at the minimum scope — specific groups, not the whole org.

Bypass 4 — Service account with direct API access: A service account with roles/run.invoker can call the Cloud Run service directly, bypassing IAP. Fix: Use VPC Service Controls to prevent direct API calls. Audit service account permissions regularly.

Bypass 5 — Missing OS Login for SSH: If IAP TCP tunnelling is used but OS Login is not enforced, an attacker with IAP access can SSH using any key stored on the VM. Fix: Enable OS Login (enable-oslogin=true) — only IAM-authorised users can get an OS account.
IAP6How do services authenticate to IAP-protected backends programmatically?
Service-to-ServiceIntermediate
▼
Service-to-service calls to IAP-protected resources require an OIDC token, not just a service account's normal access token.

How it works: IAP validates an OIDC ID token, not an OAuth2 access token. The calling service must generate an ID token specifically for the IAP client ID (the OAuth client associated with the IAP resource).

Steps:
(1) The calling service's service account needs roles/iap.httpsResourceAccessor on the IAP resource.
(2) Generate an OIDC ID token with the target IAP client ID as the audience: fetch the metadata server's identity endpoint with audience=IAP_CLIENT_ID. On GCE/Cloud Run this is available at http://metadata.google.internal/computeMetadata/v1/instance/service-accounts/default/identity?audience=IAP_CLIENT_ID.
(3) Include the ID token in the Authorization: Bearer header of the outgoing HTTP request.
(4) IAP validates the token signature and audience, checks the service account's IAM binding, and forwards the request if authorised.

Use case example: A Cloud Run service (upstream) needs to call an internal admin Cloud Run service (downstream, IAP-protected). The upstream service fetches an OIDC token for the downstream IAP client ID and includes it in the request. IAP validates it and the downstream service receives the verified identity in the X-Goog-IAP-JWT-Assertion header.
IAP7How do you monitor and audit IAP access in a production environment?
MonitoringIntermediate
▼
Cloud Audit Logs: IAP generates Admin Activity logs (configuration changes) and Data Access logs (access attempts). Enable Data Access logs for IAP in Cloud Logging — these capture every access check: who requested access, which resource, what access level was applied, and whether access was granted or denied.

Key log fields to monitor: principal_email (who is accessing), http_request.request_url (what they accessed), iap_audit_data.device_state (device compliance status), severity:ERROR (access denied events).

Alerting scenarios:
(1) Repeated access denied: Multiple 403 responses for one user within 10 minutes could indicate a compromised account trying to access something it shouldn't. Alert security team.
(2) Access from unusual geography: User accessing IAP resource from a new country. Especially suspicious for privileged admin portals.
(3) Device compliance failure: IAP access denied due to non-compliant device — may indicate a user bypassing endpoint verification.
(4) Configuration changes: Any change to IAP policy (access level modification, IAM binding addition) generates Admin Activity log — alert in near-real-time for production IAP resources.

Dashboard: Build a Cloud Monitoring dashboard or Looker Studio report on IAP access patterns. Baseline normal access patterns — identify anomalies as deviations from baseline.
IAP8How do you migrate a VPN-dependent organisation to IAP — what is the transition strategy?
MigrationSenior
▼
Migrating from VPN to IAP is a cultural and technical change. Engineers who are used to 'connect VPN, then access anything' need to shift to per-application access requests.

Phase 1 — Audit and catalogue: Map all internal applications currently accessed via VPN. Categorise: web apps (good candidates for IAP HTTPS), SSH/RDP (good for IAP TCP tunnelling), legacy on-premises (may need IAP connector). Identify the user groups that need access to each application.

Phase 2 — IAP alongside VPN (parallel): Deploy IAP for web applications without removing VPN. Both paths work — engineers can access the same apps via VPN or via IAP (direct). This builds confidence and familiarity without disrupting productivity. Run for 4-8 weeks, monitor IAP adoption metrics.

Phase 3 — VPN restriction: Start requiring IAP for new applications — no new apps get VPN-accessible routes. Enforce context-aware access (device policy) on IAP. Begin deprecation notices for VPN access to specific applications.

Phase 4 — VPN elimination: Remove VPN access routes to applications that are fully IAP-protected. Provide runbooks for common access patterns (SSH, database access, admin portals). Maintain VPN only for legacy systems that cannot be IAP-protected.

Key success factor: Developer tooling. If IAP SSH access requires 3 more steps than VPN SSH, engineers will resist. Invest in gcloud wrappers, IDE plugins, and documentation.
IAP9How do you design an IAP architecture for a multi-region platform?
Multi-RegionSenior
▼
IAP with the Global External Application Load Balancer: The recommended multi-region IAP architecture uses the global LB. IAP is enabled on the backend service (not per-region) — IAP policy evaluation happens at Google's edge PoPs, close to the user.

Multi-region backend services: The global LB backend service can point to Cloud Run NEGs in multiple regions. IAP policy is consistent across all regions — the same access level and IAM bindings apply regardless of which region serves the request. This is a significant advantage over per-region deployments where you'd have to synchronise IAP config across regions.

Failover behaviour: Because IAP is evaluated at the global LB layer (not in the application), IAP failover is handled automatically alongside backend health-based failover. If us-central1 backends fail, traffic moves to northamerica-northeast1 with no IAP re-configuration needed.

Session persistence: IAP uses JWT tokens with a 1-hour validity. If a user's session crosses a regional failover, their IAP token remains valid in the new region — no re-authentication needed.

Limitation — IAP TCP tunnelling: TCP tunnelling is region-specific — you tunnel to a specific VM in a specific zone. There is no global TCP tunnelling. For multi-region VM access, engineers need to be aware of which region they're targeting.
IAP10How does IAP integrate with BeyondCorp Enterprise for a complete zero-trust access architecture?
BeyondCorp IntegrationSenior
▼
IAP is the enforcement point for BeyondCorp in GCP. BeyondCorp Enterprise adds advanced context-aware access capabilities on top of IAP's core functionality.

BeyondCorp Enterprise adds:
(1) Chrome Device Trust: Endpoint Verification (Google's device agent) collects device signals. BeyondCorp Enterprise uses these signals to define and enforce device trust levels — a higher level than the standard Cloud Identity Endpoint Verification.
(2) Risk-based access: Machine learning models assess the risk of each access attempt based on user behaviour, device health, and contextual signals. High-risk requests are challenged with additional MFA even if they meet the base access level.
(3) Threat and data protection: Integration with Chrome browser for DLP on web applications — prevent copy-paste of sensitive data, block downloads, watermark sensitive content viewed through IAP-protected apps.
(4) Reporting and analytics: BeyondCorp Enterprise dashboard shows all access attempts, device compliance posture, and blocked requests across all IAP-protected resources.

Integration architecture: Access Context Manager access levels → IAP (enforcement) → BeyondCorp Enterprise (device trust + risk scoring + DLP). Each layer adds a dimension of trust verification. The user's request must pass all layers before reaching the application.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank Zero Trust work is the foundation — position BeyondCorp as the Google-managed implementation of the same principles you designed from scratch.

📋 Org Policy & Security Command Center Q&As

10 questions covering Org Policy constraints · Custom constraints · SCC findings · SCC Premium · SIEM integration · Compliance as code

Click any question to expand the model answer. Senior questions are the differentiating ones that set Platform Architect candidates apart.
10
Questions
4
Senior
4
Intermediate
2
Core
OP1What is GCP Organisation Policy and how does it differ from IAM?
Org Policy BasicsCore
▼
Organisation Policy is a configuration constraint system that defines what can be done with GCP resources — regardless of who is asking. It enforces rules on resource configuration at creation time or continuously.

The key distinction from IAM: IAM answers 'Can user X perform action Y?' Organisation Policy answers 'Can action Y be performed at all in this environment?' These are complementary controls.

Example: A developer has roles/compute.instanceAdmin (IAM — they can create VMs). But an Org Policy constraint/compute.vmExternalIpAccess is set to DENY. Result: the developer can create VMs, but all VMs created in this environment will have no external IP — regardless of what they request. IAM cannot override Org Policy.

Constraint types:
(1) Boolean constraints: On or off. Example: constraints/compute.disableSerialPortAccess = true.
(2) List constraints: Specify allowed or denied values. Example: constraints/gcp.resourceLocations ALLOW [northamerica-northeast1, northamerica-northeast2].

Inheritance: Policies set at the organisation level inherit to all folders and projects. Policies can be set at lower levels — inheritance mode: MERGE (combines with parent) or REPLACE (overrides parent). REPLACE can only make policies more restrictive, not more permissive, unless the constraint allows override.
OP2What are the most important Org Policy constraints to enforce in a financial services GCP organisation?
Key ConstraintsIntermediate
▼
For a regulated financial institution, these constraints form the baseline security posture:

Network security:
- constraints/compute.vmExternalIpAccess: Deny external IP on all VMs — all workloads must use private IPs.
- constraints/compute.restrictSharedVpcSubnetworks: Only approved subnets can be used in service projects — prevents teams from creating their own subnets outside your IP plan.
- constraints/compute.restrictCloudNATUsage: Control which projects can use Cloud NAT — prevent uncontrolled internet egress.

Identity and access:
- constraints/iam.allowedPolicyMemberDomains: Only corporate domain identities can be granted IAM — prevents @gmail.com bindings.
- constraints/iam.disableServiceAccountKeyCreation: Block service account key creation org-wide — force Workload Identity Federation.
- constraints/iam.disableServiceAccountCreation: In sensitive projects, prevent any service account creation (only pre-approved SAs allowed).

Resource location:
- constraints/gcp.resourceLocations: Allow only northamerica-northeast1/2 (Canada data sovereignty for OSFI B-13).

Compute hardening:
- constraints/compute.requireShieldedVm: All VMs must have Secure Boot, vTPM, and integrity monitoring enabled.
- constraints/compute.disableSerialPortAccess: Block serial port access — common backdoor vector.

Storage:
- constraints/storage.uniformBucketLevelAccess: Enforce uniform IAM on all GCS buckets — no legacy ACLs.
- constraints/storage.publicAccessPrevention: Prevent any GCS bucket from being made public.
OP3How do you manage Organisation Policies as code using Terraform?
Terraform ManagementIntermediate
▼
Managing Org Policies via Terraform ensures they are version-controlled, peer-reviewed, and consistently applied — not set ad-hoc through the console.

Terraform resources:
(1) google_org_policy_policy (recommended): The newer, more flexible resource supporting all constraint types.
(2) google_organization_policy (legacy): Still works but deprecated in favour of the above.

Structure example for a financial institution:
A dedicated foundation Terraform module contains all org-level policies. A sub-module applies environment-specific policies at the folder level (NonProd folder can have looser policies for dev experiments, Prod folder has the strictest set).

Inheritance control via Terraform: Use the inherit_from_parent field on resource policies and the reset field to create clean overrides. Document clearly where each policy is set and what it overrides.

Org Policy Factory pattern: Define all policies in a YAML file (policy-catalog.yaml) with fields: constraint, scope (org/folder/project), enforcement, allowed_values. A Terraform local maps this YAML to google_org_policy_policy resources. New policies are added by PR to the YAML file — platform team reviews and merges.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank IaC governance work — this is exactly the pattern you would have implemented for org-level guardrails.
OP4What are Custom Organisation Policy constraints and when would you create one?
Custom ConstraintsSenior
▼
Custom constraints allow you to define your own enforcement rules beyond Google's built-in constraints. They evaluate the resource configuration at creation/update time using CEL (Common Expression Language) and can block or allow resource creation based on any property of the resource payload.

When to use custom constraints:
(1) Enforce naming conventions: All GCS buckets in production must start with 'prod-'. Custom constraint evaluates resource.name.startsWith('prod-').
(2) Require specific labels: All Cloud Run services must have the 'team' label present. Custom constraint evaluates 'team' in resource.labels.
(3) Enforce specific settings: All Cloud SQL instances must have deletion_protection enabled. Custom constraint evaluates resource.settings.deletionProtectionEnabled == true.
(4) Restrict machine type families: Only N2 or C2 machine types allowed in production — block E2 (burstable) instances.

How they work: Custom constraints are associated with a specific GCP resource type (e.g., sqladmin.googleapis.com/Instance). When a resource of that type is created or updated, the constraint CEL expression is evaluated against the API request payload. If the expression returns false and the policy is DENY, the API request is rejected.

Limitation: Custom constraints only apply at resource creation/update time — they do not retroactively fix existing resources. Audit existing resources separately.
OP5What is Security Command Center and what value does it provide to a platform architect?
SCC OverviewCore
▼
Security Command Center (SCC) is GCP's centralised security management and risk visibility platform. It aggregates security findings from across the entire GCP organisation into a single view — vulnerabilities, misconfigurations, threats, and compliance posture.

Value to a Platform Architect:
(1) Continuous misconfiguration detection: Security Health Analytics scans every GCP resource and flags violations of security best practices — 24/7, without manual audits. You get alerted when someone creates a public GCS bucket or adds a primitive IAM role.
(2) Compliance posture: SCC maps findings to PCI-DSS, CIS GCP Benchmark, ISO 27001, NIST 800-53. As a Platform Architect, you can show CISO and QSA a real-time compliance dashboard — not a point-in-time spreadsheet.
(3) Threat detection: Event Threat Detection analyses Cloud Audit Logs for malicious activity patterns — cryptocurrency mining, data exfiltration, lateral movement. These are threats your firewalls don't see.
(4) Governance metrics: SCC findings are the KPI for your security posture. Reducing findings from 200 to 120 over a quarter is a measurable outcome you can present to leadership.

Editions: SCC Standard (free, limited findings), SCC Premium (additional threat intelligence, compliance frameworks, Rapid Vulnerability Detection). Regulated financial institutions should use Premium.
OP6How do you operationalise SCC findings — what is your process for triage and remediation?
SCC FindingsIntermediate
▼
SCC findings without a response process are just noise. The goal is to turn findings into tracked, assigned, time-bounded remediation actions.

Finding severity triage:
- Critical: Public GCS bucket with sensitive data, IAM Anomaly (unusual access pattern), Malware in a running container. Fix within 4 hours, treat as security incident.
- High: Primitive IAM role (Owner/Editor) assigned, service account key in use, public IP on a database. Fix within 24 hours.
- Medium: Unencrypted GCS bucket, unused service account keys, disabled audit logs on a project. Fix within 1 week.
- Low: Unused service accounts, non-Shielded VMs, missing labels. Fix within 1 month during sprint capacity planning.

Automation via Pub/Sub: SCC can publish findings to a Pub/Sub topic in real-time. A Cloud Function subscribes, categorises the finding, and: (1) Creates a Jira ticket assigned to the project owner. (2) Sends a Slack message to the team channel for High/Critical. (3) Pages on-call for Critical findings.

Tracking: Export all findings to BigQuery via a Log Sink. Build a remediation SLA dashboard: average time to fix by severity, findings opened vs closed per week, top recurring finding types (points to systemic issues to fix in Terraform templates).

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your 40% audit finding reduction at Scotiabank — SCC findings management is the operational process behind that metric.
OP7What does SCC Premium offer beyond the Standard tier, and how would you justify the cost?
SCC PremiumSenior
▼
SCC Premium adds:

(1) Web Security Scanner: Automated scanning of web applications (App Engine, GKE-hosted apps) for OWASP Top 10 vulnerabilities — XSS, SQL injection, mixed content. Runs continuously, not on demand.
(2) Container Threat Detection: Monitors running containers for: added malicious binaries, modified library code at runtime, reverse shell spawning. Standard tier doesn't detect runtime threats.
(3) Virtual Machine Threat Detection (VMTD): Analyses memory of running VMs for rootkits and malicious code — without installing any agent. Unique to Google, runs at the hypervisor level.
(4) Compliance monitoring against frameworks: PCI-DSS v4.0, CIS GCP Benchmark, NIST 800-53, ISO 27001. Standard only covers CIS Benchmark basics. Premium maps findings to specific control requirements — QSA-ready output.
(5) Rapid Vulnerability Detection: Active network scanning to detect unpatched services and exposed admin interfaces — identifies vulnerabilities before attackers do.

Cost justification for financial services: At a regulated financial institution, SCC Premium is the automated control testing and evidence generation platform for your compliance program. The cost (typically $0.06/compute asset/month) is far less than the manual effort of quarterly security assessments. One avoided PCI-DSS audit remediation finding saves more than a year of SCC Premium costs.
OP8How do you integrate SCC with your SIEM and incident response platform?
SCC IntegrationSenior
▼
SCC findings are most valuable when they feed into your existing security operations workflow — not as a standalone console engineers have to remember to check.

Pub/Sub export (real-time): Configure SCC to publish all new or updated findings to a Pub/Sub topic. A Cloud Function or Dataflow job processes the stream: enriches findings (adds asset metadata, owner info), normalises to your SIEM's event format (OCSF or CEF), and forwards to Splunk, Chronicle, or another SIEM via the SIEM's ingestion API.

Chronicle integration (native): SCC Premium natively integrates with Google Chronicle SIEM. Findings automatically appear in Chronicle's timeline alongside other security events. Chronicle's YARA-L rules can correlate SCC findings with other signals — e.g., IAM anomaly + unusual network traffic = escalated alert.

BigQuery export (historical): Enable SCC's continuous export to BigQuery. All findings (and their state changes) are in BigQuery. This enables: trend analysis, compliance reporting, custom dashboards in Looker Studio, SQL-based investigation queries.

Jira/ServiceNow integration: Use the Pub/Sub → Cloud Function → Jira API pattern to auto-create tickets. Include: finding title, resource name, project, recommended fix link, and severity. Assign to the project owner (pulled from a resource-owner mapping in Firestore). Track remediation SLA within your normal ticket workflow.

Feedback loop: Mark findings as resolved in SCC when the ticket is closed. This keeps SCC clean and provides accurate metrics for leadership reporting.
OP9What is Organisation Policy Simulator and how do you use it before applying changes?
Policy SimulationIntermediate
▼
The Organisation Policy Simulator lets you preview the impact of a proposed Org Policy change before applying it — identifying which existing resources would be non-compliant with the new policy.

Why this matters: A platform architect changing an Org Policy that applies to the entire organisation can inadvertently break existing workloads. For example, adding constraints/compute.vmExternalIpAccess=DENY might block existing VMs with external IPs from being recreated after maintenance. Policy Simulator reveals this before it happens.

How to use:
(1) In Cloud Console → Org Policies → select a constraint → Test Policy.
(2) Or via the API: POST to orgpolicies.googleapis.com/v2/projects/{project}/policy:simulate with the proposed policy.
(3) The simulator evaluates all existing resources in scope against the proposed constraint. It returns a list of 'would violate' resources with their current configuration.

Workflow integration: In your Terraform PR workflow, add a step that calls the Policy Simulator API with the proposed Org Policy change and includes the simulation output in the PR description. The reviewer sees exactly which resources would be affected before approving the PR.

Limitations: Policy Simulator evaluates resource configurations at the time of simulation — it cannot predict future resources or resource configurations that change at runtime.
OP10How do you detect and alert on Org Policy violations and configuration drift?
Drift DetectionSenior
▼
Org Policies prevent violations at resource creation time — but they cannot retroactively fix resources created before a policy was applied, or resources modified by infrastructure outside Terraform. You need ongoing drift detection.

Layer 1 — SCC Security Health Analytics: SCC continuously evaluates resource configurations against security best practices (many of which map to Org Policy intents). SCC findings such as 'Public bucket' or 'VM with external IP in restricted project' are effectively Org Policy drift alerts.

Layer 2 — Cloud Asset Inventory change feed: Asset Inventory sends real-time change notifications to Pub/Sub when any resource is created or modified. A Cloud Function subscribes and evaluates each change against your policy intents — for example, checking that any new VM has no external IP and has Shielded VM enabled. Non-compliant resources trigger an alert and optionally a remediation action (Terraform plan against the resource to restore compliant state).

Layer 3 — Scheduled compliance queries: BigQuery scheduled query against Asset Inventory exports: 'Find all GCS buckets with uniform access disabled', 'Find all service accounts with keys older than 90 days', 'Find all VMs with external IPs'. Run nightly, generate a compliance delta report showing new violations since yesterday.

Automated remediation: For well-understood violations with safe remediation, automate the fix: a Cloud Function that removes allUsers from a GCS bucket's IAM policy when SCC detects a public bucket finding. Alert the owning team after remediation — not instead of it.

🔗 Private Service Connect & Private Google Access Q&As

PSC1What is Private Service Connect and how does it differ from VPC Peering?
PSC OverviewCore
▼
Private Service Connect (PSC) creates a private endpoint in your VPC that gives access to a specific service — without exposing your entire network or the producer's entire network.

VPC Peering (traditional approach): Creates a full bidirectional route between two VPCs. All resources in VPC A can route to all resources in VPC B (within IAM constraints). Non-transitive (A-B and B-C doesn't give A-C). Route leakage risk — it's hard to control what routes are actually visible. Often over-exposes network topology.

Private Service Connect: Creates a single forwarding rule (PSC endpoint) in your VPC pointing to a specific service, not a whole network. The endpoint appears as a private IP in your VPC. Traffic to that IP goes to the service — nothing else. The producer's VPC topology is completely hidden. Works transitively — the consuming VPC has no insight into how the service is hosted.

PSC use cases:
(1) GCP APIs: Access Google APIs (storage, bigquery, vertex AI) via a private IP in your VPC — no internet, no Public Google Access needed.
(2) Published services: Service A (in project A) publishes itself via a PSC service attachment. Service B (in project B, different VPC) creates a PSC endpoint to consume it. Zero route sharing between VPCs.
(3) Third-party SaaS: Snowflake, Elastic, MongoDB Atlas exposed via PSC — private consumption without peering.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank VPC Service Controls and network segmentation work is the context where PSC adds the service-level granularity.
PSC2What is Private Google Access and what are its modes?
Private Google AccessCore
▼
Private Google Access (PGA) allows VM instances with only private IP addresses to access Google APIs and services (GCS, BigQuery, Pub/Sub, etc.) without needing internet access or a public IP.

How it works (standard PGA): Enable Private Google Access on the subnet (--enable-private-google-access). DNS for Google APIs resolves to internal IP ranges (199.36.153.8/30 for restricted.googleapis.com, 199.36.153.4/30 for private.googleapis.com). Traffic routes through Google's network, not the internet. The VM's traffic never leaves Google's network backbone.

Modes:
(1) private.googleapis.com (default): Access all Google APIs, including unrestricted ones. Traffic stays private but the range of accessible APIs is broad.
(2) restricted.googleapis.com (recommended for regulated environments): Subset of Google APIs that support VPC Service Controls. Routes to a different IP range that VPC-SC perimeters can enforce. Use this in PCI-DSS or sensitive data environments.

vs PSC for Google APIs: PGA is simpler (just a subnet flag) but doesn't let you control the endpoint IP. PSC for Google APIs creates a specific forwarding rule with a custom private IP — more control, better integration with DNS, and required for certain VPC-SC configurations.

Prerequisite: Firewall rules must allow egress to 199.36.153.8/30 (or whatever range your PGA mode uses). No allow-all egress needed — just the specific Google API ranges.
PSC3How do you design a PSC architecture for a microservices platform where teams have separate VPCs?
PSC ArchitectureIntermediate
▼
In a microservices platform with separate VPCs per team (or per workload), PSC is the recommended pattern for service-to-service connectivity because it preserves VPC isolation while enabling selective service exposure.

Producer side (service team):
(1) Service is deployed behind an Internal Load Balancer (Internal Application LB or Internal TCP/UDP LB) in the service's VPC.
(2) A PSC Service Attachment is created referencing the ILB's forwarding rule. This publishes the service for PSC consumption.
(3) The service attachment specifies a consumer accept list (which project/org is allowed to create endpoints) and optionally requires explicit approval for each endpoint request.

Consumer side (calling team):
(1) In the consumer's VPC, create a PSC Forwarding Rule (endpoint) pointing to the producer's service attachment. Specify a reserved private IP in the consumer's VPC for the endpoint.
(2) Create a Cloud DNS private zone entry mapping the service's DNS name to the PSC endpoint IP. Consumers call the service by its DNS name — no hard-coded IPs.
(3) Egress firewall rules: allow traffic from consumer pods/VMs to the PSC endpoint IP on the service port.

Hub-and-spoke PSC pattern: Instead of M×N endpoints (every team needs an endpoint to every service), publish all shared services from the hub project and have each spoke project create endpoints only to the services it needs. Clean, auditable, least-privilege networking.
PSC4How do you configure PSC endpoints for GCP APIs (BigQuery, Vertex AI, GCS) in a regulated environment?
PSC Endpoints for GCP ServicesIntermediate
▼
Configuring PSC endpoints for GCP APIs gives you a specific private IP for each API in your VPC — enabling strict control over which APIs can be accessed and ensuring all traffic stays on your private network.

Why PSC endpoints for GCP APIs over standard PGA:
(1) Custom private IPs — you control the IP addresses assigned to each API endpoint.
(2) Better integration with VPC Service Controls — PSC endpoints for restricted APIs respect VPC-SC perimeters more precisely.
(3) DNS control — you configure DNS privately, making it hard for applications to accidentally route to public API endpoints.
(4) Better auditing — traffic to PSC endpoints generates VPC Flow Logs with the endpoint IP, enabling per-API traffic visibility.

Setup steps:
(1) Reserve a private IP in the consumer VPC for each API endpoint (e.g., 10.0.0.10 for storage.googleapis.com).
(2) Create a PSC forwarding rule: target = googleapis.com service bundle (or individual service), network = your VPC, IP = the reserved private IP.
(3) Create a Cloud DNS private zone for each API: storage.googleapis.com A record → 10.0.0.10. This overrides public DNS for all clients in the VPC — they will only ever resolve to the private endpoint.

In regulated environments: Use restricted.googleapis.com service bundle to ensure only VPC-SC-supported APIs are accessible. This prevents applications from accidentally calling Google APIs outside the VPC-SC perimeter.
PSC5How do VPC Service Controls and Private Service Connect work together for data exfiltration prevention?
VPC-SC and PSCSenior
▼
VPC-SC and PSC are complementary — PSC provides private network paths, VPC-SC provides data perimeter enforcement at the API level. Together they create a two-layer defence against data exfiltration.

The exfiltration scenario PSC alone doesn't prevent: A compromised VM in your VPC has a service account with BigQuery read access. Via Private Google Access, it can query BigQuery in your project (legitimate). It can also query BigQuery in an attacker's project (exfiltration) — the private path doesn't help here because the attacker's project is also accessible via private.googleapis.com.

How VPC-SC closes this: A VPC-SC perimeter wraps your project's BigQuery. Even when requests come from your private IP range (via PGA or PSC), VPC-SC checks: is the calling project inside the perimeter? If the compromised VM tries to access BigQuery in the attacker's project, VPC-SC blocks the request — even though the network path is technically open.

PSC + VPC-SC together: Use restricted.googleapis.com PSC endpoints (not private.googleapis.com). All API traffic routes to the restricted endpoint. VPC-SC perimeter enforces that only in-perimeter projects can be accessed. PSC ensures the network path is entirely private. The combination makes it impossible to exfiltrate data to an external project via the GCP data plane — even from inside your VPC.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank PCI-DSS architecture and VPC Service Controls work is directly applicable here.
PSC6How do you design DNS for Private Service Connect endpoints in a hybrid cloud environment?
DNS for PSCIntermediate
▼
DNS is one of the trickiest parts of PSC deployments — especially in hybrid environments where DNS queries originate both from GCP and on-premises.

GCP-side DNS for PSC endpoints: Create Cloud DNS private managed zones for each PSC endpoint. For a PSC endpoint for storage.googleapis.com at 10.0.0.10: create a private zone for storage.googleapis.com with an A record pointing to 10.0.0.10. This zone is attached to the consumer VPC — all queries from that VPC for storage.googleapis.com resolve to 10.0.0.10 (the PSC endpoint).

Hybrid DNS challenge: On-premises servers making API calls need to resolve Google API hostnames to PSC endpoints (not public IPs) to ensure traffic goes via Cloud Interconnect and PSC. Solution: (1) Cloud DNS inbound policy on the VPC — creates inbound forwarding endpoints in the VPC. (2) Configure on-premises DNS forwarder to send queries for googleapis.com to the Cloud DNS inbound endpoints via Interconnect. On-premises clients now get private PSC IP resolution.

For service-to-service PSC: Each PSC endpoint needs a DNS entry in a private zone shared across all consumer VPCs. Using a central DNS project (shared DNS hub): create the private zones in the DNS project, attach them to all consumer VPCs using peered zones. One DNS update in the DNS hub propagates to all consumers automatically.

Validation: Test from VMs using dig storage.googleapis.com — should return your private PSC IP, not Google's public IPs.
PSC7What are the security considerations and controls for Private Service Connect?
PSC SecuritySenior
▼
Consumer controls (who can create endpoints to your service):
(1) Consumer accept list: Specify which GCP projects or organisations are allowed to create PSC endpoints to your service. Without this, any GCP project could connect if they know your service attachment URI.
(2) Manual approval: Require explicit approval for each PSC endpoint request. Use this for sensitive services — the service team reviews each consumer request before the connection is established.
(3) Connection limit: Set a maximum number of PSC connections to prevent a single service from being overwhelmed.

Producer controls (data that flows through PSC):
(1) Service attachment IAM: Who can see and reference the service attachment. Keep service attachments private — don't expose the URI publicly.
(2) Backend service IAM: Even if a consumer creates a PSC endpoint, the backend service (Cloud Run, ILB backend) should enforce its own authentication. PSC provides network access — application-layer auth is still required.

Monitoring PSC connections: VPC Flow Logs on the PSC endpoint subnet capture all traffic traversing PSC connections. Set up alerting for unusual traffic volumes. Audit PSC connection approvals in Cloud Audit Logs — every connection accept/reject is logged.

Revoking access: Reject or disconnect a PSC connection from the producer side — this immediately cuts off the consumer's access to your service without changing any network routes.
PSC8How does Private Service Connect complement Cloud Interconnect for on-premises connectivity?
Cloud Interconnect and PSCSenior
▼
Cloud Interconnect and PSC serve different connectivity needs — together they provide complete private connectivity coverage for hybrid environments.

Cloud Interconnect covers: VM-to-VM and VM-to-on-premises traffic. GCP workloads communicating with on-premises data centres, databases, and legacy systems. Network-level connectivity between GCP VPCs and on-premises private IP ranges. BGP route exchange — on-premises CIDRs advertised into GCP and GCP subnets advertised back to on-premises.

PSC covers: Service-level access to GCP managed services (APIs, published services) from on-premises — without going over the internet. Example: on-premises application reads from GCS via Cloud Interconnect → Shared VPC → PSC endpoint for storage.googleapis.com → Google's storage backend. Zero internet exposure for this data path.

Combined architecture for a Canadian bank: Cloud Interconnect: Toronto DC ↔ northamerica-northeast1 GCP. VPC with PSC endpoints for all GCP APIs needed (BigQuery, GCS, Vertex AI, Pub/Sub). Private DNS forwarding: on-premises DNS → Cloud DNS inbound policy endpoints → PSC-aware private zones. Org Policy: constraints/gcp.resourceLocations = northamerica-northeast1/2 only. Result: on-premises applications access GCP services via Interconnect+PSC — data never traverses the internet, data never leaves Canada.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Cloud Interconnect and Azure ExpressRoute work at Scotiabank is the network foundation. PSC adds the managed service access layer on top.
PSC9How do PSC Network Endpoint Groups work with Cloud Load Balancing?
PSC NEGsIntermediate
▼
PSC Network Endpoint Groups (NEGs) allow Cloud Load Balancing to use a PSC endpoint as a backend — enabling the LB to route traffic to services in other VPCs or to Google-managed services accessed via PSC, without direct VPC peering.

Use case: Your platform needs to proxy traffic to a third-party SaaS service exposed via PSC (e.g., Snowflake data warehouse). Rather than connecting your Cloud Run services directly to the PSC endpoint, you use a Global Load Balancer with a PSC NEG as the backend. The LB handles TLS termination, logging, and routing rules — the PSC connection to Snowflake is abstracted.

Setup:
(1) Create a PSC endpoint (forwarding rule) in your VPC to the third-party service attachment.
(2) Create an internet NEG (for external services) or a PSC NEG with psc_target_service set to the PSC endpoint's forwarding rule ID.
(3) Create a backend service in Cloud Load Balancing referencing the PSC NEG.
(4) Configure URL routing rules, timeout settings, and Cloud Armor policies on the backend service.

Benefit over direct PSC access: Centralised traffic management. All traffic to third-party services goes through your LB — you get Cloud Armor WAF protection, access logging, and the ability to route different paths to different backends. The LB also provides health checking against the PSC endpoint.
PSC10Design a complete private connectivity architecture for a financial services GCP platform — no public internet traffic for any service.
ImplementationSenior
▼
This is an end-to-end architecture challenge for a zero-internet GCP deployment — all traffic stays on private networks.

User access (humans): Corporate users → Cloud Interconnect → Shared VPC → Identity-Aware Proxy → Cloud Run / GKE. IAP replaces VPN. BeyondCorp context-aware access enforces device compliance. No public LB endpoints — Global LB with IAP, ingress restricted to corporate IP ranges via Cloud Armor.

GCP API access (workloads): PSC endpoints for all required Google APIs (storage, bigquery, vertex AI, pubsub, secretmanager). restricted.googleapis.com bundle for VPC-SC compliance. Cloud DNS private zones resolving all googleapis.com domains to PSC endpoint IPs. No outbound internet routes in the routing table — default route is a Null route. Only Interconnect routes and PSC endpoint IPs are reachable.

On-premises connectivity: Cloud Interconnect (Dedicated, 10Gbps, 2 connections per region). Cloud Router with BGP advertising GCP subnets to on-premises. On-premises DNS forwarding via Cloud DNS inbound policy for googleapis.com resolution. Cloud Interconnect for on-premises → GCP API traffic (via PSC endpoints).

Inter-service communication (microservices): PSC service attachments for published internal services. Internal Application Load Balancers in each VPC. No VPC peering — PSC endpoints only. VPC Flow Logs on all subnets for traffic visibility.

Egress control: Cloud NAT disabled or restricted by allow-list (only specific package registries for build systems). Security Command Center + VPC Flow Log anomaly alerts for any unexpected egress. VPC Service Controls perimeter around all data-plane GCP services.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: This maps directly to your Scotiabank GCP network architecture — position this as an evolution of what you built.
