GHA1What is GitHub Actions and how does it fit into a GCP platform delivery pipeline?
OverviewCore
▼
GitHub Actions is GitHub's native CI/CD automation platform. Workflows are defined as YAML files stored in the .github/workflows/ directory of a repository. They are triggered by events (push, pull_request, schedule, workflow_dispatch) and execute jobs on GitHub-hosted or self-hosted runners.

How it fits into GCP delivery: GitHub Actions is the orchestration layer that ties together code quality, security scanning, Terraform infrastructure-as-code, and application deployment to GCP.

A typical GCP platform pipeline structure:
(1) On PR: Lint + unit tests → Security scan (Snyk/Trivy for containers, tfsec for Terraform) → terraform plan (posted as PR comment) → cost estimate (Infracost) → OPA policy check.
(2) On merge to main: Build and push container image to Artifact Registry → Sign image (Binary Authorization) → terraform apply → Deploy to Cloud Run (or GKE).
(3) Scheduled: Nightly drift detection (terraform plan, alert if non-empty) → weekly dependency vulnerability scan.

GCP-specific integrations: Workload Identity Federation for keyless GCP authentication. google-github-actions/* official action library (auth, setup-gcloud, deploy-cloudrun, deploy-gke). Artifact Registry as the container image registry. Cloud Deploy for progressive delivery orchestration.
GHA2Explain Workload Identity Federation for GitHub Actions — how does it work and why is it the required approach?
WIF AuthSenior
▼
Workload Identity Federation (WIF) allows GitHub Actions to authenticate to GCP without storing any service account key JSON in GitHub Secrets. It is the modern, secure approach and should be used for all new pipelines.

How it works step-by-step:
(1) GitHub generates an OIDC token for each workflow run. This token contains claims: the repository name, the branch, the triggering event, the workflow file path, and the runner environment.
(2) The GitHub Actions workflow calls gcloud/Auth (google-github-actions/auth) with the WIF pool ID, provider ID, and service account email.
(3) The auth action sends the GitHub OIDC token to GCP's Security Token Service (STS).
(4) STS validates the token against the GitHub OIDC discovery document (public keys). Checks the token's claims against the WIF provider's attribute conditions (e.g., assertion.repository == 'myorg/myrepo').
(5) If valid, STS issues a short-lived GCP access token (1-hour lifetime) impersonating the specified service account.

Attribute conditions (critical for security): You must set conditions on the WIF provider binding — otherwise any GitHub repo could impersonate your service account. Minimum conditions: assertion.repository == 'your-org/your-repo' AND assertion.ref == 'refs/heads/main' (for prod deploys). For environment-specific deploys: assertion.job_workflow_ref to validate the calling workflow file.

Why over service account keys: No secret to rotate or leak. No risk of key appearing in logs, git history, or error messages. Full audit trail in Cloud Audit Logs showing exactly which repo/branch/workflow authenticated.
GHA3How do you structure a GitHub Actions workflow for a Terraform-managed GCP platform with multiple environments?
Pipeline StructureIntermediate
▼
Repository structure:
.github/workflows/
├── pr-checks.yml — runs on all PRs
├── deploy-staging.yml — runs on merge to main
├── deploy-production.yml — requires manual approval
└── scheduled-drift.yml — runs nightly

pr-checks.yml: Triggered on pull_request to main. Jobs (all parallel): (1) terraform fmt + validate, (2) tfsec security scan, (3) OPA/Conftest policy check, (4) terraform plan with output posted to PR via actions/github-script, (5) Infracost cost delta comment.

deploy-staging.yml: Triggered on push to main. Steps: (1) WIF auth to staging service account, (2) terraform apply -auto-approve (using the exact plan saved from PR), (3) Build + push container to Artifact Registry, (4) Deploy to staging Cloud Run, (5) Run smoke tests against staging endpoint, (6) Post result to Slack.

deploy-production.yml: Triggered manually (workflow_dispatch) OR by GitHub Environment protection rules after staging deploy succeeds. Requires approval from 2 reviewers (GitHub Environment: production). Steps: same as staging but targeting prod service accounts and prod GCP project.

Environments and secrets: GitHub Environments (staging, production) provide approval gates and environment-specific variables. No actual secrets stored — only WIF pool IDs and service account emails (non-sensitive). Env-specific variables: TF_BACKEND_BUCKET, GCP_PROJECT_ID, WORKLOAD_IDENTITY_PROVIDER.
GHA4What are reusable workflows in GitHub Actions and why are they important for platform governance?
Reusable WorkflowsIntermediate
▼
Reusable workflows (called workflows) are GitHub Actions workflow files that can be invoked from other workflows — like a function call. They are defined with the on: workflow_call trigger and accept inputs and secrets.

Why critical for platform governance: In a large organisation with hundreds of repos, you cannot maintain a copy of the CI/CD pipeline in every repo. Reusable workflows allow the platform team to define the golden path pipeline once and have all application repos consume it. When the platform team updates the security scan step (e.g., adds a new CVE check), all repos get the update automatically on next run — no PR to each repo needed.

Structure: Platform repo (.github/workflows/): deploy-cloudrun.yml (the reusable workflow). Application repo (.github/workflows/): ci.yml (calls the platform workflow).

Security model: Reusable workflows called with uses: myorg/.github/.github/workflows/deploy.yml@main are pinned to the platform repo's main branch. Application teams can't modify the workflow logic — only provide inputs (service name, image tag, target environment). Platform team controls what the pipeline does.

Governance enforcement: The reusable workflow includes mandatory steps: security scan (cannot be skipped), required approvals for prod, drift detection post-deploy. These run for all repos that use the shared workflow — no team can opt out.
GHA5How do you implement container image security in a GitHub Actions → GCP Artifact Registry → Cloud Run pipeline?
Container SecuritySenior
▼
Container image security must be enforced at every stage — from build time through to deployment.

Build stage (GitHub Actions):
(1) Base image scanning: run Trivy against the base image before building. Fail if critical CVEs exist in the base image — force a base image update.
(2) Dockerfile linting: Hadolint checks Dockerfile best practices (non-root user, no ADD with URLs, no plaintext secrets in ENV).
(3) Build the image: docker build with build args for version metadata.
(4) Layer-level scan: run Trivy against the newly built image. Set --exit-code 1 for critical and high CVEs — the pipeline fails if any exist.
(5) Push to Artifact Registry: docker push to your GCP region's Artifact Registry repository.

Signing and attestation (Binary Authorization): After a successful scan, create a vulnerability scan attestation using the Container Analysis API. The attestation records: image digest, scanner version, scan result. Cloud KMS signs the attestation. Binary Authorization policy on the GKE cluster or Cloud Run: only images with a valid attestation from the approved scanning step may be deployed. An unsigned image is blocked from running — even if someone manually pushes it to Artifact Registry.

Artifact Registry policies: Enable Vulnerability Scanning on the Artifact Registry repository (automatic re-scanning when new CVEs are discovered). Set an Artifact Registry cleanup policy to delete untagged images older than 30 days — reduce storage costs and prevent deployment of stale images.
GHA6When would you use self-hosted runners for GitHub Actions in a GCP environment, and how do you secure them?
Self-Hosted RunnersSenior
▼
When to use self-hosted runners:
(1) Network access to private resources: GitHub-hosted runners are on GitHub's network. They cannot directly access private GCP resources (Cloud SQL private IP, GKE private cluster API server, internal Artifact Registry) without Cloud NAT or VPN. Self-hosted runners deployed in your VPC can reach private resources directly.
(2) Regulatory compliance: Some regulated environments require that CI/CD pipeline execution happens on infrastructure you control — not GitHub's shared infrastructure. Your compliance team may require runners on-premises or in your GCP project.
(3) Custom tools and performance: Runners with specific software pre-installed, GPU access for ML pipelines, or larger machine types than GitHub-hosted offer.

Self-hosted runner architecture on GCP: GKE cluster (dedicated runner pool) with Auto-Provisioning. Actions Runner Controller (ARC) — open-source Kubernetes operator that auto-scales runners based on queue depth. Runners are ephemeral — created for each job and destroyed after. This prevents state leakage between runs.

Securing self-hosted runners:
(1) Never use self-hosted runners on public repos — anyone can submit a PR and run code on your runners.
(2) Runners should have minimal IAM permissions — only what the pipeline needs, via Workload Identity.
(3) Restrict which repos can use self-hosted runners via GitHub Actions runner group settings.
(4) Audit runner activity — all runner logs shipped to Cloud Logging via a logging sidecar.
GHA7How do you manage secrets securely in GitHub Actions for GCP deployments?
Secrets ManagementIntermediate
▼
The goal is zero stored secrets — every credential used by the pipeline should be short-lived and automatically provisioned.

No secrets needed (the ideal): With Workload Identity Federation, GitHub Actions has no GCP credentials to store. The WIF provider configuration (pool ID, provider ID, service account email) are not secrets — they're project-specific identifiers that are safe to store as GitHub Variables (not Secrets).

Application secrets (databases, APIs): Application secrets should NOT be passed through GitHub Actions environment at all. Instead: (1) Store them in GCP Secret Manager. (2) In the deployment step, the CI/CD service account (via WIF) has roles/secretmanager.secretAccessor on specific secrets. (3) Cloud Run pulls secrets from Secret Manager at runtime — not from the deployment pipeline. The pipeline never touches the actual secret value.

If you must use GitHub Secrets: Use GitHub's encrypted secrets (AES-256 at rest). Use repository or organisation secrets, not environment-level secrets for sensitive values. Rotate regularly. Mask values in logs (GitHub does this automatically for registered secrets). Audit secret access in the GitHub audit log.

OIDC tokens for other services: If your pipeline needs to authenticate to Vault, AWS, or Azure alongside GCP, use the same OIDC federation approach: configure each platform's OIDC provider to trust GitHub's token issuer, and exchange for short-lived credentials at runtime.
GHA8How do you run Terraform safely in GitHub Actions with proper locking, state management, and rollback?
Terraform in CISenior
▼
State backend configuration: All Terraform state in GCS with versioning and object locking. Each environment has its own state file. The WIF service account for CI has storage.objectAdmin on state buckets (minimum: get, create, update for state operations).

Plan-then-apply separation: Never run terraform apply in a PR workflow — only terraform plan. Save the plan: terraform plan -out=tfplan. Upload tfplan as a GitHub Actions artifact (encrypted). On merge, download the exact plan artifact and apply it: terraform apply tfplan. This ensures you apply exactly what was reviewed, not a re-computed plan that might differ.

Concurrency control: Use GitHub Actions concurrency groups to prevent parallel Terraform runs on the same environment: concurrency: group: terraform-${{ github.env }} / cancel-in-progress: false. This queues concurrent runs rather than cancelling them — preventing state corruption from parallel applies.

Rollback strategy: Keep previous Terraform state versions in GCS (versioning enabled). If an apply fails or breaks something, restore the previous state version (gcloud storage objects copy gs://bucket/terraform.tfstate#version_id) and re-apply. Maintain a rollback runbook documenting exact steps. For application deployments, Cloud Run revision rollback is separate and near-instant.
GHA9How do you instrument GitHub Actions workflows to measure DORA metrics for a GCP platform?
DORA MetricsIntermediate
▼
DORA metrics (Deployment Frequency, Lead Time for Changes, Change Failure Rate, Time to Restore) are the standard measures of software delivery performance. GitHub Actions provides the raw data.

Deployment Frequency: Count successful deploy workflow runs per time period. GitHub Actions API: GET /repos/{owner}/{repo}/actions/runs filtered by workflow_id and conclusion:success. Export to BigQuery via a scheduled Cloud Function → query for weekly/daily deployment counts.

Lead Time for Changes: Time from first commit to production deployment. GitHub API: get the commit timestamp of the oldest commit in the PR. Get the production deploy workflow completion timestamp. Lead time = deploy time - commit time. Complex but achievable with GitHub API + BigQuery.

Change Failure Rate: Percentage of deployments that caused a rollback or incident. Tag Cloud Run revisions with the GitHub workflow run ID. Monitor Cloud Run error rate spikes after deployments. Alert + create a Jira 'rollback' ticket when a deployment causes error rate to exceed SLO. Count rollbacks / total deployments per time period.

Time to Restore Service (MTTR): Time from incident creation to resolution. Correlate PagerDuty incident timestamps with Cloud Run revision rollback timestamps or next successful deployment.

Dashboard: All metrics land in BigQuery → Looker Studio DORA dashboard shared with engineering leadership. Track trends quarter over quarter — DORA metrics improvement is a direct measure of platform engineering impact.
GHA10What are advanced GitHub Actions patterns for a large GCP platform — matrix builds, composite actions, and workflow templates?
Advanced PatternsSenior
▼
Matrix builds: Run the same job across multiple dimensions simultaneously. Use case: deploy to 3 GCP regions in parallel, or test across 3 Node.js versions. strategy: matrix: region: [us-central1, northamerica-northeast1, europe-west1]. Each matrix combination runs as a separate job — all 3 regional deployments happen simultaneously, reducing total deploy time. Combine with fail-fast: false to continue other regions if one fails.

Composite actions: Package a sequence of steps into a reusable action (action.yml with using: composite). Use cases: a 'gcp-auth' composite action that performs WIF auth, sets up gcloud, and configures Docker for Artifact Registry — all in 3 lines in any workflow. A 'terraform-init' composite action that configures the backend, sets workspace, and runs init. Composite actions are version-pinned and maintained by the platform team.

Workflow templates (organisation level): Stored in the .github repo of the organisation. When engineers create a new workflow in any repo, GitHub suggests these templates. Templates include the golden path pipeline structure — engineers fill in variables (service name, target environment) and get a compliant CI/CD pipeline in minutes.

Dynamic workflow generation: Use the workflow_call + matrix pattern to dynamically generate deployment targets based on changed files (paths-filter action). Only redeploy the services affected by a PR — dramatically reduces deployment time in a monorepo with 50 services.

🚀 Cloud Run Q&As

10 questions covering Networking · Scaling · Security · Jobs · Traffic management · Observability · Multi-region

Click any question to expand the model answer. Senior questions are the differentiating ones that set Platform Architect candidates apart.
10
Questions
4
Senior
5
Intermediate
1
Core
CR1What is Cloud Run and when would you choose it over GKE or App Engine?
OverviewCore
▼
Cloud Run is Google's fully managed serverless container execution platform. You deploy a container image and Cloud Run handles: autoscaling (including scale-to-zero), load balancing, TLS, health checks, and infrastructure. You pay only for the CPU and memory consumed while requests are being processed.

vs GKE: Cloud Run requires zero cluster management — no nodes to provision, patch, or monitor. GKE offers more control: custom node types, GPUs, DaemonSets, stateful workloads (StatefulSets), privileged containers, and persistent volumes. Choose Cloud Run for: stateless services, REST/gRPC APIs, event-driven processors, and workloads with variable or unpredictable traffic. Choose GKE for: stateful services, workloads requiring GPUs, complex scheduling requirements, or when running a large fleet of services where cluster-level economies of scale matter.

vs App Engine: App Engine is older and more opinionated — it manages your language runtime (Python, Java, Go) and handles deployment of source code. Cloud Run is container-based — you bring any language and any framework. Cloud Run is more flexible, better suited for microservices, and has a simpler programming model.

Sweet spot for Cloud Run: REST APIs, webhook processors, background workers, ML inference endpoints, and developer tooling — any stateless container that benefits from zero-maintenance scaling.
CR2How do you configure Cloud Run networking for a production deployment with VPC integration?
NetworkingIntermediate
▼
Default Cloud Run networking: Cloud Run services get a public HTTPS URL by default. No VPC connectivity — the service cannot reach private GCP resources (Cloud SQL private IP, Memorystore, internal services).

VPC integration options:
(1) Serverless VPC Access Connector (legacy): A small VM-based bridge between Cloud Run and your VPC. Cloud Run traffic routes through the connector. Supports egress-only (Cloud Run → VPC). Limited bandwidth and adds latency. Still works but newer deployments should prefer Direct VPC Egress.
(2) Direct VPC Egress (recommended): Cloud Run instances get a direct network interface in your VPC subnet. Full bandwidth, lower latency, no separate connector to manage. Configure with --network and --subnet flags or in YAML. Cloud Run instances get ephemeral IPs from your subnet CIDR.

Ingress controls: Three modes: (1) Allow all (public internet) — default. (2) Internal only — only requests from your VPC, Shared VPC, or other GCP services. (3) Internal and Cloud Load Balancing — recommended for production. Traffic only arrives via the LB (which can have Cloud Armor and IAP applied).

Private Cloud SQL access: Cloud Run with Direct VPC Egress → private subnet → Cloud SQL Auth Proxy (or direct Cloud SQL Private IP). The Cloud SQL instance must have a Private IP in the same VPC (or peered VPC via Shared VPC). No public IP on Cloud SQL needed.
CR3How does Cloud Run autoscaling work — what are the key configuration knobs?
ScalingIntermediate
▼
Cloud Run autoscaling drivers: The primary metric is concurrent requests per instance. When requests pile up beyond the concurrency limit, Cloud Run adds new instances. When instances are idle (no active requests), they scale down — eventually to zero if no requests arrive for ~15 minutes.

Key configuration settings:
(1) --concurrency (default: 80): Maximum concurrent requests per instance. This is the most important tuning parameter. Higher concurrency = fewer instances needed for the same throughput, but more memory contention within an instance. CPU-bound workloads: concurrency=1 (one request per instance). I/O-bound workloads (waiting on DB): concurrency=80-200 (instances spend most time waiting, so many requests can share an instance).
(2) --min-instances (default: 0): Minimum number of instances to keep warm. Setting to 1+ eliminates cold start latency for latency-sensitive services. Costs money when there are no requests — size based on your SLO vs cost tradeoff.
(3) --max-instances (default: 100): Hard limit on instances. Protects downstream dependencies (Cloud SQL connection limits, Memorystore connection limits) from being overwhelmed during traffic spikes.
(4) --cpu-throttling (default: enabled): CPU is only allocated during request processing. For latency-sensitive apps: --no-cpu-throttling (CPU always allocated — eliminates throttling latency at higher cost).

Cold start mitigation: Keep your container image small (<500MB compressed), initialise connections in the global scope (not per-request), and use min-instances for latency-critical paths.
CR4How do you secure a Cloud Run service for a production financial services workload?
SecuritySenior
▼
Authentication: Cloud Run authentication has two modes: (1) Allow unauthenticated invocations: Public endpoint, no auth enforced by Cloud Run. Use Cloud Armor + IAP or application-level auth. (2) Require authentication: Every request must include a valid Google identity token. Service accounts and users need roles/run.invoker. For internal services — always require authentication. For public APIs — use Allow unauthenticated with application-level auth (OAuth2/JWT validation).

Service identity: Each Cloud Run service gets a dedicated service account (not the default Compute service account). That service account gets only the specific roles it needs: roles/bigquery.dataViewer on a specific dataset, roles/secretmanager.secretAccessor on specific secrets. Principle of least privilege at the service level.

Secrets: Never pass secrets as environment variables (visible in Cloud Run console and APIs). Mount from Secret Manager: --set-secrets SECRET_NAME=projects/PROJECT/secrets/SECRET_NAME:latest. The secret value is injected as an env var or mounted as a file at runtime — never stored in the container image or the Cloud Run revision configuration.

Container hardening: Non-root user in Dockerfile. Read-only root filesystem (--read-only in container spec). No unnecessary ports exposed. Minimal base image (distroless preferred).

Binary Authorization: Enable Binary Authorization on Cloud Run. Only container images with a valid attestation (signed by your CI/CD pipeline after security scanning) can be deployed. Prevents deployment of unscanned or tampered images.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank Cloud Run deployments with PCI-DSS controls are directly applicable here.
CR5What are Cloud Run Jobs and how do they differ from Cloud Run Services?
Cloud Run JobsIntermediate
▼
Cloud Run Services run continuously, listening for HTTP/gRPC requests. They scale based on incoming requests and stay alive to serve traffic. Designed for long-running, request-driven workloads.

Cloud Run Jobs run tasks to completion — they execute code, do work, and exit. There is no persistent endpoint. Jobs are triggered explicitly (gcloud run jobs execute), on a schedule (Cloud Scheduler), or programmatically (Cloud Run Jobs API). They run until completion or timeout.

When to use Jobs: Batch data processing, database migrations (run the migration before deploying the new service version), report generation (nightly), ML training steps (as part of a Vertex AI pipeline), scheduled cleanup tasks, and one-off maintenance scripts. Anything that has a defined start and end.

Key Job features:
(1) Parallelism: A job can run multiple task instances simultaneously (--parallelism). Each task gets a unique CLOUD_RUN_TASK_INDEX — tasks can divide work by index (process chunk [index] of [count]).
(2) Task retries: Failed tasks are automatically retried (--max-retries). Essential for idempotent batch work.
(3) VPC access: Jobs support Direct VPC Egress — can reach private databases and internal services.
(4) Timeout: Jobs have a maximum timeout (up to 24 hours) — unlike services which have a 60-minute per-request timeout.
CR6How do you implement blue-green and canary deployments with Cloud Run?
Traffic ManagementSenior
▼
Cloud Run has native traffic splitting between revisions — making zero-downtime deployments and canary releases straightforward.

Blue-green deployment:
(1) Deploy the new revision without sending any traffic: gcloud run deploy --no-traffic. New revision is created but 0% of requests go to it.
(2) Test the new revision directly by accessing its specific revision URL (revision URL is separate from the service URL).
(3) Once validated, atomically switch 100% of traffic: gcloud run services update-traffic --to-revisions NEW_REVISION=100. The switch is instant and atomically at the Cloud Run control plane — no period where both revisions serve traffic unless you want that.

Canary deployment:
(1) Deploy new revision, split traffic: gcloud run services update-traffic --to-revisions STABLE=95,CANARY=5.
(2) Monitor error rate and latency for both revisions in Cloud Monitoring (split by revision_name label).
(3) Gradually increase canary traffic: 5% → 25% → 50% → 100%.
(4) If error rate spikes: gcloud run services update-traffic --to-revisions STABLE=100 — instant rollback.

Automating canary progression: A Cloud Build trigger + Cloud Monitoring alerting policy. Every 5 minutes, query Cloud Monitoring for canary revision error rate. If below threshold, run gcloud to increment canary traffic by 10%. If above threshold, rollback to stable. This creates a continuous automated canary progression without human intervention.
CR7How do you implement observability for Cloud Run services?
ObservabilityIntermediate
▼
Logging: Cloud Run automatically captures stdout/stderr and sends to Cloud Logging. Use structured JSON logging for queryable logs:
import json, sys
print(json.dumps({'severity': 'INFO', 'message': 'Request processed', 'request_id': req_id, 'latency_ms': 45}))
Cloud Logging can then filter by any field. Create log-based metrics for error counting and alerting.

Metrics: Cloud Run automatically emits: request count, request latency (p50/p95/p99), instance count, pending requests. In Cloud Monitoring: create SLO dashboards. Set alert policies: error rate > 1%, p99 latency > 500ms, pending request count > 0 (indicates capacity shortage).

Distributed tracing: Add Cloud Trace propagation to your application. Send trace headers (X-Cloud-Trace-Context) to downstream services. Traces appear in Cloud Trace showing the full request lifecycle across services. For Python: use opentelemetry-sdk with the Cloud Trace exporter. For Go/Java/Node: similar opentelemetry libraries exist.

Profiling: Cloud Profiler supports Cloud Run — continuously profiles CPU and memory usage with near-zero overhead. Identifies hot code paths, memory leaks, and opportunities for optimisation. Particularly valuable for cost optimisation: profiling reveals which functions consume the most CPU-seconds (directly translating to cost).

Error Reporting: Unhandled exceptions in Cloud Run are automatically captured by Cloud Error Reporting. Errors are grouped, deduplicated, and show first-seen/last-seen timestamps. Set up email/PagerDuty notifications for new errors.
CR8How do you integrate Cloud Run with IAP and Cloud Load Balancing for a production internal portal?
Cloud Run and IAPSenior
▼
Cloud Run's default URL (*.run.app) gives a public endpoint. For an internal portal, you want: no public access, user authentication, corporate SSO, and potentially device compliance checking. This requires Cloud Load Balancing + IAP in front of Cloud Run.

Architecture: Global External Application LB → Cloud Armor → IAP → Serverless NEG → Cloud Run

Step-by-step setup:
(1) Lock down Cloud Run ingress: Set ingress to 'Internal and Load Balancing only'. This makes the *.run.app URL return 403 — all traffic must come via the LB.
(2) Create a Serverless NEG: type=SERVERLESS, region=your Cloud Run region, cloud-run-service=your-service-name. This creates a logical backend pointing to the Cloud Run service.
(3) Backend service: Create a backend service with the serverless NEG as the backend. Enable IAP on the backend service — configure OAuth client credentials.
(4) URL map, proxy, and forwarding rule: Standard global LB setup. Use a managed SSL certificate for your custom domain.
(5) IAP access policy: Grant roles/iap.httpsResourceAccessor to the engineering group. Optionally add an Access Context Manager access level requiring a managed device.
(6) DNS: Point your custom domain to the LB's global IP. SSL certificate provisions automatically.

Result: Engineers browse to portal.company.com → redirected to Google SSO → access checked against IAP policy → proxied to Cloud Run. No public access to the *.run.app URL.
CR9How do you optimise Cloud Run costs for a high-volume production service?
Cost OptimisationIntermediate
▼
Cloud Run's pay-per-use model is a double-edged sword: great for bursty traffic, potentially expensive for high-volume sustained traffic. Key levers to optimise:

Right-size CPU and memory: Cloud Run charges for the CPU and memory you allocate per instance, per second. Analyse actual utilisation in Cloud Profiler and Cloud Monitoring. If your service consistently uses 0.3 vCPU, set --cpu=0.5 instead of 1.0 (60% cost reduction on CPU).

Concurrency tuning: Higher concurrency = fewer instances = lower cost. Profile your service: if it spends most time waiting for I/O (database, APIs), increase concurrency from 80 to 200. Fewer instances handle the same throughput — cost drops proportionally.

CPU allocation: Default CPU allocation during request processing only. Switch to CPU always allocated (-cpu-always-allocated) only when needed (background tasks, CPU throttling latency is unacceptable). Never pay for always-allocated CPU on services that have low utilisation.

Request batching: For event-driven services (Pub/Sub triggers), process messages in batches rather than one at a time. A single invocation that processes 50 Pub/Sub messages costs the same as one that processes 1. Use Pub/Sub's batch settings and Cloud Run's max concurrency together.

Cloud Run vs GKE break-even: At sustained high volume (>100M requests/month with moderate CPU), GKE with node rightsizing and CUDs often becomes cheaper. Run the break-even calculation quarterly — traffic patterns change.
CR10How do you architect a multi-region Cloud Run deployment for high availability?
Multi-RegionSenior
▼
Architecture: Deploy identical Cloud Run services in 2+ regions. Use the Global External Application Load Balancer to distribute traffic across regions. The LB's Anycast IP routes users to the nearest healthy region automatically.

Multi-region deployment with Terraform: Use a for_each loop over a regions map. Each region gets: Cloud Run service, Serverless NEG in that region, backend in the global backend service. The same container image (from Artifact Registry, globally available) is deployed to each region. A single CI/CD pipeline deploys to all regions in parallel (GitHub Actions matrix strategy).

Health check configuration: Define an HTTP health check on /healthz (return 200 only if the service is ready to handle traffic — check downstream dependencies). The global LB checks each region's backend every 10 seconds. If a region's health check fails 3 consecutive times (~30 seconds), the LB stops routing traffic there and sends it all to the remaining healthy regions.

Session state: Cloud Run instances are stateless — all session state must be in an external store (Memorystore Redis, Firestore) that is accessible from all regions. Cross-region Redis (Memorystore for Redis cluster with multi-region capability) or Firestore (globally replicated by default).

Data layer: For SQL, Cloud Spanner multi-region is the cleanest option. For Cloud SQL: primary in one region, cross-region read replica in the second. Writes from Cloud Run instances go to the primary region (accept the cross-region latency or use Spanner). Reads can be served locally from the read replica.

🖥️ Google Compute Engine (GCE) Q&As

10 questions covering Machine types · Persistent disk · Shielded VMs · MIGs · Security hardening · Spot VMs · OS Config

Click any question to expand the model answer. Senior questions are the differentiating ones that set Platform Architect candidates apart.
10
Questions
3
Senior
5
Intermediate
2
Core
GCE1What is Google Compute Engine (GCE) and when would you choose it over Cloud Run or GKE?
OverviewCore
▼
Google Compute Engine (GCE) is GCP's Infrastructure-as-a-Service (IaaS) offering — virtual machines that give you full control over the OS, hardware configuration, networking, and software stack.

When to choose GCE over Cloud Run: Workloads requiring persistent local storage (databases, stateful services), workloads needing specific OS configurations or kernel versions, legacy applications that can't be containerised, specialised hardware (GPUs/TPUs for HPC, high-memory instances for SAP HANA), and applications requiring persistent SSH access for management.

When to choose GCE over GKE: Single-VM workloads that don't need orchestration, legacy application migrations that aren't Kubernetes-native, and when the Kubernetes learning curve isn't justified for the workload size.

GCE strengths: Maximum hardware customisation (96+ vCPU, 624GB RAM instances). Persistent disk (block storage) support. Spot VMs for massive cost reduction on batch workloads. Shielded VMs for enhanced security. Live migration (VM migrated to healthy hardware with no downtime during host maintenance). Custom machine types (no need to fit into predefined sizes).

Platform Architect perspective: In a modern GCP platform, GCE is typically used for: bastion hosts (or replaced by IAP tunnelling), self-hosted runners (GitHub Actions), database servers when managed options aren't viable, and legacy lift-and-shift workloads.
GCE2What are the GCE machine type families and how do you choose the right one?
Machine TypesIntermediate
▼
GCP offers several machine type families optimised for different workload characteristics:

General Purpose:
- N2/N2D: Best balance of price/performance for most workloads. Good for web servers, app servers, databases. N2D uses AMD processors (typically 5-15% cheaper than N2).
- E2: Cost-optimised, burstable CPU (shares physical cores). Cheapest option — good for dev/test, low-traffic services. Not recommended for consistent high-CPU workloads — burstable CPU can throttle under sustained load.
- N4 (Emerald Rapids): Latest generation Intel, better price/performance than N2 for most workloads.

Compute Optimised:
- C2/C2D: Highest per-core performance. Best for compute-intensive workloads: real-time processing, game servers, HPC, scientific simulations. Most expensive per vCPU but fewest cores needed.

Memory Optimised:
- M2/M3: Up to 12TB RAM per instance. For SAP HANA, large in-memory databases, and analytics workloads that need massive RAM.

Accelerator Optimised:
- A2/A3: NVIDIA GPUs (A100, H100). For ML training, GPU-accelerated computing.

Custom machine types: Define exact vCPU and memory ratios. Useful when standard types don't match your memory-to-CPU ratio — avoids paying for resources you won't use. Costs slightly more than equivalent predefined types.
GCE3What are GCE persistent disk types and how do you choose the right storage for a workload?
Persistent DiskIntermediate
▼
Persistent Disk types:
(1) Standard Persistent Disk (pd-standard): HDD-backed. Cheapest storage. Throughput-oriented, high latency for random I/O. Best for: large sequential workloads (logs, archives, cold data), Hadoop/Spark scratch storage.
(2) Balanced Persistent Disk (pd-balanced): SSD-backed. Best price/performance balance. Suitable for most production workloads — databases, boot disks, web servers. Recommended default.
(3) SSD Persistent Disk (pd-ssd): Higher IOPS and throughput than balanced. For workloads needing consistent low latency: production databases with high transaction rates, latency-sensitive applications.
(4) Extreme Persistent Disk (pd-extreme): Highest IOPS available (up to 350,000 IOPS). For the most demanding database workloads (SQL Server, Oracle, SAP HANA) requiring extreme random I/O.
(5) Hyperdisk Balanced/Extreme/Throughput: Newer generation. Provisioned IOPS and throughput (you configure the exact performance). Decouples disk performance from disk size — you're not forced to over-provision size to get IOPS.

Key performance gotcha: Persistent Disk IOPS and throughput scale with disk size AND VM machine type. A 100GB pd-ssd performs very differently than a 1TB pd-ssd. Always check the sustained performance limits for your disk size and VM combination.
GCE4What are Shielded VMs and why should you require them organisation-wide?
Shielded VMsIntermediate
▼
Shielded VMs are GCE instances with hardware-based security features that protect against boot-level attacks, rootkits, and firmware compromise.

Components:
(1) Secure Boot: Only signed OS images and bootloaders are allowed to run. Prevents unsigned bootloaders or OS kernels from executing — blocks boot-level malware and rootkits that install before the OS starts.
(2) vTPM (Virtual Trusted Platform Module): A virtualised cryptographic processor. Stores integrity measurements (hashes of boot components). If a boot component changes unexpectedly (potential tampering), the vTPM detects the integrity violation and can refuse to unseal encryption keys.
(3) Integrity Monitoring: Compares the current boot measurements with a known-good baseline. Deviations trigger a Cloud Monitoring alert — you know immediately if a VM's boot sequence changed.

Why require org-wide: In regulated environments (PCI-DSS, FedRAMP, NIST 800-53), boot-level integrity is a required control. Shielded VMs provide this automatically. The Org Policy constraint constraints/compute.requireShieldedVm enforces that all VMs in the organisation must have all three Shielded VM features enabled at creation time.

Performance impact: Negligible — Secure Boot adds <1ms to boot time. vTPM operations are hardware-accelerated. No reason not to enable for all workloads.
GCE5How do you use Instance Templates and Managed Instance Groups (MIGs) for scalable, resilient GCE deployments?
Instance TemplatesSenior
▼
Instance Template: An immutable specification for VM configuration: machine type, image, disk configuration, network, service account, metadata, and startup script. A template is the 'golden image recipe' for a group of identical VMs.

Managed Instance Group (MIG): Creates and manages a group of identical VMs from an instance template. Provides: autoscaling (scale up/down based on CPU, custom metrics, or schedules), auto-healing (automatically replaces unhealthy VMs detected by health check), rolling updates (gradually replace VMs with a new template version — zero downtime), and multi-zone distribution (VMs spread across zones for HA).

Rolling updates: Update the MIG to use a new instance template: gcloud compute instance-groups managed rolling-action start-update MIG_NAME --version=template=NEW_TEMPLATE --max-surge=1 --max-unavailable=0. With maxSurge=1 and maxUnavailable=0: one extra VM is provisioned with the new template before an old one is deleted. Zero-downtime update at the cost of temporary extra capacity.

Canary updates: Deploy a second version to 10% of the MIG: add a new template version with target-size=10%. Traffic to the MIG is distributed proportionally. Monitor the canary VMs — if healthy, increase to 100%.

Stateless autoscaling pattern: Application tier MIG (stateless, autoscales on CPU/request count) + separate managed database tier (no MIG — single instance or Cloud SQL). This is the GCE equivalent of Cloud Run's autoscaling for applications that can't be containerised yet.
GCE6What are the key security hardening steps for GCE virtual machines in a financial services environment?
SecuritySenior
▼
Security hardening for GCE VMs requires both GCP-level controls and OS-level hardening:

GCP-level controls:
(1) No public IP: Private IP only. Access via IAP TCP tunnelling (no bastion host needed). Org Policy constraint/compute.vmExternalIpAccess=DENY enforces this.
(2) Shielded VM: Secure Boot + vTPM + Integrity Monitoring. Org Policy constraint/compute.requireShieldedVm enforces this.
(3) OS Login: Replace SSH key management with IAM-based login. --metadata=enable-oslogin=true. Engineers authenticate with their corporate identity — revoke access by removing IAM role, not by collecting SSH keys.
(4) Dedicated service account: Each VM gets a dedicated service account with least privilege. Remove the default service account. Disable service account key creation (use Workload Identity or attached SA).
(5) VPC firewall rules: Deny-all default, allow only required ports from required sources. Use firewall rule tags, not IP ranges, for maintainability.

OS-level controls (via startup script or Ansible): CIS Benchmark hardening (disable unused services, SSH hardening — no root login, no password auth). auditd for kernel-level audit logging. OSConfig for patch management (automatic security patches, compliance reporting). Automated patch schedule via OS Config patch jobs.

Monitoring: VM Manager for patch compliance. Cloud Logging agent (Ops Agent) for OS-level logs to Cloud Logging. Container Threat Detection (if running containers on VMs) and Virtual Machine Threat Detection (VMTD) via SCC Premium.
GCE7How do you architect a GCE workload to take advantage of Spot VMs — what patterns work?
Spot VMsIntermediate
▼
Spot VMs are preemptible — Google can reclaim them with 30 seconds notice when capacity is needed. They are 60-91% cheaper than on-demand VMs. Using them effectively requires designing for interruption.

Patterns that work well with Spot VMs:
(1) Batch processing with checkpointing: ML training jobs, data pipeline processing. Save progress to GCS or Bigtable every N iterations. When a Spot VM is preempted, a new VM starts and resumes from the last checkpoint. Vertex AI custom training supports this natively.
(2) Stateless workers: Task queue workers (Pub/Sub subscribers). If a Spot VM is preempted mid-task, the task returns to the queue after the ack deadline expires and is picked up by another VM. Requires idempotent task processing.
(3) Rendering / simulation farms: Frame-by-frame rendering, Monte Carlo simulations. Each unit of work is independent — preemption loses only the current frame/iteration, not all previous work.

Patterns that don't work: Stateful services holding in-memory session data. Database servers (data loss risk). Long-running jobs without checkpointing (preemption = start over).

Implementation: Use a Managed Instance Group with Spot VM configuration. Set instance redistribution to NONE (MIG won't proactively replace Spot VMs that were preempted — prevents churn). Create a second MIG with on-demand VMs as a fallback — if the Spot MIG size drops below minimum, the on-demand MIG automatically picks up the slack.
GCE8What is GCE Live Migration and why is it important for production workloads?
Live MigrationCore
▼
Live Migration is GCE's ability to migrate a running VM from one physical host to another — without stopping the VM, without rebooting, and with virtually zero downtime. This happens transparently during host maintenance events (hardware upgrades, patching, planned outages).

Why it matters: Traditional VM platforms require planned maintenance windows — stop VM, patch host, restart VM. With Live Migration, Google can maintain the underlying hardware 24/7 without scheduling maintenance windows with customers. The VM keeps running while being silently moved.

How it works (simplified): Google's infrastructure detects an upcoming maintenance event on the host. It starts copying the VM's memory to a target host in the same zone while the VM continues running. When memory sync is nearly complete (residual dirty pages), the VM is briefly paused (typically <250ms), the final dirty pages are copied, and the VM resumes on the new host. To the application, this appears as a brief CPU spike, not an outage.

Limitations: GPU instances do not support live migration — they receive a 60-second termination notice. Spot/Preemptible VMs do not support live migration (they're preempted, not migrated). Instances using local SSDs do not support live migration (local SSD data would need to be migrated too — too slow).

Impact on your SLOs: For most applications, the <250ms migration pause is invisible. For latency-sensitive applications, design for it: use multiple VMs behind a load balancer so a single migration pause doesn't break the SLO.
GCE9How do you design GCE networking for a multi-tier application with strict security segmentation?
NetworkingSenior
▼
A multi-tier application on GCE (web → app → database) requires network segmentation to limit lateral movement if any tier is compromised.

Subnet segmentation: Separate subnets per tier in the same VPC: 10.0.1.0/24 (web tier), 10.0.2.0/24 (app tier), 10.0.3.0/24 (data tier). Subnets in the same VPC can communicate by default — you control this with firewall rules.

Firewall rule design (tag-based): Use network tags, not IP ranges, for maintainability. Tags: http-server, app-server, db-server. Rules:
- Allow tcp:80,443 from 0.0.0.0/0 to tag=http-server (web tier ingress)
- Allow tcp:8080 from tag=http-server to tag=app-server (web → app tier)
- Allow tcp:5432 from tag=app-server to tag=db-server (app → DB)
- Deny all other ingress (default deny-all implicit)
- Deny all egress except specific allowed destinations (outbound deny-default)

Hierarchical firewall policies: Define org-level and folder-level firewall policies that apply before VPC firewall rules. Use for: deny known malicious IP ranges (Google Threat Intelligence), mandatory allow rules (Cloud Logging health checks, SCC agents).

VPC Flow Logs: Enable on all subnets. Essential for: security incident investigation (trace lateral movement), anomaly detection (data exfiltration spikes), compliance (PCI-DSS network traffic logging requirement).

Private Google Access: Enable on all subnets so VMs can reach GCP APIs without needing a public IP.
GCE10How do you manage GCE VM patches and configuration compliance at scale using OS Config?
OS ConfigIntermediate
▼
Managing patches across tens or hundreds of GCE VMs manually is unscalable and a compliance risk. OS Config provides centralised patch and configuration management natively in GCP.

Patch Management (OS Config Patch):
(1) Patch jobs: Execute a patch operation across a fleet of VMs filtered by zones, labels, or instance names. Supports all major Linux distributions and Windows Server. Define: which patches to apply (security only, all, or specific packages), rollout rate (concurrent instances or percentage), reboot policy (always, if needed, never), maintenance window.
(2) Patch deployments: Scheduled recurring patch jobs. Define a schedule (every Tuesday at 2am) and OS Config runs patches automatically. Critical for PCI-DSS Requirement 6 (timely patching of vulnerabilities).
(3) Compliance dashboard: Shows patch compliance across your fleet — which VMs are missing which patches. Export to Cloud Logging and BigQuery for compliance reporting to QSA.

OS Policy Management (OS Config Policies): Define desired configuration state for VMs (CIS Benchmark baseline, custom package lists, file configurations). OS Config agents on each VM continuously enforce the desired state and report compliance. Non-compliant VMs are automatically remediated or flagged.

Agent deployment: Ops Agent (replaces Logging and Monitoring agents). Deploy via startup script, Ansible, or OS Config's own agent installation mechanism. The Ops Agent collects VM metrics, system logs, and application logs — sends to Cloud Monitoring and Cloud Logging.

🔒 Security Guardrails — Architect Perspective Q&As

10 questions covering Prevention layer · Detection layer · Terraform guardrails · Zero trust · Shared responsibility · Supply chain · Posture management
