Core
API1What are the core principles of REST API design and how do they differ from gRPC?
API Design PrinciplesCore
▼
REST (Representational State Transfer) principles: (1) Resource-oriented — URLs represent resources (nouns), not actions. /accounts/123 not /getAccount?id=123. (2) Stateless — each request contains all information needed; server holds no session state. (3) Uniform interface — HTTP verbs (GET, POST, PUT, PATCH, DELETE) convey intent. (4) Layered system — intermediaries (caches, load balancers) are transparent to clients. (5) HATEOAS — responses include links to related actions (hypermedia); largely ignored in practice.

gRPC uses Protocol Buffers (binary serialisation), HTTP/2 for transport, and a schema-first contract (.proto file). Advantages: 5-10x smaller payload than JSON, bidirectional streaming, strongly typed contract, generated client stubs in any language. Disadvantages: less human-readable, harder to test without tooling, not natively browser-accessible without grpc-web.

When to use each: REST — public APIs, browser clients, broad interoperability, simple request/response. gRPC — internal service-to-service communication, high-throughput microservices, streaming data, polyglot environments where codegen reduces integration effort.

On GCP: Cloud Endpoints and Apigee support both. Cloud Run and GKE can serve gRPC natively. Global External Application Load Balancer supports gRPC (HTTP/2 backend protocol).
API2How do you design API versioning for a platform that will have multiple clients with different upgrade cadences?
API VersioningIntermediate
▼
API versioning must balance backward compatibility (don't break existing clients) with forward evolution (allow the API to improve). Three common strategies:

1. URL path versioning (most common in enterprise): /v1/accounts, /v2/accounts. Clear, explicit, cacheable. Clients can predictably pin to a version. Operationally: maintain N and N-1 versions simultaneously, deprecate with 6-12 month notice.

2. Header versioning: Accept: application/vnd.company.v2+json. Keeps URLs clean but makes versioning invisible in browser and harder to route at the load balancer. Better for internal APIs.

3. Query parameter versioning: /accounts?api-version=2024-01-01. Used by Azure, Stripe. Date-based versions make deprecation timelines explicit.

Recommended approach for a platform team: URL path versioning for all external and partner APIs — operationally explicit, easy to route via URL maps in API Gateway or Apigee. Header versioning for internal service-to-service where you control all clients and can do coordinated upgrades.

Backward compatibility rules: NEVER remove a field or change its type in a released version. Adding optional fields is always backward compatible. Adding required fields is a breaking change — requires a new version or a migration period where the field is optional-then-required.

Deprecation lifecycle: Announce deprecation at least 6 months before removal. Monitor per-version traffic in Apigee/Cloud Endpoints. Remove only when traffic drops to zero or SLA window closes.
API3Compare Apigee and API Gateway on GCP — when would you choose each for a financial services platform?
API GatewaySenior
▼
Both services provide API management, but at very different scales and feature levels.

Cloud API Gateway: Lightweight, serverless, low-overhead. Backed by Envoy. Provides: OpenAPI spec-based routing, IAM-based authentication, Cloud Endpoints service management (quotas, monitoring). Best for: internal microservice APIs where you need auth + routing without enterprise policy management. Managed per-project.

Apigee: Enterprise-grade API management platform. Features include: developer portal, OAuth 2.0 / API key management, rate limiting by product tier, request/response transformation, analytics with 90-day retention, monetisation, security policies (Threat Protection, JSON/XML validation, OWASP patterns), multi-cloud deployment (hybrid/private cloud). Best for: partner/customer-facing APIs, regulated industries, API program management at scale.

For a financial institution: Apigee is the clear choice for external and partner APIs because: (1) PCI-DSS compliance — Apigee provides audit logs, threat detection, and security policies meeting requirements. (2) Rate limiting and SLA management per customer/partner. (3) Developer portal for onboarding banking partners. (4) mTLS support for B2B bank-to-bank communication. (5) Response masking — prevent sensitive fields (PAN, CVV) from appearing in analytics logs.

Combined pattern (common): Apigee for external/partner APIs, Cloud API Gateway for internal service mesh APIs where the overhead of Apigee is unnecessary.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank API/integration architecture is directly relevant here.
API4How do you implement API-first design and what does a contract-first workflow look like in practice?
OpenAPI / Contract DesignIntermediate
▼
API-first means the API contract (OpenAPI spec or Protobuf) is written and reviewed before any implementation begins. The contract is the product specification, not a generated artefact.

Workflow:
(1) Design the contract: API designer (platform architect or product team) writes the OpenAPI 3.0 YAML. Defines endpoints, request/response schemas, error codes, authentication mechanism. Uses tools like Stoplight Studio or Swagger Editor — visual editors catch inconsistencies early.
(2) Contract review: All consuming teams (frontend, mobile, partner integrations) review and give feedback before any backend code is written. This is where breaking change discussions happen cheaply.
(3) Generate mocks: Prism (Stoplight) or Mockoon generates a mock server from the OpenAPI spec. Consuming teams can develop against the mock in parallel with backend implementation.
(4) Implementation: Backend team implements against the contract. Contract validation tests (Dredd, schemathesis) run in CI to verify the implementation matches the spec.
(5) Contract testing in CI: Consumer-driven contract tests (Pact) verify that producer responses match what consumers expect — catching breaking changes before they reach production.

On GCP: Store OpenAPI specs in GCS or Git. Register with Cloud Endpoints for management. Deploy Apigee proxy generated from the spec. Use Artifact Registry for spec versioning.

Financial services benefit: Contract-first documents the API surface for QSA audits. The OpenAPI spec becomes part of the compliance artefact package.
API5How do you design a multi-tiered rate limiting strategy for a public financial services API platform?
Rate Limiting & ThrottlingSenior
▼
Rate limiting protects the platform from abuse while ensuring fair access across customer tiers. A multi-tiered strategy applies limits at different granularities.

Tier structure: (1) Global rate limit: total requests per second across all clients — protects infrastructure. Applied at Cloud Armor or Apigee global policy. (2) API product tier: different limits per product tier (Free: 100 rpm, Standard: 1,000 rpm, Premium: 10,000 rpm). Applied at Apigee product level — clients get different API keys with different quota assignments. (3) Per-client / per-IP: prevents a single compromised key from exhausting a tier's quota. Applied at Apigee consumer level. (4) Per-endpoint: sensitive endpoints (authentication, payment initiation) have stricter limits regardless of tier. Applied via Apigee conditional flow policies.

Response design: Always return 429 Too Many Requests with: Retry-After header (seconds until quota resets), X-RateLimit-Limit (total allowed), X-RateLimit-Remaining (remaining in window), X-RateLimit-Reset (epoch timestamp). This allows clients to implement graceful back-off without needing documentation.

Distributed rate limiting (for multiple API Gateway replicas): Use Memorystore Redis for shared counter state — atomic INCR + EXPIRE operations ensure accurate counting across replicas. Sliding window algorithm (more accurate than fixed window, which allows 2x burst at window boundaries).

Burst handling: Token bucket algorithm allows controlled bursting — a client can burst above the rate limit for short periods (drawing down tokens) then must slow down. Better UX than hard rejection at the first exceedance.
API6Walk me through the OAuth 2.0 flows relevant to a financial services API platform and when you use each.
OAuth2 & API SecuritySenior
▼
OAuth 2.0 provides delegated authorisation — clients access resources on behalf of resource owners without sharing credentials. Four flows for different client types:

1. Authorization Code with PKCE (public clients / web apps): User is redirected to the authorisation server (Cloud Identity, OKTA), authenticates, grants consent. The server returns a code. The client exchanges the code (+ PKCE verifier) for access and refresh tokens. Use for: customer-facing web and mobile applications where the resource owner is an end user. PKCE is mandatory for public clients — prevents authorization code interception attacks.

2. Client Credentials (machine-to-machine): Client sends its client_id and client_secret directly to the token endpoint. Receives an access token. No user involved. Use for: B2B bank-to-bank integration (your partner bank's system calling your API), internal service-to-service within controlled networks. Complement with mTLS (client certificate + client credentials) for highest assurance level — required by Open Banking standards.

3. Device Code (IoT / headless devices): Device displays a code, user authenticates on a secondary device. Use for: ATMs, in-branch kiosks, trading terminals without keyboards.

4. Implicit (DEPRECATED): Do not use. Superseded by Authorization Code + PKCE.

Token design for FinServ: JWTs for stateless validation at API Gateway (no round-trip to auth server on every request). Short expiry (15 minutes for high-value transactions, 1 hour for read APIs). Refresh token rotation — old refresh token invalidated on use (detects token theft). Token binding — bind tokens to mTLS certificate thumbprint (FAPI-compliant for Open Banking).
API7When would you recommend GraphQL over REST for a platform API, and what are the risks?
GraphQL vs RESTIntermediate
▼
GraphQL allows clients to request exactly the fields they need in a single query, avoiding over-fetching (REST returning more than needed) and under-fetching (needing multiple REST calls to assemble a view).

When GraphQL makes sense: (1) Complex, interconnected data models with many relationships — e.g., a portfolio management API where a dashboard needs account, positions, transactions, market data, and user preferences in one query. (2) Multiple client types (mobile, web, TV) with different data requirements — each can ask for its own field subset. (3) Rapid product iteration — frontend teams can adjust queries without backend API changes. (4) BFF (Backend for Frontend) pattern — a GraphQL layer sits between clients and REST microservices, composing the response.

Risks and mitigations: (1) Query complexity attacks: a malicious or badly designed query can traverse millions of nodes. Mitigate with query depth limits (max depth: 10), query complexity scoring (each field costs N points; reject queries over threshold), and query allowlisting in production (only pre-approved queries). (2) N+1 problem: resolving a list of accounts may fire one query per account for related data. Mitigate with DataLoader (batch + cache). (3) Caching: REST responses are easily cached at CDN level by URL. GraphQL POST requests are not cacheable by default. Use persisted queries (GET with query hash). (4) Schema governance: without a contract review process, the schema becomes chaotic at scale. Implement Apollo schema checks in CI/CD.

Financial services caution: For regulated APIs (Open Banking, PSD2), REST with OpenAPI spec is mandated by the standards. GraphQL is more appropriate for internal platform APIs and customer product dashboards.
API8How do you design an event-driven API using webhooks and how does it differ from a polling or streaming approach?
Event-Driven APIsSenior
▼
Event-driven APIs notify consumers when something happens, rather than requiring them to periodically check (poll) or maintain a persistent connection (stream).

Webhooks (HTTP callbacks): The consumer registers a callback URL. The producer sends an HTTP POST to that URL when an event occurs. The consumer responds with 200 OK to acknowledge receipt.

Design requirements for reliable webhooks:
(1) At-least-once delivery: retry on failure (exponential back-off: 1s → 5s → 30s → 5min → 30min). Log delivery attempts and outcomes per event.
(2) Idempotency key: include a unique event_id in every delivery. Consumers must deduplicate by event_id — network retries can cause the same event to be delivered multiple times.
(3) Signature verification: sign the payload with HMAC-SHA256 using a shared secret. Consumer verifies signature before processing — prevents spoofed events. Rotate secrets without downtime using dual-secret overlap.
(4) Envelope schema: standardise payload structure: {event_id, event_type, api_version, timestamp, resource_type, resource_id, data: {...}}. Consumers can route and filter by event_type without parsing the data field.
(5) Dead letter management: events failing delivery after N retries go to a dead letter queue. Provide a replay API so consumers can re-request missed events.

Webhook vs polling vs streaming: Polling — simple but inefficient (unnecessary requests when no change, latency proportional to poll interval). Webhooks — efficient, near-real-time, but consumer must expose a public HTTPS endpoint. Server-Sent Events (SSE) — server pushes events over HTTP, suitable for browser clients. gRPC streaming — bidirectional, highest throughput, internal use only.

On GCP: Eventarc delivers GCP events (Cloud Storage, BigQuery, Pub/Sub) to Cloud Run endpoints via webhooks. For custom webhook delivery at scale: Pub/Sub → Cloud Run per consumer, with Cloud Tasks for retry management.
API9What HTTP security headers should every API response include, and how do you enforce them on GCP?
API Security HeadersIntermediate
▼
Security headers reduce the attack surface at the HTTP layer. For financial services APIs, these are non-negotiable:

Essential headers:
(1) Strict-Transport-Security: max-age=31536000; includeSubDomains — forces HTTPS for 1 year. First-visit attack only; supplement with HSTS preload submission for new APIs.
(2) Content-Security-Policy: for API responses (JSON), a strict CSP prevents response content from being interpreted as executable by a browser: default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none'.
(3) X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff — prevents browsers from MIME-sniffing the content type. Stops content injection attacks where an API response is misinterpreted as HTML or JavaScript.
(4) X-Frame-Options: DENY — prevents the API response from being loaded in an iframe (clickjacking). Superseded by CSP frame-ancestors but include both for compatibility.
(5) Referrer-Policy: no-referrer — prevents the API URL (which may contain path parameters with sensitive data) from appearing in Referer headers to third parties.
(6) Permissions-Policy: disable all browser features the API doesn't use: geolocation=(), microphone=(), camera=().
(7) Cache-Control: no-store, max-age=0 — for sensitive API responses (account data, transactions). Prevents caching of sensitive data in proxy servers or browser history.

Enforcement on GCP: Add response headers via Apigee AssignMessage policy (applied to all proxies in an environment via Shared Flow). For Cloud Run: implement as middleware in the application framework (Express, FastAPI). Cloud Armor does not add response headers — implement in the application or gateway layer.
API10How do you design comprehensive API observability for a production financial services API platform?
API ObservabilitySenior
▼
API observability goes beyond uptime monitoring — it provides the business and technical context needed to understand platform health, debug issues, and meet SLAs.

The four signals:
(1) Metrics: request rate (rpm), error rate (% 4xx, % 5xx), latency distribution (p50, p95, p99, p99.9), payload size distribution. Emitted from Apigee Analytics or Cloud Endpoints to Cloud Monitoring. Alert on: error rate > 1%, p99 latency > SLA threshold, error spike (sudden >3x increase).
(2) Logs: structured JSON logs per request: client_id, api_product, endpoint, method, status_code, latency_ms, request_size, response_size, correlation_id, country, ip. Never log request/response body (PCI-DSS) — log metadata only. Export to BigQuery for ad-hoc analysis and SIEM for security correlation.
(3) Traces: distributed tracing with Cloud Trace. Inject trace context (traceparent header) at the API gateway. Trace propagates through all downstream services. Identify which service in the call chain is causing latency.
(4) Business metrics: API calls per product, per partner, per endpoint over time. Revenue attribution per API product (for monetised APIs). Funnel analysis — where do developers drop off in onboarding?

SLO framework for APIs: Define SLOs per API product: availability (99.9%), latency (p99 < 500ms), error rate (< 0.1%). Error budget tracking in Cloud Monitoring SLO feature. Burn rate alerts (fast burn: page immediately; slow burn: ticket for next sprint).

Apigee-specific: Apigee Analytics provides out-of-box dashboards. Custom reports on: top error-producing apps, slowest endpoints, geographic traffic distribution, quota utilisation per client. Export to BigQuery for custom correlation analysis.
API11How do you design consistent, actionable error responses across a multi-service API platform?
API Error DesignIntermediate
▼
Inconsistent error responses are one of the biggest developer experience failures in platform APIs — consuming teams cannot build reliable error handling if every service returns a different format.

Standard error envelope:
{
  "error": {
    "code": "ACCOUNT_NOT_FOUND",
    "message": "No account found with ID acc_12345",
    "status": 404,
    "trace_id": "abc123xyz",
    "documentation_url": "https://developer.bank.com/errors/ACCOUNT_NOT_FOUND"
  }
}

Design rules:
(1) Machine-readable code: a stable string constant the client can switch on. Never rely on the human-readable message to drive logic. The code never changes between API versions; the message can be localised or improved.
(2) HTTP status code alignment: 400 (Bad Request — client validation), 401 (Unauthenticated — missing/expired token), 403 (Forbidden — valid token, insufficient permissions), 404 (Not Found), 409 (Conflict — e.g., duplicate request), 422 (Unprocessable — validation failure with context), 429 (Rate limited — with Retry-After header), 500 (Server error — never expose internals).
(3) Never expose internals: stack traces, database error messages, internal service names — all are security vulnerabilities and compliance risks. Sanitise at the API gateway before returning to clients.
(4) Correlation ID: include a trace_id in every error response. Clients include this in support tickets. Your support team can find the full trace in Cloud Trace within seconds.
(5) Validation errors include field context: 422 responses should include which field failed and why.

Enforcement on GCP: Apigee FaultRules define standard error formats. Catch any upstream service error in the Apigee PostClientFlow and reformat it to the standard envelope before returning to the consumer.
API12Explain the Backend for Frontend (BFF) pattern and when you would implement it on GCP.
API Gateway PatternsSenior
▼
The BFF (Backend for Frontend) pattern creates a dedicated API layer for each client type (web, mobile, partner), rather than having all clients consume the same generic API.

Why BFF: A generic API must satisfy all clients simultaneously — it becomes bloated with optional fields, multiple response formats, and complex conditional logic. Mobile apps need small payloads, low latency, offline-friendly structures. Web dashboards need rich aggregated data with multiple joined resources. Partner integrations need stable, strictly versioned contracts. These requirements are in direct conflict in a single API.

Architecture on GCP: Each BFF is a Cloud Run service. BFFs are owned by the team that owns the corresponding client. BFFs call downstream domain APIs (account service, payment service, notification service) — they compose and transform, they don't own data. Apigee sits in front of all BFFs for auth, rate limiting, and observability. Internal downstream APIs are reached via Private Service Connect or internal load balancers — not through Apigee.

What a BFF does: Aggregates multiple service calls (parallel, fan-out pattern). Transforms response shape for client consumption (e.g., mobile BFF strips large fields, adds mobile-specific pagination). Handles client-specific auth flows (mobile BFF handles PKCE, partner BFF handles mTLS). Caches aggressively for mobile (Memorystore Redis) — the mobile client has high latency tolerance for cached data but needs sub-100ms for fresh data.

What a BFF does NOT do: Own business logic. Write to databases. Make decisions about data. Enforce security rules. These stay in the domain services — the BFF is a composition and transformation layer only.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank API architecture and Apigee experience positions you to describe a real BFF implementation.
API13How do you implement contract testing for APIs in a microservices architecture to prevent breaking changes?
API Contract TestingSenior
▼
Contract testing verifies that an API producer's implementation matches what consumers expect — catching breaking changes before they reach shared environments.

Consumer-Driven Contract Testing (CDCT) with Pact:
(1) Consumer writes tests: the consuming service (frontend, mobile, partner adapter) writes Pact tests that define exactly what responses they expect for specific requests. These expectations are serialised to a Pact file (JSON).
(2) Pact file published: consumer CI pipeline publishes the Pact file to a Pact Broker (can-i-deploy? will tell you if consumer and provider are compatible).
(3) Provider verifies: producer CI pipeline downloads all consumer Pact files and runs provider verification tests — replaying the consumer's expected requests against the real API and verifying the responses match expectations.
(4) Deployment gate: can-i-deploy? command in CI blocks deployment if provider verification fails for any consumer. Breaking changes are caught before any code reaches the environment where consumers could be affected.

Complementary approach — OpenAPI contract validation: Tools like schemathesis generate property-based tests from the OpenAPI spec. Run against the deployed API to catch spec drift (implementation diverging from contract).

On GCP: Pact Broker deployed to Cloud Run. Pact files stored in GCS. GitHub Actions runs consumer tests on PR, provider verification on merge. Cloud Build triggers cross-service verification when producer deploys.

What contract testing replaces: Expensive end-to-end integration tests in shared environments. It does NOT replace integration testing entirely — it catches contract violations early, before you even need a shared environment.
API14How do you manage API versioning, deprecation, and sunset for a platform with long-lived partner integrations?
API DeprecationIntermediate
▼
Financial services API consumers (partner banks, fintech integrators) are slow to upgrade — contracts, compliance reviews, and enterprise change processes mean 12-24 month migration timelines are realistic. Your deprecation strategy must account for this.

Deprecation lifecycle:
(1) Announce: publish deprecation notice at minimum 12 months before sunset for public/partner APIs. Include in Changelog, email to registered consumers, API response headers (Deprecation: date, Sunset: date, Link: migration-guide-url).
(2) Measure: use Apigee analytics to track version traffic by consumer. Export to BigQuery — build a migration tracking dashboard: which consumers are still on v1, how fast is traffic declining.
(3) Engage: proactively contact consumers still on deprecated version at T-6 months and T-3 months. Provide migration support — a migration guide, sandbox access, support tickets.
(4) Sunset: at the sunset date, return 410 Gone (not 404) with a migration guide URL. Never silently proxy to a new version — that hides the migration need from monitoring.
(5) Remove: after sunset, retire infrastructure. Keep documentation online for 12 months post-sunset.

Apigee implementation: Traffic to deprecated version emits a deprecation_warning metric to Cloud Monitoring. Alert fires when traffic increases (regression — a consumer who upgraded has rolled back). Sunset policy automatically returns 410 at the configured date without code change.

Parallel versions: Never run more than N and N-1 simultaneously. Running 3+ versions simultaneously creates unsustainable test and support burden — enforce this as a governance policy.
API15How do you design a layered API architecture that distinguishes internal platform APIs from external customer-facing APIs?
Internal vs External APISenior
▼
Different API consumers have different requirements for stability, security, discovery, and governance. A layered architecture explicitly separates them.

Three-layer model:
(1) System APIs (bottom layer): fine-grained, service-specific APIs owned by each domain team. Account Service API, Payment Service API, Notification Service API. Not directly accessible to external consumers — only called by the layers above or by peer services. High change velocity, versioned for internal compatibility only, may use gRPC.
(2) Process APIs (middle layer): orchestration APIs that compose multiple System APIs to implement a business process. Onboarding Process API calls Account Service + KYC Service + Notification Service. Process APIs are owned by cross-functional teams. REST, medium change velocity.
(3) Experience APIs (top layer, = BFFs): tailored for specific consumer types. Mobile Experience API, Partner Integration API, Internal Dashboard API. Owned by the teams closest to the consumers. Highest stability guarantee — partner contracts depend on these.

Security boundary: System APIs and Process APIs are private — only accessible via Private Service Connect or internal load balancers, never over the internet. Experience APIs are exposed via Apigee with OAuth 2.0 and API key management.

Governance: System APIs have internal SLAs (99.9%). Process APIs have customer-facing SLAs (99.95%). Experience APIs have partner SLAs (99.99% with penalties). Different change management processes at each layer — internal changes can move fast, partner-facing changes require CAB and advance notice.

🏢 Enterprise Architecture Design
