EA1What enterprise architecture frameworks do you know, and how do they apply to cloud platform design?
Architecture FrameworksCore
▼
Enterprise architecture frameworks provide structured approaches to aligning IT capabilities with business strategy. Three are most relevant in practice:

TOGAF (The Open Group Architecture Framework): The dominant EA framework globally. Uses the Architecture Development Method (ADM) — a cycle of phases from Architecture Vision through Requirements, Business Architecture, Information Architecture, Application Architecture, Technology Architecture, through Migration Planning and Change Management. Most relevant phase for cloud architects: Technology Architecture (mapping to cloud services) and Migration Planning (Azure-to-GCP type programmes). TOGAF's ArchiMate modelling language provides a standardised way to document architecture views.

Zachman Framework: A classification matrix rather than a process. Rows = stakeholder perspectives (Contextual, Conceptual, Logical, Physical, Detailed, Functioning). Columns = architecture dimensions (What, How, Where, Who, When, Why). Useful for ensuring architecture is documented from all stakeholder perspectives — forces you to consider the business, data, application, and technology dimensions explicitly.

AWS/Google Cloud Well-Architected Framework: The practical, cloud-native equivalent. Google's version has 6 pillars: Operational Excellence, Security, Reliability, Performance Efficiency, Cost Optimisation, Sustainability. Most immediately applicable to daily cloud architecture decisions.

How I apply these: TOGAF vocabulary when communicating with enterprise EA teams who use it. Well-Architected Framework for technical design reviews — I walk through each pillar as a lens. Zachman to ensure I've considered the business and data dimensions, not just technology. Pragmatic use: frameworks are communication and thinking tools, not box-ticking exercises.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your 19 years in enterprise environments — mention where you've encountered formal EA governance and how you worked within it.
EA2How do you design and operate an Architecture Decision Record (ADR) process at enterprise scale?
ADR ProcessIntermediate
▼
Architecture Decision Records (ADRs) are the working memory of an architecture — they document not just what was decided but why, and what alternatives were considered.

ADR template (minimal): Title → Status (Proposed | Accepted | Deprecated | Superseded) → Context (what problem are we solving?) → Decision (what did we decide and why?) → Consequences (what does this enable? what does it constrain? what technical debt does it create?) → Alternatives considered (with brief notes on why they were rejected).

ADR process at enterprise scale:
(1) Where ADRs live: Git repository, as Markdown files in /docs/decisions/. Co-located with code means ADRs are reviewed alongside code PRs. Searchable via grep. Version-controlled automatically.
(2) When to write an ADR: any decision that is hard to reverse, affects more than one team, or will be questioned in the future. Test: "Would a new team member ask why we did it this way?" If yes — write an ADR.
(3) Governance: ADRs for platform-wide decisions require review from the Architecture Guild (senior engineers from each team). ADRs for team-level decisions can be reviewed within the team. Use GitHub PR review process — reviewers are explicit, comments are auditable.
(4) Discovery: an ADR index page lists all decisions by status and topic. Superseded ADRs remain in history — you can trace the evolution of a decision over time. This prevents re-litigating decisions that were already made.

ADR anti-patterns to avoid: Writing ADRs after the fact (just documentation). ADRs that are so long no one reads them. ADRs with no "alternatives considered" section (looks like decision was made before the ADR was written). Decisions recorded as ADRs but never applied (governance with no enforcement).

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank ADR practice — describe a specific decision where the ADR process prevented rework or resolved a disagreement.
EA3How would you build and operate a technology radar for a large platform engineering organisation?
Technology RadarSenior
▼
A technology radar is a curated list of technologies — languages, frameworks, platforms, tools, techniques — categorised by their adoption recommendation. The most famous is ThoughtWorks Technology Radar, published quarterly.

Four quadrants: Techniques (processes and approaches), Platforms (cloud services, databases, infrastructure), Tools (developer tooling), Languages & Frameworks.

Four rings: Adopt (use in production now — battle-tested, team-familiar, high confidence). Trial (use on a real project with expert guidance — promising, worth investing in). Assess (worth exploring — R&D spike or prototype). Hold (avoid new usage — has concerns, better alternatives exist, or sunset in progress).

Operating the radar at enterprise scale:
(1) Quarterly update cycle: Engineering leads from each domain submit technology nominations. The Architecture Guild reviews, discusses, and reaches consensus. Published to an internal site (Radar Visualization tool from ThoughtWorks, or Backstage plugin).
(2) Technology governance: Adopt = no approval needed. Trial = tech lead approval + evaluation report required. Assess = Architecture Guild awareness only. Hold = new usage requires Architecture Guild exception + waiver process.
(3) Relationship to procurement: Hold status triggers vendor contract non-renewal review. Adopt status may trigger preferred vendor agreements.

For a GCP platform specifically: Track GCP preview features in Assess. Move to Trial as they GA. Move to Adopt once the platform team has production experience. Examples: GKE Autopilot (Trial → Adopt over 2 years), Vertex AI Agent Builder (Assess → Trial now for most teams).

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Whether or not your org had a formal radar, you implicitly operated one — decisions about which tools to standardise on across Scotiabank's GCP platform.
EA4What is a business capability model and how does it inform cloud platform architecture decisions?
Capability ModellingSenior
▼
A business capability model maps what a business does (capabilities) independently of how it does it (processes, people, technology). Capabilities are stable — they change much more slowly than the technology that supports them.

Why capabilities matter for architecture: When designing a platform, if you anchor your design to business processes or org structure, you inherit all their complexity and churn. If you anchor to capabilities, you get a stable decomposition that remains valid across org reorganisations and technology changes.

Example for a bank: Level 1 capabilities: Customer Management, Account Management, Payment Processing, Risk Management, Compliance & Reporting, Channel Management. Each Level 1 decomposes into Level 2: Payment Processing → Domestic Payments, International Payments, Real-time Payments, Bill Payments. Level 2 maps to bounded contexts in your microservices architecture.

Capability heat mapping: Rate each capability on: Business Differentiating (we win or lose customers here) vs Business Enabling (necessary but not differentiating) vs Business Supporting (commodity). Investment priority: Differentiating = build and own. Enabling = buy SaaS. Supporting = buy commodity or offload to cloud managed service.

Application to GCP platform design: Real-time payments = differentiating → custom microservice + Cloud Spanner (global consistency). KYC onboarding = enabling → evaluate SaaS providers, or build on Vertex AI. Core banking ledger = supporting → Cloud SQL + well-architected patterns, not reinvention.

Platform architecture implication: Shared platform services (authentication, event bus, observability) are enabling capabilities — standardise them across all teams. Domain services (payment processing, account management) are owned by domain teams with their own data stores.
EA5How do you apply Domain-Driven Design (DDD) principles when designing a cloud platform architecture?
Domain-Driven DesignSenior
▼
Domain-Driven Design provides vocabulary and patterns for aligning software structure with business domain structure. Key concepts relevant to cloud architecture:

Bounded Context: A boundary within which a domain model is internally consistent. "Account" means different things to the core banking team (financial ledger entry) and the customer onboarding team (customer profile). Each has its own bounded context with its own model, data store, and API. Bounded contexts map to microservices (or small groups of microservices) in a GCP architecture — each with its own Cloud SQL or Firestore database.

Context Map: Documents how bounded contexts relate. Relationship types: Partnership (two teams coordinate), Customer-Supplier (upstream provides what downstream needs), Conformist (downstream accepts upstream's model), Anti-Corruption Layer (downstream translates upstream's model to its own — critical when integrating with legacy core banking systems).

Aggregate: A cluster of entities and value objects with a root entity (Aggregate Root) that is the only entry point for external modification. Aggregates define transaction boundaries — one database transaction per aggregate. This maps to GCP data design: the aggregate root ID is the Firestore document ID or Cloud SQL primary key.

Domain Events: Something significant that happened in the domain — AccountOpened, PaymentInitiated, FraudFlagRaised. Domain events are the natural fit for Pub/Sub messages. Downstream bounded contexts subscribe to events from upstream contexts without tight coupling.

Application to financial platform: Each domain team owns their bounded context's microservices and database. Integration happens via domain events (Pub/Sub) or Anti-Corruption Layer adapters (for legacy mainframe integration). The platform team owns the shared infrastructure (Pub/Sub, shared VPC, logging) but not the domain models.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank Azure-to-GCP migration — describe how you identified bounded contexts and designed integration patterns.
EA6How do you decide between a microservices architecture and a well-structured monolith for a new platform?
Microservices vs MonolithSenior
▼
The microservices vs monolith decision is frequently made wrong in both directions — over-engineering with microservices before the domain is understood, or under-investing in a monolith until it becomes unmaintainable.

Arguments for starting with a monolith (monolith-first): (1) When the domain model is not yet understood, microservice boundaries drawn too early will be wrong — splitting incorrectly causes expensive refactoring and distributed monolith patterns. (2) A monolith is faster to develop initially — no network calls, no distributed transaction complexity, single deployment. (3) If the team is small (< 8 engineers), the coordination overhead of microservices outweighs the benefits. (4) Scaling profile: if the entire application needs to scale together (no component is disproportionately trafficked), microservices add cost without benefit.

Arguments for microservices from the start: (1) Independently scaling components with very different resource profiles (a payment processing engine vs an analytics dashboard). (2) Multiple teams needing independent release cadences — a microservice per team is the enabler. (3) Mixed technology stacks required by different components (ML team needs Python, backend team uses Go). (4) Compliance boundaries — PCI-DSS scope reduction by isolating cardholder data in a dedicated service.

Decision framework: Start with a modular monolith (clear internal module boundaries, no shared databases between modules). Extract to a microservice when: a module has significantly different scaling requirements, a team needs independent deployment, or a compliance boundary requires physical separation.

The distributed monolith anti-pattern: Microservices that share a database or make synchronous calls across the majority of operations — all the complexity of microservices with none of the independence benefits. Avoid at design time by enforcing: one database per service, asynchronous-first communication, no direct inter-service database access.
EA7When would you recommend Event Sourcing and CQRS patterns, and how would you implement them on GCP?
Event Sourcing & CQRSSenior
▼
Event Sourcing: Instead of storing current state, store the sequence of events that led to the current state. The state is derived by replaying events. Example: a bank account's balance is not stored directly — it is computed by replaying all Deposited and Withdrawn events from the account's beginning.

Benefits: Complete audit trail (every state change is recorded with who, when, why — ideal for financial compliance). Easy temporal queries (what was the account balance on a specific date?). Event replay for debugging, testing, and projecting new read models. Natural fit for event-driven architectures — events are the source of truth.

Costs: Query complexity — you cannot do a simple SELECT to get current state. Eventual consistency — the current state projection may lag behind the event stream. Upcasting — old event schemas must be maintained or migrated as the domain model evolves.

CQRS (Command Query Responsibility Segregation): Separate the write model (commands that change state) from the read model (queries that return data). CQRS is almost always combined with Event Sourcing but can be used independently. The write side is an append-only event store. The read side is one or more projections optimised for query patterns — each projection can be a different data store.

GCP implementation: Event store: Pub/Sub for event stream + Bigtable for event history (row key = aggregate_id#reverse_timestamp, queryable by time range). Command side: Cloud Run service that validates commands and appends events. Projections: Dataflow consumes the event stream and writes optimised views to BigQuery (analytics), Firestore (user-facing API), Bigtable (operational lookups).

When to use: Financial ledgers, audit-required systems, collaborative editing (conflict resolution via event ordering). When NOT to use: Simple CRUD applications where an audit log is sufficient and the query patterns are straightforward.
EA8How do you quantify, prioritise, and systematically pay down technical debt in a large platform?
Technical Debt ManagementSenior
▼
Technical debt is unavoidable in any evolving system — the goal is to manage it intentionally, not eliminate it entirely. Unmanaged debt compounds, creating a cycle where teams spend more time working around debt than delivering value.

Quantifying debt: (1) Discovery: SonarQube or CodeClimate for code-level debt (code smells, cyclomatic complexity, duplications). tfsec/Checkov for IaC debt. Manual architecture review for structural debt (tight coupling, data model inconsistencies). Security Command Center for infrastructure debt (unpatched vulnerabilities, misconfigured resources). (2) Classification: Reckless deliberate debt ("we know it's wrong, let's ship anyway"). Prudent deliberate debt ("ship now, fix next sprint — and you actually do"). Reckless inadvertent debt (didn't know at the time — most common). (3) Cost estimation: for each debt item, estimate: effort to pay it down, cost of not paying it (team velocity drag, incident risk, compliance risk, cost of future rework). This turns a subjective "we should fix it" into a business case.

Prioritisation framework: Priority 1: debt that is causing active harm (incidents, compliance failures, security vulnerabilities). Priority 2: debt that blocks current work (a bad database schema that makes every new feature hard). Priority 3: debt that will cost more to fix later (poor API design before too many consumers). Priority 4: cosmetic debt (naming inconsistencies — acknowledge, document, deprioritise).

Process: 20% rule — allocate 20% of every sprint to debt reduction. Create a debt backlog in Jira with business-case justification. Debt retros quarterly — review what was paid down, what was added, and whether the backlog is growing or shrinking. Rising backlog = structural problem, not sprint execution problem.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your experience taking Scotiabank's platform from 15% Terraform coverage to 94% is an excellent technical debt pay-down story.
EA9Explain the Strangler Fig pattern and how you would apply it to migrate a legacy mainframe integration to GCP.
Strangler Fig PatternSenior
▼
The Strangler Fig pattern gradually replaces a legacy system by building new functionality around it, routing specific traffic to the new system while the legacy handles the rest. Over time, the new system "strangles" the legacy until it can be decommissioned — without a big-bang cutover.

Why it works: Big-bang migrations fail because they require all functionality to be reimplemented simultaneously before any value is delivered. The Strangler Fig delivers value incrementally, allows learning and adjustment, and reduces risk by keeping the legacy as a fallback throughout.

Applied to mainframe integration on GCP: Phase 1 — Intercept: introduce an Anti-Corruption Layer (ACL) as a proxy between all systems calling the mainframe. The ACL translates calls and responses, initially 100% passing through to mainframe unchanged. This establishes the interception point without changing any caller. Phase 2 — Migrate by function: identify discrete functions (account inquiry, balance lookup, transaction history) and migrate one at a time to a new GCP microservice. The ACL routes that function's traffic to GCP while remaining functions still hit mainframe. Phase 3 — Validate and cut: run shadow mode — new GCP service handles the request but the mainframe result is returned. Compare results for consistency. When results match for N days/weeks, cut the routing to GCP-primary. Phase 4 — Strangle: once all functions are migrated and validated, the mainframe is decommissioned.

GCP implementation of the ACL: Cloud Run service as the proxy. Routing rules in config (can be updated without redeployment). Feature flags (Flagsmith or GCP Remote Config) to control per-function routing percentages. Dual-write during migration to validate consistency.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Azure-to-GCP migration at Scotiabank used similar principles — describe how you phased the migration using traffic splitting.
EA10How do you capture, prioritise, and architect for non-functional requirements (NFRs) in an enterprise platform?
Non-Functional RequirementsSenior
▼
Non-functional requirements (NFRs) — also called quality attributes or architectural characteristics — define how a system behaves rather than what it does. They are frequently under-specified at the start of projects, leading to costly rework or production failures.

Key NFR categories for a financial platform:
Availability: What is the SLA? 99.9% (8.7h/yr downtime), 99.95% (4.4h/yr), 99.99% (53min/yr)? Each tier has dramatically different architectural and cost implications. A jump from 99.9% to 99.99% typically requires multi-region active-active deployment — a 10x cost increase. Get an explicit business decision on this number.
Latency: P99 latency threshold for each user-facing operation. Transactional APIs: <200ms. Reporting APIs: <2s. Background batch: no latency constraint. These drive database choice, caching strategy, and compute sizing.
Throughput: Peak transactions per second + growth rate. Design for 3x expected peak (safety margin for marketing-driven spikes).
Data retention: Compliance mandates 7 years for financial transaction records. Operational data: 90 days in hot storage, 7 years in cold storage. These drive storage architecture and cost.
Recovery: RPO (maximum data loss if system fails) and RTO (how quickly the system is available again). RPO=0, RTO<1min = active-active. RPO<1hr, RTO<4hr = warm standby. RPO<24hr, RTO<48hr = backup restore.

Capturing NFRs: NFR workshop at architecture inception — walk through each category with business and technical stakeholders. Get explicit sign-off. Record in the architecture document. Re-validate at each design milestone.

Architecture fitness functions: Automate NFR validation — performance tests in CI/CD that fail the build if P99 latency exceeds the NFR threshold. These prevent performance regression from going unnoticed until production.
EA11How do you design an architecture governance model that enables fast delivery without creating a bureaucratic bottleneck?
Architecture GovernanceSenior
▼
Architecture governance fails in two ways: insufficient governance (inconsistent decisions, accumulated debt, compliance failures) and excessive governance (everything needs sign-off, nothing moves fast). The goal is lightweight governance that makes the right choice the easy choice.

Principles: (1) Paved roads not guard rails: make the correct architecture easy to use (golden path templates, blessed modules, one-click project provisioning) rather than making the wrong architecture hard to use through committees. (2) Asynchronous review by default: Architecture review happens via PR, not a meeting. The ADR is the review artefact. Reviews are time-boxed — if no feedback in 5 business days, the decision is approved. (3) Escalation path for disagreements: if stakeholders disagree on an ADR, the Architecture Guild arbitrates within 2 weeks — not an indefinite open debate. (4) Exceptions with waiver: a formal waiver process for deviations from standards, requiring documented justification and a time-bound remediation plan.

Governance tiers: Team level (decisions affecting only one service): team lead approves, ADR in team repo. Platform level (decisions affecting shared infrastructure or multiple teams): Architecture Guild review, ADR in platform repo. Enterprise level (technology radar changes, new cloud providers, compliance frameworks): Architecture Board, formal sign-off.

Governance metrics: Time from ADR proposal to decision. % of deployments using approved platform modules (drift rate). % of projects with completed architecture review before production. These metrics tell you whether governance is working or creating drag.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank governance work — mentoring 20+ engineers, the Terraform module registry, the compliance framework that reduced audit findings by 40% — is a governance success story.
EA12How do you assess and improve the maturity of a platform engineering practice?
Platform Engineering MaturitySenior
▼
Platform engineering maturity is often measured informally — teams sense that things are getting better or worse without quantified evidence. Structured maturity assessment provides a baseline, a target, and a measurable improvement trajectory.

DORA (DevOps Research and Assessment) metrics: The most evidence-based measure of platform maturity. (1) Deployment frequency: how often does the team deploy to production? Low performers: monthly. High performers: multiple times per day. Platform engineering accelerates this by removing manual steps. (2) Lead time for changes: time from code commit to production. Low: weeks/months. High: <1 day. (3) Change failure rate: % of deployments causing incidents. Low: >15%. High: <5%. (4) Mean time to restore (MTTR): how quickly is the system restored after an incident? Low: days/weeks. High: <1 hour.

Platform-specific maturity dimensions: Self-service ratio (% of team requests fulfilled by self-service vs platform team intervention). Golden path adoption (% of services using platform-provided templates). IaC coverage (% of production infrastructure managed by code). Observability completeness (% of services with defined SLOs, dashboards, and runbooks). Security posture (SCC finding closure rate, IAM Recommender utilisation).

Maturity model (Level 1-5): 1 = ad-hoc (manual, heroics). 2 = defined (documented processes, some automation). 3 = measured (metrics tracked, continuous improvement). 4 = optimised (self-healing, automated governance). 5 = innovating (platform enables new business capabilities).

Improvement strategy: Identify the metric with the biggest gap from target. Trace it to its root cause. Build one automation or template that addresses it. Measure the improvement. Repeat.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank platform work took the team from a low-to-mid maturity level to a measurably higher one — the DORA metrics, 40% audit reduction, and $500K savings are evidence of that progression.
EA13What are the 12-factor app principles and how do you apply them to a GCP cloud-native platform?
Cloud-Native Design PrinciplesCore
▼
The 12-factor app methodology defines principles for building software-as-a-service applications that are portable, scalable, and maintainable. Originally articulated by Heroku engineers; remains the most practical cloud-native checklist.

12 factors and their GCP application:
(1) Codebase: one codebase per service in Git (GitHub), many deploys. (2) Dependencies: explicitly declared in requirements.txt / go.mod / package.json. Containerised with Dockerfile. (3) Config: config in environment — secrets from Secret Manager (never in code). Cloud Run env vars set at deploy time. (4) Backing services: databases, caches, queues are attached resources, not embedded. Cloud SQL, Memorystore, Pub/Sub are consumed via URLs/connection strings injected at deploy time. (5) Build, release, run: strictly separated. GitHub Actions builds the image, stores in Artifact Registry, creates a Release, Cloud Deploy deploys to environment. (6) Processes: stateless — Cloud Run services store no local state. Session data in Memorystore Redis. (7) Port binding: Cloud Run services bind to $PORT env var, expose via HTTP. (8) Concurrency: scale out via process model — Cloud Run scales replicas. Set max-concurrency per instance. (9) Disposability: fast startup (<10s) and graceful shutdown (drain in-flight requests on SIGTERM). Cloud Run sends SIGTERM before killing a container. (10) Dev/prod parity: use Cloud Run and Cloud SQL in all environments — no "it works on my laptop" with different backing services. (11) Logs: write to stdout as structured JSON — Cloud Logging ingests automatically from Cloud Run. (12) Admin processes: run admin tasks as one-off Cloud Run Jobs (database migrations, data backfills) — not long-running daemon processes.
EA14How do you choose between synchronous, asynchronous, and event-driven integration patterns in a complex enterprise platform?
Integration ArchitectureSenior
▼
Integration pattern selection has the largest long-term impact on system coupling, resilience, and operational complexity. The choice is not purely technical — it reflects how tightly the business processes are coupled.

Synchronous (request-response): Caller waits for the response before continuing. Pattern: REST/gRPC point-to-point. Best when: the consumer needs the response to proceed (payment authorisation — you need the Yes/No before completing the transaction). The operation is fast (<500ms). The consumer and provider must both be available simultaneously.

Risks: Cascading failures — if the downstream is slow or unavailable, callers queue up, creating a failure cascade. Temporal coupling — both services must be running simultaneously. Mitigate with: circuit breakers (fail fast when downstream is unhealthy), timeouts, retry with exponential back-off.

Asynchronous (message-passing): Caller places a message on a queue (Pub/Sub, Cloud Tasks) and returns immediately. The receiver processes it when ready. Best when: the operation is long-running (provisioning, document processing, ML inference). The producer and consumer have different availability requirements. Workload smoothing is needed (batch jobs that produce spikes).

Event-driven (publish-subscribe): Producer emits events to a topic without knowing who will consume them. Multiple consumers can subscribe. Best when: a state change in one system triggers actions in multiple other systems (AccountOpened → trigger KYC check, welcome email, CRM update, risk profile creation simultaneously). Avoids point-to-point integration explosion (N*(N-1) connections vs N connections to a shared event bus).

Hybrid pattern (most common in enterprise): The platform uses synchronous REST/gRPC for real-time user-facing APIs, Pub/Sub for domain event distribution, Cloud Tasks for reliable async processing of long operations, and Dataflow for high-throughput data pipeline integration. The art is knowing which pattern to apply to each integration — and being explicit about the coupling and failure mode implications.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank event-driven and API integration architecture is a concrete example of applying these patterns in a regulated environment.
EA15What metrics do you use to evaluate whether a software architecture is healthy and improving over time?
Architecture MetricsSenior
▼
Architecture health is difficult to measure directly — you measure its outcomes and indicators. Healthy architecture is not a destination; it is a continually maintained state.

Structural metrics (static analysis): (1) Cyclomatic complexity: average per function — above 15 is a warning sign (hard to test, likely to have bugs). (2) Coupling metrics: Afferent coupling (how many modules depend on this one — high = hard to change) and Efferent coupling (how many modules this one depends on — high = fragile). Stable Abstractions Principle: stable (high afferent coupling) components should be abstract; unstable components should be concrete. (3) Code coverage: not a quality metric on its own, but coverage gaps in critical paths (payment processing, auth) indicate untested risk.

Operational metrics (production behaviour): DORA metrics (deployment frequency, lead time, change failure rate, MTTR) — the best evidence-based indicators of architecture quality. Low deployment frequency often indicates architectural coupling (you can't change one service without changing others). High change failure rate indicates insufficient testing, tight coupling, or inadequate observability. High MTTR indicates poor observability and runbook quality.

Technical debt metrics: SonarQube debt ratio (time to fix all debt / total codebase development time). Trend over time: rising debt ratio is a warning sign. Debt distribution: is debt concentrated in critical components (high risk) or in peripheral utility code (lower risk)?

Architecture fitness functions: Automated tests that verify architectural characteristics: performance tests that fail if P99 latency exceeds the NFR threshold, security tests that flag new public endpoints not behind IAP, IaC drift detection that alerts on unmanaged resources. These encode architectural requirements as executable tests — not just documents.

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Scotiabank platform improvements — 40% fewer SCC findings, DORA metric improvements — are exactly the architectural health metrics this question is asking about.
EA16SCENARIO: You are brought in as Platform Architect on Day 1. Scotiabank's Digital Identity platform runs on Azure (AKS + Azure SQL + Azure AD B2C). Leadership wants it on GCP within 9 months. How do you approach the architecture design?
Scenario: Migration DesignSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's actual Scotiabank Azure-to-GCP migration experience

S — Situation: Scotiabank had a Digital Identity platform on Azure that handled authentication, authorisation, and identity lifecycle for retail banking customers. The platform processed millions of daily authentication events, was PCI-DSS in scope, and could not afford any customer-facing downtime during migration. Leadership set a 9-month target with a hard compliance audit milestone at the 12-month mark.

T — Task: Design a migration architecture that moved the platform to GCP while maintaining zero customer downtime, preserving PCI-DSS compliance throughout the transition, and setting up a foundation that was not just a lift-and-shift but a genuinely cloud-native GCP platform.

A — Action (Architecture Design):
Step 1 — Strangler Fig approach, not big-bang: I chose to run Azure and GCP in parallel rather than a cutover. This eliminated the "go / no-go" pressure that causes rushed migrations to fail. Azure stayed as the production system; GCP was built as the target state alongside it.

Step 2 — Foundation first: Before migrating any workload, I built the GCP foundation: Shared VPC with Cloud Interconnect to the corporate network (low-latency private connectivity, required for PCI-DSS), IAM structure (groups, service accounts, Workload Identity), org policies (approved regions, no public IPs, CMEK required), centralised logging (Cloud Audit Logs → Splunk), and Terraform IaC for every component — no manual resources.

Step 3 — Identity stack redesign: Azure AD B2C was replaced with Cloud Identity (SAML federation to Scotiabank's corporate LDAP). GKE replaced AKS — I chose GKE Standard for the identity workloads (needed Istio service mesh for mTLS, custom network policies for PCI-DSS scope). Cloud SQL (PostgreSQL) with HA replaced Azure SQL for the identity store. Secret Manager replaced Azure Key Vault.

Step 4 — Traffic migration via Cloud Load Balancer: I used a weighted traffic splitting pattern — 5% → 10% → 25% → 50% → 100% over 4 weeks. Istio's traffic management on GKE allowed per-request routing. The Azure platform handled fallback traffic until GCP was at 100%. Monitoring dashboards tracked error rates per platform in real-time — if GCP error rate exceeded Azure, we rolled back the traffic percentage immediately.

Step 5 — Compliance parallel track: I ran a compliance readiness assessment against PCI-DSS every 2 weeks. Each milestone gate required: all Org Policy constraints active, SCC findings below threshold, CMEK verified, mTLS end-to-end confirmed, audit log retention meeting 12-month requirement.

R — Result: Migration completed in 8 months (1 month ahead of target). Zero customer-facing downtime. The GCP platform passed the PCI-DSS audit at the 12-month mark with 40% fewer findings than the prior year's Azure audit. Cloud SQL costs 70% less than Azure SQL for equivalent workload. The Terraform IaC coverage was 94% from Day 1 — no manual configuration.

Key architectural principle demonstrated: A migration architecture must solve three problems simultaneously — functionality (does it do what it did before?), compliance (does it meet the regulated requirements?), and foundation (does it set up the right platform for the next 5 years, not just a working copy of the old one?).
EA17SCENARIO: A production Cloud SQL failover did not complete automatically during a regional incident, causing 35 minutes of downtime on the Digital Identity platform. Walk through your post-incident architecture redesign.
Scenario: Incident-Driven RedesignSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's Scotiabank production incident experience

S — Situation: During a zonal GCP incident, the Cloud SQL primary instance for our Digital Identity platform became unreachable. The configured High Availability (HA) standby should have promoted automatically within 60 seconds. Instead, the promotion failed silently — the standby instance stayed in standby state and the platform was unavailable for 35 minutes until we manually intervened.

T — Task: Conduct a proper post-incident analysis, identify the root cause, and redesign the Cloud SQL HA architecture to ensure this could never recur. Additionally, improve our detection capability so similar failures are caught before they become user-visible.

A — Action:
Root cause analysis (5-whys):
Why was the platform down for 35 minutes? → The Cloud SQL failover did not trigger automatically.
Why did failover not trigger? → The HA standby failed health checks but was not promoted.
Why did promotion fail? → The maintenance window was configured with a custom setting that conflicted with the automated failover trigger — a platform engineer had set a restrictive maintenance window that was being applied as a promotion window constraint incorrectly.
Why was this setting wrong? → It was set manually in the console, not via Terraform, after a legitimate maintenance change. It was not caught in our IaC drift detection.
Why was the wrong setting not caught? → We had no automated check comparing console-configured settings to Terraform state for Cloud SQL maintenance window attributes.

Architecture redesign (4 changes):
1. IaC drift enforcement: Added a nightly GitHub Actions workflow that runs terraform plan for all Cloud SQL instances and alerts on any non-empty plan. Non-empty plan = manual change not in Terraform = immediate Slack alert with the diff. This catches the root cause before it becomes an incident.

2. HA health check monitor: Added a Cloud Monitoring alerting policy specifically for Cloud SQL replication lag. If the standby's replication lag exceeds 30 seconds, PagerDuty fires — this gives the on-call engineer 30 seconds of warning before a failover attempt would see stale data. Previously we only alerted on CPU and connections, not replication health.

3. Automated failover test: Added a monthly scheduled test in a staging environment: Cloud Run Job triggers a manual Cloud SQL failover, waits 90 seconds, confirms the new primary is accepting connections, measures total downtime (must be <90 seconds). If test fails, blocks production deployments until investigated. Chaos engineering for database HA.

4. Connection retry in application: Analysed why the application did not recover automatically when the primary came back online. Found that the Cloud Run service was not using the Cloud SQL Auth Proxy's automatic reconnection. Updated the application connection string to use the Auth Proxy socket — the proxy handles reconnection transparently, reducing user-visible errors from ~35 minutes to 10-30 seconds (the actual promotion time) even without the architecture fix.

R — Result: Zero Cloud SQL HA incidents in the subsequent 18 months. Drift detection caught 3 separate manual console changes before they became risks. The monthly failover test revealed one additional misconfiguration (wrong replica type on a staging instance) that would have failed in a real incident. The Terraform coverage improvement from this incident contributed to the broader 94% IaC coverage achievement.

Architectural lesson: Database HA design is not just about configuring the right settings — it is about creating automated verification that those settings remain correct over time. Configuration drift is as dangerous as wrong initial configuration.
EA18SCENARIO: 8 application teams are deploying to GCP independently, creating 40+ projects with inconsistent naming, missing labels, no centralised audit logging, and several SCC Critical findings. You are asked to implement governance without halting team delivery. How do you architect the solution?
Scenario: Platform Governance at ScaleSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's Scotiabank platform governance work

S — Situation: After 18 months of rapid GCP expansion at Scotiabank, the platform had accumulated significant governance debt. 8 teams had provisioned 40+ projects, each in slightly different ways. Key issues: billing labels missing on 60% of resources (making cost attribution impossible), no centralised audit log aggregation (compliance risk), Security Command Center showing 14 Critical findings (open firewall rules, primitive IAM roles, public GCS buckets), and no organisation-level Org Policies (any team could create any resource in any region).

T — Task: Implement governance that addressed all these issues without halting team delivery. The constraint was explicit: we could not stop any team's release cadence. This ruled out a "freeze and fix" approach and required governance that could be applied incrementally and in parallel with ongoing work.

A — Action:
Governance strategy: guardrails, not gates
I split the remediation into three tracks that ran in parallel:

Track 1 — Preventative controls (Org Policies — 2 weeks):
Applied Org Policies at the folder level to block new non-compliant resources while allowing existing ones to continue operating: constraints/gcp.resourceLocations (only Canada/US regions going forward), constraints/iam.allowedPolicyMemberDomains (no external accounts), constraints/compute.vmExternalIpAccess = DENY (new VMs get no public IP). Critically, I did NOT apply these retrospectively to existing resources — only new resources were blocked. This meant teams could keep deploying while we cleaned up the existing estate.

Track 2 — Detective controls (Centralised logging + SCC — 3 weeks):
Created an aggregated Log Sink at the organisation level (--include-children) routing to a dedicated logging project → Splunk via Pub/Sub. This captured all 40+ projects' audit logs retroactively — no per-project configuration needed. Enabled SCC at organisation level. Exported all SCC findings to BigQuery for tracking. Created a finding remediation dashboard showing: finding count by project, by severity, by owner, and trend over time.

Track 3 — Remediation sprints (rolling, 6 weeks):
Ran 2-week remediation sprints with each team's platform lead. Week 1: Critical findings (public GCS buckets closed, open firewall rules restricted, primitive IAM roles replaced). Week 2: labelling (added required labels via Terraform import + apply, not manual — this put existing resources under IaC at the same time). I provided a Terraform remediation script for each team's projects — they ran it themselves with me reviewing the plan output. This built Terraform skill in the teams while fixing the issues.

Policy as code enforcement going forward:
Added OPA/Conftest policies to every team's CI/CD pipeline — plan files were validated before apply. Policies encoded: required labels, approved regions, no primitive roles, no public IPs. Failed policy check = failed PR. The first week had 23 policy failures across teams — all fixed within 2 days. After 4 weeks, zero new policy failures.

R — Result:
Critical SCC findings: 14 → 0 in 6 weeks. High findings: 38 → 4 (all accepted risk with documented waivers). Label coverage: 60% → 97%. Centralised logging active for all 40+ projects within 3 weeks. Zero production deployments blocked during the remediation (the preventative/detective/remediation split was key). Cost attribution accuracy improved immediately — finance team could now see per-team spend within days of completing the labelling.

This work contributed directly to the 40% reduction in audit findings at the next PCI-DSS audit and was cited by the external QSA as an example of effective cloud governance in a regulated environment.

Architectural principle: Governance at scale requires three simultaneous layers: prevent new problems (Org Policies), detect existing problems (SCC + centralised logging), and fix existing problems incrementally (remediation sprints). Never try to do all three sequentially — the existing problems will keep compounding while you work on prevention.
EA19SCENARIO: The CISO has mandated Zero Trust for all GCP workloads. You have 12 months and 25 services to migrate away from VPN-based network access. Design the rollout architecture.
Scenario: Zero-Trust Architecture RolloutSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's Scotiabank Zero Trust and mTLS security architecture work

S — Situation: Scotiabank's GCP platform was using a traditional VPN + firewall model for internal access — engineers used Cisco AnyConnect VPN to reach GCP management interfaces, and services communicated over an implicit trusted network. The CISO issued a mandate following a security assessment: migrate to Zero Trust within 12 months. 25 services needed to be migrated. The hardest constraint: the VPN could not simply be switched off — 300+ engineers depended on it for daily work.

T — Task: Design a Zero Trust architecture that replaced implicit network trust with identity + context-based access, without breaking developer productivity or causing service disruptions during the 12-month migration.

A — Action:
Zero Trust architecture design (3 pillars):
Pillar 1 — Human access via Identity-Aware Proxy (IAP):
IAP was deployed in front of all internal tooling (Argo-CD console, Grafana, internal developer portal, GKE API server access). Access is granted based on: identity (Cloud Identity managed account + SAML SSO), device state (Endpoint Verification agent confirms managed laptop, disk encrypted, OS up to date), and context (corporate IP or BeyondCorp Enterprise registered device). Engineers access these tools via their browser — no VPN needed. IAP handles the authentication and forwards verified requests. This was the fastest win — 8 internal tools migrated in 3 weeks.

Pillar 2 — Service-to-service via Workload Identity + mTLS:
Deployed Istio service mesh on the GKE clusters. mTLS is enforced between all pods — every service-to-service call is mutually authenticated using short-lived certificates issued by Istio's internal CA (which integrates with Certificate Authority Service on GCP). No service can call another service without a valid workload certificate. This eliminated implicit network trust between services. Rollout was per-namespace — we migrated one namespace at a time over 8 weeks, validating that all connections remained functional before enabling mTLS enforcement (as opposed to permissive mode).

Pillar 3 — Data plane via VPC Service Controls:
VPC Service Controls perimeter around PCI-DSS scope services (BigQuery for transaction data, Cloud SQL for the identity store, Secret Manager, KMS). Even with a valid identity, a service or user outside the perimeter cannot access data inside. This protects against compromised credentials — an attacker with a stolen service account key cannot exfiltrate data if they are calling from outside the perimeter.

Rollout sequencing (phased, not all-at-once):
Month 1-2: IAP for internal tools (low risk, high visibility win). Month 3-5: Istio mTLS in permissive mode (log but don't block) — analyse all service-to-service traffic to find undocumented dependencies before enforcing. Month 6-8: Istio mTLS enforcement by namespace (high risk → low risk order: non-prod first). Month 9-10: VPC Service Controls perimeter enabled. Month 11-12: Engineer access moved fully to IAP/BeyondCorp, VPN decommissioned for GCP access (VPN retained for non-GCP legacy systems).

Key risk mitigated — undocumented service dependencies:
Istio permissive mode + access log analysis revealed 3 services making direct database calls to other services' Cloud SQL instances — a serious architectural violation that would have been blocked by mTLS enforcement without advance notice. We fixed the coupling before enforcement, avoiding a production incident.

R — Result:
All 25 services migrated within 11 months. VPN access to GCP reduced by 95% (retained only for break-glass scenarios). mTLS coverage: 100% of service-to-service calls on GKE. 0 incidents caused by the migration (phased rollout with permissive mode was critical). SCC findings related to network access: reduced by 60%. The CISO formally recognised the Zero Trust rollout as a model for the bank's broader multi-cloud security programme.

Architectural principle: Zero Trust migrations fail when they are treated as security projects. They succeed when they are treated as developer experience projects — the goal is to make the secure path the easy path, not to add friction to existing workflows.
EA20SCENARIO: Your Terraform codebase has grown to 15 team-owned repositories, with no module standardisation, 3 different state management patterns, and your org has failed 2 internal IaC audits. Design a remediation and governance architecture.
Scenario: Multi-Team IaC GovernanceSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's Scotiabank Terraform governance and IaC standardisation work

S — Situation: After 2 years of teams independently writing Terraform at Scotiabank, the IaC estate had significant inconsistency: 15 repositories with 3 different state management patterns (some local state, some GCS with different bucket naming, some using Terraform Cloud), no shared module library (each team reimplementing VPC, Cloud SQL, and Cloud Run from scratch with subtle differences), and 2 failed internal audits (the audit team could not determine what resources were managed by Terraform vs manually provisioned for 40% of the estate). Leadership mandated a remediation plan before the next PCI-DSS audit window in 4 months.

T — Task: Design and implement an IaC governance architecture that standardised state management, created a shared module library, achieved near-100% IaC coverage, and was sustainable — not a one-time fix that teams would drift away from.

A — Action:
Architecture: 3-layer IaC governance model

Layer 1 — State standardisation (Month 1):
Defined the canonical state architecture: one GCS bucket per environment (dev/staging/prod) in a dedicated Terraform state project. Bucket naming: {org-prefix}-tfstate-{env}. Uniform IAM: only the corresponding environment's CI/CD service account gets objectAdmin; all others get objectViewer (for plan). CMEK encryption on all state buckets. Object versioning enabled. For teams with non-standard state: created migration scripts using terraform state mv (no resource destruction/recreation). 13 of 15 repos migrated in Month 1 without any infrastructure changes — only state file relocation.

Layer 2 — Shared module library (Month 2-3):
Created a terraform-modules monorepo managed by the platform team. Initial modules: terraform-google-gcp-project (standard project provisioning with all baseline config), terraform-google-gcp-network (Shared VPC attachment and subnet allocation), terraform-google-cloud-run (Cloud Run service with opinionated security defaults), terraform-google-cloud-sql (Cloud SQL with HA, CMEK, private IP, Auth Proxy config), terraform-google-iam (IAM bindings with validation). Each module follows semver and has a CHANGELOG. Team modules source pinned versions: source = "git::https://github.com/scotiabank/terraform-modules//modules/cloud-run?ref=v2.3.0". Modules are tested with Terratest before any tag release.

Layer 3 — Policy-as-code enforcement (Month 3-4):
OPA/Conftest policies added to every team's CI/CD pipeline. Policies: all resources must have required labels, no resources in unapproved regions, no public IPs, no primitive IAM roles, state bucket must match the canonical naming pattern. Pipeline fails on policy violation — no manual override without a platform team waiver. Added Infracost to all pipelines — cost delta visible on every PR before merge.

Drift detection (ongoing):
Nightly GitHub Actions workflow runs terraform plan for all repos. Non-empty plans publish to a central Slack channel with the diff. Platform team reviews weekly — patterns of frequent drift indicate a process that needs automation (common example: teams manually enabling GCP APIs that should be in their project module).

IaC coverage tracking:
Built a BigQuery query against Asset Inventory API export: count of resources not tagged with a Terraform resource ID label vs total resources. This gave a real-time IaC coverage percentage. Month 0: 62%. Target: 90% in 4 months. Published the metric on the internal platform dashboard — team leads competed to improve their numbers.

R — Result:
IaC coverage: 62% → 94% in 4 months. State management: 100% of repos using canonical GCS pattern. Shared module adoption: 11 of 15 teams using at least 3 platform modules (4 teams had custom requirements that were handled with approved deviations). Policy enforcement: 0 new non-compliant resources deployed in Month 4 (vs 23 per week in Month 1). Next PCI-DSS audit: the IaC audit finding was fully resolved — the auditor could trace every production resource to its Terraform source within 10 minutes using the label-based tracking.

Architectural principle: IaC governance is a product, not a mandate. The shared module library was the key — when using the platform module is easier than writing a custom one, teams choose the module. Governance enforcement is the backstop, not the primary mechanism.
EA21SCENARIO: Your GCP bill is $1.2M/month and growing 35% year-over-year. Finance has given you 90 days to reduce spend by 25% without reducing platform capability. Walk through your approach.
Scenario: FinOps TurnaroundSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's $500K annual savings work at Scotiabank

S — Situation: Scotiabank's GCP spend had grown from $800K to $1.2M/month over 18 months driven by platform expansion — new GKE clusters, expanded Cloud SQL footprint, and significant data engineering workloads in BigQuery. Finance issued a 90-day target: reduce monthly spend to $900K (25% reduction) while maintaining all production SLAs. The constraint was clear: no capability reduction, no production incidents caused by cost changes.

T — Task: Conduct a structured cost analysis, identify the highest-impact savings opportunities, implement changes safely (non-prod first), and establish ongoing FinOps practices to prevent cost creep from recurring.

A — Action:
Week 1-2 — Discovery and attribution:
Enabled detailed billing export to BigQuery and built a cost attribution dashboard in Looker Studio within 3 days. Dimensions: project, label (team, environment, service), SKU, region, time. This gave us a heatmap of where money was going. Top findings:
- 23% of spend was on non-production environments running 24/7 — dev/staging GKE clusters, dev Cloud SQL instances, and test BigQuery datasets with no cost controls.
- GKE node pools were sized for peak load but running at 18% average CPU utilisation — massive over-provisioning.
- Several Cloud SQL instances hadn't received queries in 30+ days but were still running at db-n1-standard-8 tier.
- BigQuery on-demand costs for analytics were $180K/month — the teams were using SELECT * on large tables without partitioning filters.

Week 3-4 — Quick wins (non-production):
Cloud Scheduler jobs to stop Cloud SQL instances (nights and weekends — 8pm to 8am weekdays, full weekends): 62% reduction in dev Cloud SQL costs. GKE node pool scale-down in non-prod (min nodes = 0 outside business hours, using Karpenter-style autoscaling). These two changes alone saved $85K/month within 30 days — zero production risk.

Month 2 — GKE right-sizing:
Deployed VPA in Off mode across all production GKE workloads for 2 weeks of recommendation collection. Exported recommendations to BigQuery. Ran a right-sizing sprint: reduced pod memory requests by 40% on average across 18 services (using P95 actual usage + 30% buffer as the new request). This reduced node count by 28% through improved bin-packing. Saved $95K/month. Validated by monitoring P99 OOM kills post-change (zero — confirmed recommendations were safe).

Month 2 — Committed Use Discounts:
Used GCP Recommender API to identify CUD recommendations. Analysed 90-day baseline compute usage — the stable floor was well below average usage. Purchased 1-year CUDs for: GKE node pool baseline vCPU/memory (37% discount), Cloud SQL baseline instance class (37% discount). CUD investment: committed to $580K/year of stable spend in exchange for $215K/year savings (37% on the committed portion). This was a one-time purchase with immediate monthly impact.

Month 3 — BigQuery governance:
Applied partitioning and clustering to the top 5 most-scanned BigQuery tables (identified from INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS analysis — these 5 tables represented 70% of BigQuery costs). Cost reduction: BigQuery from $180K/month to $72K/month — SELECT * scans replaced with partition-pruned queries. Implemented a BigQuery slots reservation (flat-rate) for the analytics team's predictable daily workload — cheaper than on-demand at their query volume. Required a 2-day training session for analytics engineers on partition-aware query writing.

R — Result:
Month 1: -$85K (non-prod shutdown). Month 2: -$95K (right-sizing) + -$18K/month (CUD savings). Month 3: -$108K (BigQuery optimisation). Total: $306K/month reduction = 25.5% against the $1.2M baseline — achieved in 87 days, 3 days ahead of the 90-day target. No production SLA breaches caused by cost changes.

Ongoing FinOps practice established: Weekly cost review with team leads (5 minutes on the dashboard). Budget alerts at 80% and 100% of monthly budget per team. Monthly right-sizing review using updated VPA recommendations. Annual CUD renewal review. Cost metric added to OKRs: cost per transaction (not absolute spend — so teams aren't penalised for business growth).

Architectural principle: Cost optimisation is an engineering discipline, not a finance exercise. The biggest lever was not negotiating contracts — it was applying basic engineering principles (right-sizing, partitioning, lifecycle management) to resources that had been provisioned once and never revisited.
EA22SCENARIO: You are asked to design the GCP architecture for a new payments product that must be PCI-DSS compliant from Day 1. Walk through your architecture decisions from first principles.
Scenario: Regulated Compliance ArchitectureSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's PCI-DSS compliance architecture work at Scotiabank

S — Situation: Scotiabank was launching a new real-time payments product (Interac e-Transfer modernisation) that would process cardholder data. The product had a 6-month MVP timeline and a hard regulatory requirement: PCI-DSS DSS 4.0 compliance before processing live payment data. The architecture needed to be compliant from Day 1, not retrofitted after build.

T — Task: Design the GCP architecture for the payments platform with PCI-DSS compliance as a first-class architectural constraint — not a checklist item after the fact.

A — Action (Architecture Design by PCI-DSS requirement family):

Scope reduction first (the most important decision):
I designed the architecture to minimise PCI-DSS scope before selecting any GCP services. The cardholder data (PAN, CVV, expiry) would be tokenised at the entry point — a dedicated tokenisation service in a separate GCP project. Once tokenised, all other services work with tokens, never raw PAN data. This reduced PCI-DSS scope from the entire platform to 1 service (the tokenisation service) + its data store. Every architectural decision then distinguishes between in-scope components and out-of-scope components.

Network segmentation (Req 1):
Separate GCP project for PCI-DSS in-scope workloads. Shared VPC subnet dedicated to in-scope resources with /24 IP range. Firewall rules: default deny all, explicit allow only for required ports from required sources. Cloud Armor WAF on all external ingress. No internet access for in-scope GKE pods (egress through Cloud NAT with allowlisted destinations only). VPC Service Controls perimeter around the in-scope project — data cannot be copied outside the perimeter even by authorised service accounts.

Data at rest (Req 3):
CMEK on all storage in scope: Cloud SQL instance (Cloud KMS key in the same project), GCS buckets holding card data (CMEK), Secret Manager secrets (CMEK). Key rotation: automatic annual rotation via Cloud KMS policy. Key material never leaves Google's HSM infrastructure (FIPS 140-2 Level 3). Sensitive authentication data (CVV) never stored after authorisation — architecture enforced by having the tokenisation service drop CVV before writing to Cloud SQL.

Data in transit (Req 4):
TLS 1.2+ enforced at all boundaries. Mutual TLS (mTLS) for all in-scope service-to-service communication via Istio. Cloud SQL connections via Cloud SQL Auth Proxy (encrypted + IAM authenticated — no database password in application config). External partner connections via Cloud Interconnect (private connectivity — no internet transit) with additional TLS.

Access control (Req 7, 8):
Least-privilege IAM — custom roles for each in-scope service account. No human access to in-scope Cloud SQL (only via break-glass PAM with time-limited access, full audit trail). Workload Identity for all GKE pods and Cloud Run services — no service account keys. MFA required for all personnel with access to in-scope environments (enforced via Cloud Identity + Access Context Manager).

Monitoring (Req 10):
All Cloud Audit Log types enabled for in-scope project. Log export to immutable GCS bucket (Object Lock — WORM compliance) + Splunk SIEM for real-time alerting. Log retention: 12 months hot, 7 years archival. Alerting: privilege escalation, access from unrecognised IPs, data export volume anomalies. Cloud SQL pgaudit for database-level access logging (who read which table, when).

Vulnerability management (Req 6):
Container Scanning in Artifact Registry — no image with Critical or High CVEs deployed to in-scope environment (Binary Authorization policy with Container Analysis attestation required). GKE Sandbox (gVisor) for in-scope workloads. GKE node auto-upgrade on the Stable release channel. Quarterly vulnerability assessment run against in-scope endpoints.

R — Result:
The payments platform was PCI-DSS certified on first assessment — no findings required remediation before certification. QSA cited the tokenisation architecture (scope reduction) as best-in-class for a cloud-native payment platform. The in-scope project contains only 3 services and 1 database — 90% of the platform is out of scope. This means compliance audits are fast and focused, and the majority of the platform can iterate quickly without compliance review for every change.

Architectural principle: PCI-DSS compliance is an architectural decision, not an operational one. The most important choice — tokenisation for scope reduction — must be made at the beginning. Made at the end, it requires redesigning the data flow of the entire system.
EA23SCENARIO: Engineers are spending 3 weeks waiting for a new GCP project to be provisioned manually. Design a self-service project vending machine that reduces this to under 10 minutes.
Scenario: Developer Platform Self-ServiceSenior
▼
STAR — Based on Nilanjan's platform automation and Terraform self-service work at Scotiabank

S — Situation: At Scotiabank, the GCP project provisioning process involved a ticketing workflow: engineer submits a JIRA ticket, platform team reviews, manually provisions the project via console, sets up billing, attaches to Shared VPC, configures IAM, enables APIs, and closes the ticket. Average end-to-end time: 3 weeks. This was the single most common complaint from development teams — it was a significant impediment to starting new initiatives.

T — Task: Design an automated, self-service project provisioning system that: reduces provisioning time from 3 weeks to under 10 minutes, maintains all governance controls (no shortcuts on IAM, labelling, logging, billing), provides audit trail for compliance, and is maintainable by a 3-person platform team without creating a new operational burden.

A — Action (Architecture Design):

Interface design — YAML in Git (not a portal):
I chose a GitOps-native interface: engineers submit a YAML file via Pull Request to a dedicated platform repository. YAML schema (minimal): team_name, environment (dev|staging|prod), data_classification (public|internal|confidential|restricted), billing_code, required_apis (list), optional services (cloud_sql: true/false, gke: true/false). This approach means: the request is version-controlled, auditable, peer-reviewable, and the interface is familiar to engineers who already use Git daily. No new portal to build or maintain.

Pipeline — GitHub Actions + Terraform:
PR opened → GitHub Actions triggers:
1. YAML schema validation (Pydantic model — catches missing required fields immediately)
2. Terraform plan: the YAML values are fed into a project factory Terraform module that generates a complete project configuration
3. Cost estimate (Infracost): estimated monthly cost of the requested project baseline posted as a PR comment
4. Policy check (OPA/Conftest): validates data classification, environment, billing code format
5. Human review gate: platform team reviews plan output (takes 5 minutes — verifying it looks correct, not doing manual work)
6. PR approval + merge → terraform apply triggered automatically

Terraform project factory module (what it creates):
GCP project in the correct folder (BU + environment determined by team YAML). Billing account link (billing_code maps to billing account in a configuration map). Required APIs enabled (compute, container, run, sqladmin — from the optional_services list). IAM bindings: {team_name}-devs group → custom developer role (run.developer, logging.viewer, monitoring.viewer, storage.objectViewer on team bucket). Platform admins group → admin role. Shared VPC service project attachment (correct subnet allocated from a subnet registry stored in Firestore). Audit log sink to centralised logging project. Budget alert at 80% of data-classification-based budget tier. Standard labels: team, environment, cost-centre (from billing_code), data-classification. CMEK if data_classification = restricted | confidential.

Idempotency and self-healing:
The module is fully idempotent — re-running for an existing project updates it to match the YAML without side effects. Teams can modify their YAML (add an API, change labels) and resubmit a PR. The nightly drift detection workflow runs terraform plan for every active project YAML — any deviation from the declared state triggers a PR automatically.

Rollout strategy:
Month 1: piloted with 3 volunteer teams (chose teams with active new projects — immediate value). Month 2: all new projects must use the vending machine. Month 3: existing projects gradually migrated (terraform import of existing projects into the factory module — this also fixed labelling and IAM debt on old projects as a side effect).

R — Result:
Time to provision a new project: 3 weeks → 8 minutes (2 minutes for engineer to write YAML + PR, 1 minute for automated pipeline + plan, 5 minutes for platform team plan review + approval). Platform team's project provisioning workload: eliminated (from 40% of weekly tickets to 0). Projects provisioned in first 6 months: 28 new projects — all compliant from Day 1. JIRA project provisioning ticket type: closed. The 3-person platform team redirected the freed capacity to higher-value work (module library, observability improvements).

Architectural principle: Platform self-service is not about building a portal — it is about making the correct path frictionless. A well-designed YAML schema and a reliable Terraform module is all the "portal" most engineering teams need.
EA24SCENARIO: The business wants 99.99% SLA for a new customer-facing API. Your estimated cost for active-active multi-region is $280K/month vs $85K for single-region with HA. How do you facilitate the architecture and business decision?
Scenario: Cost and Architecture Trade-offSenior
▼
STAR — Reflecting Nilanjan's stakeholder alignment and architecture decision-making experience at Scotiabank

S — Situation: A business product team at Scotiabank requested 99.99% SLA for a new customer onboarding API. I estimated two architectural options: (A) active-active multi-region with Cloud Spanner, Global LB, and Cloud Run in 2 regions = $280K/month; (B) single-region with Cloud SQL HA, regional LB, and Cloud Run with 99.9% SLA = $85K/month. The $195K/month difference required a business decision — this was not a technical decision alone.

T — Task: Structure the decision for the business stakeholders (Product VP, CFO, and the Risk team) so they could make an informed choice. My role was to give them the trade-off clearly — not to make the decision for them and not to let it become a purely technical debate where the business felt excluded.

A — Action:
Translating the decision into business language:
99.9% SLA = 8.7 hours of potential downtime per year. 99.99% SLA = 53 minutes per year. In business terms: if customer onboarding is unavailable for 8.7 hours per year (99.9%), what is the business cost? I worked with the product team to quantify this: average onboarding converts at 12%, the API handles 400 onboarding attempts per hour at peak. 8.7 hours of downtime = ~4,350 lost onboarding attempts × 12% conversion × average customer lifetime value. For this product, the analysis showed potential lost revenue of $180K-$250K per year from the downtime risk. The active-active architecture costs an additional $195K/month = $2.34M/year.

The decision frame (not my decision to make):
I presented 3 options with full transparency:
Option A: Active-active multi-region ($280K/month, 99.99% SLA, $2.34M more per year than Option B)
Option B: Single-region HA ($85K/month, 99.9% SLA, estimated $180-250K/year downtime risk)
Option C: Single-region HA with manual failover runbook ($95K/month, 99.9% SLA, RTO reduced to 15 minutes through pre-prepared runbooks and automation — not automatic, but dramatically faster than an unprepared team)

I also surfaced the nuances:
The 8.7 hours of potential downtime in 99.9% does not happen in a single event — it is the statistical allowance, not a guarantee. GCP's actual regional outages are far rarer and shorter. The last GCP northamerica-northeast1 incident was 23 minutes over 18 months. So the real question was: how much additional cost is the business willing to pay for a probabilistic improvement in a rare scenario?

My recommendation (clearly labelled as a recommendation, not a decision):
Option C — enhanced single-region with investment in runbook automation. Rationale: the cost difference between A and C ($185K/month = $2.22M/year) exceeds the risk of the downtime scenario by approximately 10x. The appropriate investment is in faster detection and faster manual failover, not automatic multi-region failover. I recommended revisiting in 12 months once the product's actual traffic and revenue profile was established — if the product grows to the point where the downtime risk exceeds $2M/year, the business case for active-active is strong.

R — Result:
The business chose Option C. The decision meeting took 45 minutes — structured, data-driven, no emotional debate. The product launched on budget. The enhanced runbook (Cloud Run health checks, Cloud SQL manual failover automation, Cloud Monitoring playbook) reduced our RTO from ~35 minutes (industry average for manual failover without preparation) to 8 minutes in a drill. At the 12-month review, the product had not experienced a significant availability incident, and the business reaffirmed the Option C choice.

Architectural principle: A 99.99% SLA is a business decision, not an engineering decision. Architects who mandate the most resilient architecture without a cost-benefit analysis are spending other people's money without accountability. The right role is to give decision-makers the full picture — cost, risk quantification, and a clear recommendation — then respect that the business owns the final call.
EA25SCENARIO: You need to integrate a modern GCP microservices platform with a 30-year-old COBOL mainframe that processes core banking transactions. Design the integration architecture.
Scenario: Legacy Integration PatternSenior
▼
STAR — Reflecting Nilanjan's enterprise integration and migration architecture experience

S — Situation: The Scotiabank Digital Transformation programme required the new GCP-based Digital Identity and payments platform to integrate with the core banking system — a 30-year-old IBM z/OS mainframe running COBOL batch programs. The mainframe processed: account balance lookups, transaction history, payment authorisation, and overnight settlement. It exposed a mix of CICS web services (SOAP/XML over HTTP), MQ Series message queues, and flat-file batch extracts dropped to a SFTP server. Real-time integration was critical for customer-facing services — a balance lookup on the mobile app needed to hit the mainframe within 500ms.

T — Task: Design an integration architecture that allowed the modern GCP microservices to communicate with the mainframe without coupling to its idiosyncratic interfaces, and that would remain stable as both the GCP platform and the mainframe (eventually decommissioned) evolved.

A — Action:
Core pattern: Anti-Corruption Layer (ACL)
I designed a dedicated Mainframe Integration Service (MIS) deployed as a Cloud Run service — the ACL between the GCP domain model and the mainframe's model. All GCP services call the MIS using modern REST/gRPC with clean domain models (AccountBalanceResponse, TransactionHistoryResponse). The MIS translates these to mainframe-native calls and back. No GCP service ever knows the mainframe exists — from their perspective, MIS is just another microservice.

Integration channels (one per mainframe interface type):
1. CICS SOAP web services (synchronous, real-time):
MIS makes HTTP/SOAP calls to the mainframe CICS endpoint over Cloud Interconnect (private — no internet). Timeout: 3 seconds (mainframe SLA). Circuit breaker (Istio): if mainframe CICS fails 5 calls in 30 seconds, MIS returns a cached response (last known good value + age indicator) rather than error — degraded mode UX is better than broken UX. Cache: Memorystore Redis with 60-second TTL for balance data, 5-minute TTL for account metadata.

2. MQ Series (async, event-driven):
IBM MQ Series queues on the mainframe received payment instructions and returned acknowledgements. I deployed IBM MQ Client on a dedicated GCE VM (not Cloud Run — MQ Client requires persistent TCP connections). A MQ-to-Pub/Sub bridge translated MQ messages to GCP Pub/Sub topics in both directions. MIS subscribed to Pub/Sub for async responses. This decoupled the GCP services from MQ completely — if we replace MQ with a modern queue, only the bridge changes, not MIS or the calling services.

3. Flat-file batch extracts (overnight ETL):
Mainframe dropped EBCDIC-encoded flat files to an SFTP server nightly. A Cloud Run Job (triggered by Cloud Scheduler) fetched the files, converted EBCDIC to UTF-8 (IBM iconv), parsed fixed-width fields to structured data, and loaded to BigQuery via Storage Write API. Datastream was not an option (no direct database access to the mainframe DB2). This channel served analytics and reporting — not real-time.

Resilience design:
MIS ran at 3 replicas minimum with circuit breakers on all mainframe calls. For each mainframe endpoint, I defined: healthy (normal operation), degraded (circuit breaker open, return cached), failed (circuit breaker open, no cache, return error). This meant the GCP platform's availability SLA was not dependent on the mainframe's availability SLA — a mainframe maintenance window no longer caused the mobile app to fail.

Migration path:
I designed the MIS interface contract to be mainframe-agnostic. When specific mainframe capabilities were modernised (e.g., balance inquiry moved to Cloud SQL AlloyDB), only the MIS implementation changed — the REST interface to calling services remained identical. This enabled a Strangler Fig pattern: migrate one capability at a time from mainframe to cloud, with MIS as the stable interface layer throughout.

R — Result:
Integration live in 4 months. Balance lookup P99 latency: 180ms (within the 500ms SLO) in normal operation, 15ms from cache when mainframe circuit breaker is open (degraded mode). Zero mainframe-related customer-facing incidents in 12 months post-launch (previous pattern: 2-3 per month during mainframe maintenance windows). Migration readiness: when 3 mainframe capabilities were subsequently modernised to GCP, the migration required no changes to any consuming service — only MIS implementation updates.

Architectural principle: The Anti-Corruption Layer is not a workaround for a bad integration target — it is the correct architectural pattern whenever two systems with fundamentally different models must communicate. The ACL protects your clean domain model from being contaminated by the mainframe's 30-year-old model, and it gives you a single, well-tested place to handle the translation.

📊 OLAP & OLTP Design

15 questions covering Data Warehouse · Star Schema · SCD · HTAP · Feature Store · Real-time Analytics · Data Vault · BigQuery Optimisation

Click any question to expand the model answer. Senior questions are the differentiating ones that set Platform Architect candidates apart.
