DM1Explain the fundamental differences between OLTP and OLAP systems and give GCP examples of each.
OLTP vs OLAP FundamentalsCore
▼
OLTP (Online Transaction Processing): Handles high volumes of short, atomic transactions — inserts, updates, deletes on individual records. Characteristics: row-oriented storage, normalised schema (3NF), low latency (<100ms per transaction), high concurrency (thousands of simultaneous users), ACID guarantees (Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability). Workload pattern: many concurrent small transactions, read + write mix, real-time.

OLAP (Online Analytical Processing): Handles complex analytical queries over large datasets — aggregations, joins, scans. Characteristics: columnar storage, denormalised schema (star/snowflake), high throughput on large scans, lower concurrency, read-heavy workload. Workload pattern: few concurrent complex queries, read-dominant, batch or near-real-time.

GCP examples by system type:
OLTP → Cloud SQL (PostgreSQL, MySQL, SQL Server) for web application databases, transactional backends, small-to-medium datasets. AlloyDB for high-throughput OLTP + analytics queries on the same dataset (hybrid). Cloud Spanner for globally distributed, strongly consistent OLTP at horizontal scale (financial ledgers, global inventory).

OLAP → BigQuery for petabyte-scale analytics, data warehouse, BI reporting. Looker as the semantic layer and visualisation on top of BigQuery. BigQuery BI Engine for sub-second dashboard queries on frequently-accessed datasets.

Hybrid (HTAP — Hybrid Transactional/Analytical Processing): AlloyDB with its columnar engine handles both OLTP and moderate OLAP on the same dataset — no need to ETL to a separate analytical system for operational analytics. Cloud Spanner's Analytics APIs provide limited analytics on Spanner data without impacting OLTP performance.
DM2How do you design a modern cloud data warehouse architecture on GCP for a financial institution?
Data Warehouse ArchitectureSenior
▼
A modern financial data warehouse on GCP uses BigQuery as the analytical engine, with a layered transformation architecture and strong governance.

Ingestion layer: Batch: Cloud Storage (Parquet/Avro files from operational systems) → BigQuery load jobs (most cost-efficient). Streaming: Pub/Sub → Dataflow → BigQuery Storage Write API (near-real-time, exactly-once). CDC: Cloud SQL / on-premises DB → Datastream → BigQuery (real-time database replication). Third-party SaaS: BigQuery Data Transfer Service (Salesforce, Google Ads, etc.) — no code required.

Transformation layers (dbt-managed): Raw/Landing zone: data as-loaded, no transformation, immutable. For compliance: WORM policy on GCS source files. Staging/Bronze: light cleaning, standard data types, basic validation. Intermediate/Silver: business logic applied, joins between domains, deduplication. Mart/Gold: aggregated, presentation-ready tables for BI. Semantic layer: Looker LookML or BigQuery BI Engine defines business metrics (ARR, CAC, NPS) consistently across all reports.

Schema design for FinServ: Star schema for most fact tables — simplifies queries, improves readability. Fact tables: transactions, payments, positions, risk events. Dimension tables: customers, accounts, products, dates, branches. Slowly Changing Dimensions (SCD Type 2) for customer data — preserve history of changes (address, status, risk rating) for regulatory reporting.

Governance layer: Policy tags (Data Catalog) for PII and sensitive financial fields. Column-level masking for analysts without Fine-Grained Reader role. Row Access Policies for regional data partitioning. Dataplex for data quality checks and lineage. dbt tests for freshness, uniqueness, and referential integrity.

Cost architecture: Partition all fact tables by date. Cluster by account_id or customer_id. Use materialised views for frequently-run dashboard queries. BigQuery editions (flat-rate) once query volume exceeds $10K/month on-demand.
DM3How do you design a normalised OLTP schema for a financial payments system and what are the key considerations?
OLTP Schema DesignIntermediate
▼
Normalisation goals: Eliminate data redundancy (each piece of data stored once), ensure data integrity (updates in one place, no inconsistencies), support ACID transactions, minimise write amplification.

Third Normal Form (3NF) schema for a payments system:
customers (customer_id PK, name, email, created_at, kyc_status)
accounts (account_id PK, customer_id FK, currency, type, status, balance, opened_at)
payment_methods (method_id PK, account_id FK, type, last_four, expiry_date, is_default)
payments (payment_id PK, from_account_id FK, to_account_id FK, method_id FK, amount, currency, status, initiated_at, completed_at)
payment_events (event_id PK, payment_id FK, event_type, timestamp, actor, metadata JSONB)

Key design decisions:
(1) Separation of payment and events: the payments table holds current state; payment_events holds the complete lifecycle history — this supports both real-time queries (get payment status) and audit queries (what happened to payment P at time T?) without conflating them. (2) Status vs event sourcing: for most OLTP systems, store current status + event log (not full event sourcing which adds complexity). (3) JSONB for metadata: flexible key-value store for payment metadata (risk score, device fingerprint, geolocation) — avoids schema churn for evolving metadata without sacrificing query capability. (4) Optimistic locking: add version column to accounts table — update only succeeds if version matches, preventing lost updates in concurrent balance updates. (5) Soft deletes: financial records are never hard-deleted — add deleted_at timestamp and filter WHERE deleted_at IS NULL in normal queries.

Indexing strategy: Primary key index on all tables. Foreign key indexes (account_id on payments). Status + date composite index for operational queries (WHERE status = 'PENDING' AND initiated_at > NOW() - INTERVAL '1 hour'). Partial index for common filtered queries (WHERE status = 'PENDING') to avoid scanning the full table.
DM4Compare the star schema and snowflake schema for an analytical data model — when does each make sense?
Star Schema vs SnowflakeIntermediate
▼
Both schemas are dimensional modelling patterns for analytical systems, representing facts and dimensions.

Star Schema: A central fact table surrounded by denormalised dimension tables. Each dimension is a single table, even if it has hierarchical attributes. Example: a fact_transactions table with dimension tables dim_customer (includes region, country, city directly), dim_product, dim_date. Simple join pattern: fact + dimensions = one join per dimension. Query performance is excellent — few joins, large dimension tables fully fit in memory for hash joins.

Snowflake Schema: Dimension tables are further normalised — a snowflake dimension has child tables. dim_customer → dim_region → dim_country. Reduces storage redundancy (country code not repeated in every customer row). More complex queries (more joins). Slightly slower than star schema but storage-efficient for large, highly repetitive dimensions.

Practical recommendation for BigQuery: Always use star schema. Storage is cheap ($20/TB/month) — the cost of dimension denormalisation is negligible. The query complexity and performance degradation of snowflake schema is not worth the storage savings in a columnar analytical database. BigQuery's columnar engine only scans the columns you select — even a wide denormalised dimension table costs little if you only select a few fields.

NESTED RECORDS (BigQuery-specific): A third option unique to columnar databases — nest repeating data directly in the fact table as STRUCTs and ARRAYs. Example: a transaction row contains a STRUCT for merchant_info instead of a join to a merchant dimension. For 1-to-1 relationships, this eliminates joins entirely. For 1-to-many, ARRAYs allow nested records. BigQuery processes nested records without the join overhead — often the highest-performance option for BigQuery-specific models.
DM5Explain Slowly Changing Dimensions (SCD) types and which you would use for customer data in a regulated financial environment.
Slowly Changing DimensionsSenior
▼
Slowly Changing Dimensions (SCDs) handle how dimension attributes change over time — a customer changes address, or an account changes status. Choosing the right SCD type balances historical accuracy, query simplicity, and regulatory compliance.

SCD Type 1 — Overwrite: Old value is replaced by the new value. No history preserved. Use for: corrections (fixing a spelling error), non-meaningful attribute changes (internal reference codes). Not appropriate for regulatory data — if a regulator asks what a customer's address was on a specific date, Type 1 cannot answer it.

SCD Type 2 — Add row (recommended for FinServ): When an attribute changes, a new row is added with the new value and a date range (effective_from, effective_to). A surrogate key distinguishes the versions. The current row has effective_to = NULL or a far-future date (9999-12-31). Full history preserved — temporal queries are precise: "what was the customer's risk rating on 2022-06-15?" Regulators can always ask this. Fact table foreign keys to dimension surrogate keys — each transaction points to the customer version active at transaction time.

SCD Type 3 — Add column: Add a "previous_value" column alongside the "current_value" column. Only the last change is preserved. Limited use — only when tracking one specific change is meaningful (e.g., "previous address" for mail forwarding). Not suitable for tracking the full history of a financial attribute.

SCD Type 6 (Hybrid 1+2+3): Combines Type 2 (new row on change) with a current_flag and current_value column for easy querying without date range joins. Useful for high-query-volume scenarios where Type 2 join performance is a concern.

Implementation in BigQuery: MERGE statement for SCD Type 2 updates (expire old row, insert new row atomically). Scheduled dbt model runs the MERGE daily. Filter patterns: WHERE effective_to IS NULL for current records, WHERE effective_from <= '2022-06-15' AND (effective_to > '2022-06-15' OR effective_to IS NULL) for point-in-time queries.
DM6What is HTAP (Hybrid Transactional/Analytical Processing) and when does it make sense vs separate OLTP and OLAP systems?
HTAP PatternSenior
▼
HTAP combines transactional and analytical workloads in a single system, eliminating the latency and complexity of ETL pipelines between OLTP and OLAP.

Traditional architecture (OLTP → ETL → OLAP): OLTP system handles transactions. Nightly or hourly ETL extracts data and loads to data warehouse. Analytical queries run on the warehouse. Drawbacks: stale data (hours of lag), ETL complexity and fragility, operational overhead of two systems, data synchronisation bugs.

HTAP architecture: A single system handles both transactional writes and analytical reads simultaneously, with no ETL. Row-oriented storage for transactional access patterns, columnar storage for analytical access — maintained in sync internally.

GCP HTAP options:
(1) AlloyDB for PostgreSQL: the strongest GCP HTAP option. Maintains a live columnar engine (AlloyDB Columnar Engine) automatically synchronised with the transactional data. Analytical queries on the same database return real-time results without impacting OLTP performance. The columnar engine is used automatically when the query planner determines it's faster. Best for: operational analytics — dashboards showing real-time account activity, fraud detection queries on live data.
(2) Cloud Spanner + Analytics API: Spanner provides limited analytical capabilities via its Analytics API (Spanner to BigQuery federation). For complex analytics, this is a stepping stone, not a full replacement for BigQuery.
(3) BigQuery + Streaming: not true HTAP, but close. Write events to BigQuery via Storage Write API (committed stream) — data visible within seconds. For near-real-time (seconds latency), this approximates HTAP at BigQuery scale.

When to use HTAP vs separate systems: Use HTAP when: data freshness requirement <1 minute, dataset fits in one database, operational analytics use case. Use separate OLAP when: historical analytics on years of data, petabyte-scale, complex ML/data science workloads, multiple data sources combined.
DM7How do you design a data model for a machine learning feature store that serves both training and real-time inference?
Data Modelling for MLSenior
▼
A feature store solves the training-serving skew problem — the same feature computation used during model training must be reproduced exactly at inference time. If training uses one formula and inference uses another, model performance degrades silently.

Feature store components:
(1) Feature registry: metadata catalogue of all defined features — name, data type, description, owner, computation logic, entity it belongs to (customer, transaction, device). Implemented in Data Catalog or a custom Firestore document per feature. (2) Offline store: historical feature values for model training. BigQuery is the natural choice — queryable, partitioned by date, supports point-in-time lookups. (3) Online store: current feature values for real-time inference. Bigtable or Vertex AI Feature Store (Bigtable-backed). Sub-millisecond read latency. (4) Materialisation pipeline: Dataflow job that computes features from raw events and writes to both offline (BigQuery) and online (Bigtable) stores.

Point-in-time correctness (training data generation): When creating training data, features must be retrieved as they were at the time of the training event — not the current values. A model trained to detect fraud at time T must use the feature values that were available at T, not today's values. This requires querying the offline store with an AS_OF timestamp. Vertex AI Feature Store handles this automatically; raw BigQuery requires a time-range query against the event history table.

Schema design for Bigtable (online store): Row key = entity_id (customer_id, device_fingerprint). Column families by feature group (realtime_features, daily_features, model_features). Columns = individual feature names (tx_velocity_1h, avg_amount_30d, risk_score_v3). TTL on column families to automatically expire stale features.

Feature serving for inference: Cloud Run inference service calls Bigtable for online features (<5ms). Joins with real-time transaction features computed in the service. Calls Vertex AI endpoint with the assembled feature vector.
DM8How do you optimise complex analytical queries in BigQuery — walk through your diagnostic and optimisation approach.
OLAP Query OptimisationSenior
▼
Query optimisation in BigQuery follows a systematic diagnosis before intervention — changing schema or query structure without understanding the problem wastes effort and can make things worse.

Step 1 — Read the execution plan: Click "Execution Details" in the BigQuery console after running the query. The plan shows stages (read, compute, write) with: input rows, output rows, slot time consumed, bytes shuffled. A stage with output rows ≈ input rows means no filtering happened there — a filter you expected to prune data is not being applied. A high bytes-shuffled stage indicates a JOIN or GROUP BY distributing data across workers.

Step 2 — Bytes scanned vs expected: If INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS shows bytes_processed ≈ total table size, partitioning is not being applied. Check: is the WHERE clause filtering on the partition column directly? EXTRACT(YEAR FROM timestamp) = 2024 does NOT prune partitions. DATE(timestamp) = '2024-01-15' DOES prune. Use _PARTITIONDATE or the partition column name directly.

Step 3 — Identify common patterns:
(a) Partition/cluster pruning failure: fix the WHERE clause to use the partition/cluster column directly.
(b) SELECT *: replace with explicit column list — BigQuery scans only selected columns.
(c) JOIN skew: one join key has disproportionately many rows (e.g., NULL or 'UNKNOWN' for 30% of rows). Fix: pre-filter NULL join keys before the JOIN. Or split into two queries (NULL case + non-NULL case).
(d) Repeated subquery: the same subquery referenced multiple times. Replace with a CTE (WITH clause) — BigQuery may cache the CTE result.
(e) DISTINCT on large datasets: if approximate counts are acceptable, APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT is 100x faster than COUNT(DISTINCT ...).
(f) Cross-join: a JOIN without ON clause — cartesian product, always catastrophic. Check for missing join conditions.

Step 4 — Schema changes: If query optimisation is insufficient, consider: adding partitioning (requires table recreation or CTAS), adding a clustering column, creating a materialised view for a frequently repeated query pattern, or pre-aggregating data in a scheduled dbt model.
DM9What is the Lakehouse pattern and how would you design one on GCP?
Data Lakehouse ArchitectureSenior
▼
The Lakehouse combines the cost-efficiency and flexibility of a data lake (raw data storage) with the query performance and governance of a data warehouse, in a single architecture.

The problem it solves: Traditional two-tier architecture (data lake + data warehouse) duplicates data (high storage cost), has latency between the two tiers (nightly ETL), and creates governance fragmentation (data quality rules in the warehouse don't cover the lake). The Lakehouse collapses this into one system.

Core components:
(1) Open table format: Delta Lake, Apache Iceberg, or Apache Hudi — layered over GCS raw files. Provides ACID transactions on data lake files, schema evolution, time travel (query data as it was on a specific date), partition pruning. BigQuery supports querying Iceberg tables directly (BigLake). This is the "warehouse-like" capability applied to lake storage.
(2) Unified storage: GCS as the single storage tier. Raw data (ingestion layer), refined data (transformation layer), and consumption data (serving layer) are all GCS files — just in different buckets/prefixes with different access controls and lifecycle policies.
(3) BigLake: Google's Lakehouse implementation. Creates an abstraction layer over GCS data (Parquet, Iceberg, Delta) that exposes it as BigQuery-querable tables. Supports: fine-grained access controls (column-level, row-level) on GCS files, consistent data governance across BigQuery native tables and GCS files.
(4) Dataform or dbt: transformation orchestration across both the lake and warehouse layers.

GCP Lakehouse reference architecture: Raw zone (GCS, any format) → Bronze zone (GCS, Parquet/Iceberg, cleaned) → Silver zone (GCS, Parquet/Iceberg, business logic applied) → Gold zone (BigQuery native tables, presentation-ready). All zones queryable via BigQuery (native tables directly, GCS files via BigLake external tables). Single access control policy via Data Catalog.

When to choose Lakehouse over traditional DW: Multi-format data (images, JSON, Parquet, CSV all in one architecture). ML workloads needing raw data access. Large volumes where BigQuery native storage cost is prohibitive. Teams needing Python/Spark access alongside SQL analytics.
DM10How do you design a real-time analytics architecture on GCP with sub-minute data freshness?
Real-Time Analytics ArchitectureSenior
▼
Real-time analytics means decisions and dashboards are driven by data that is seconds-to-minutes old, not hours old. This requires fundamentally different architecture from batch analytics.

Ingestion pattern: Events produced → Pub/Sub (ingestion buffer, <10ms latency) → Dataflow streaming job (transformation, enrichment) → BigQuery Storage Write API committed stream (visible within seconds). End-to-end latency: typically 30-90 seconds from event to queryable in BigQuery.

BigQuery streaming considerations: Streaming data lands in a streaming buffer before being written to persistent storage. Data in the streaming buffer is immediately queryable but not available for TABLE_DATE_RANGE queries. Budget: Storage Write API streaming costs ~$0.01/GB written — much cheaper than the legacy streaming insertAll API ($0.01/200MB = ~$0.05/GB).

Real-time aggregation (sub-second dashboards): BigQuery alone may not be fast enough for true real-time dashboards with many concurrent users querying fresh data. Strategies: (1) BigQuery BI Engine reservation — caches hot datasets in memory, provides sub-second latency for dashboard queries. (2) Materialised views with real-time refresh — BigQuery incrementally refreshes materialised views as streaming data arrives. (3) Looker's Aggregate Awareness — automatically routes queries to the fastest materialised aggregate. (4) Redis time-series cache — Dataflow writes pre-aggregated metrics to Memorystore Redis; dashboards read from Redis for near-zero latency counters (transactions per second, current queue depth).

Lambda vs Kappa architecture: Lambda: separate batch (accurate, slow) and streaming (fast, approximate) pipelines, merge results. Kappa: single streaming pipeline handles all data — simpler operationally, but requires careful design to handle late data and corrections. With Dataflow's unified batch/stream model (same Beam code runs both ways), Lambda vs Kappa is less of a binary choice — use Kappa for simplicity, fall back to batch reprocessing for corrections.
DM11How do you design data quality checks into a production data pipeline for a financial institution?
Data Quality in PipelinesSenior
▼
Financial data quality failures have direct business consequences — incorrect balances, regulatory reporting errors, fraud model trained on bad data. Quality must be designed in, not added as an afterthought.

Quality dimensions (DAMA framework): Completeness (are required fields populated?), Accuracy (do values match the source?), Consistency (same values across systems?), Timeliness (how fresh is the data?), Uniqueness (no duplicate records?), Validity (values within allowed range/format?).

Quality check layers:
(1) Source validation (ingestion gate): validate schema and business rules on every incoming record before it enters the pipeline. Pub/Sub schema registry enforces Avro/Protobuf schema on publish. Invalid records → quarantine topic with failure reason, not silently dropped. Metric: quarantine rate by source system and error code — spike indicates upstream change.
(2) Pipeline checks (Dataflow transforms): range checks (amount > 0 and < 10M), referential integrity (account_id exists in accounts table — side input lookup), format validation (IBAN format, currency code in ISO list). Failed records → quality_failures BigQuery table with rule_id, record_id, failure_reason, pipeline_run_id.
(3) Post-load checks (dbt tests or Dataplex): Completeness: no NULLs in mandatory columns. Uniqueness: no duplicate payment_id in fact_payments. Referential integrity: all customer_id in fact_transactions exist in dim_customer. Freshness: fact_transactions updated within the last 2 hours (freshness test). Volume anomaly: transaction count within 20% of 7-day average (statistical check).
(4) Business rule checks: total debits = total credits for a given settlement date (balance reconciliation). Net position per account matches sum of transaction amounts.

Alerting: Failed dbt tests → Slack alert to data engineering team. Quarantine rate spike → PagerDuty. Data freshness failure → critical alert (SLA breach risk). All quality check results published to a Data Quality Dashboard in Looker.
DM12Explain columnar vs row-oriented storage and why columnar storage is optimal for analytical workloads.
Columnar vs Row StorageIntermediate
▼
Storage orientation determines how data is physically laid out on disk (or in memory), which directly determines query performance for different access patterns.

Row-oriented storage (OLTP): All columns of a row are stored contiguously: [cust1, John, Smith, London, 2020] [cust2, Jane, Doe, Toronto, 2021]. When you need cust1's complete record (all columns), a single disk read retrieves everything. Fast for: full-record lookups (SELECT * WHERE id = X), inserts and updates of individual rows, OLTP patterns with many small reads and writes.

Columnar storage (OLAP): Each column is stored separately: [cust1, cust2, cust3] [John, Jane, Bob] [Smith, Doe, Jones] [London, Toronto, Sydney]. When you need all customer cities (SELECT city FROM customers), only the city column is read from disk — all other columns are skipped entirely. For a table with 1000 columns and a query selecting 5 columns, columnar storage reads 0.5% of the data that row storage would read.

Why columnar wins for analytics: (1) Column projection: analytical queries typically touch 5-20% of available columns. Row storage reads all columns unnecessarily. (2) Compression efficiency: a column contains values of the same type (and often similar values — e.g., a status column with 3 distinct values repeated millions of times). Run-length encoding and dictionary encoding achieve 10-100x compression on columns vs 2-5x on rows. (3) Vectorised execution: modern CPUs can apply the same operation to an entire column vector in a single instruction (SIMD). BigQuery's Dremel engine and DuckDB exploit this for massive parallelism. (4) Late materialisation: operate on compressed column data before decompressing — filters applied to compressed data skip irrelevant rows without decoding.

BigQuery implementation: BigQuery uses Capacitor, its proprietary columnar format. Partitioning provides horizontal partitioning (which rows to read). Clustering provides column-level ordering (which blocks within a column to read). Combined, they give both row-level and column-level pruning.
DM13What is Data Vault 2.0 and when would you recommend it over a traditional Kimball dimensional model?
Data Vault MethodologySenior
▼
Data Vault 2.0 is an agile data warehousing methodology designed for auditability, scalability, and resilience to source system changes — characteristics that make it attractive for regulated financial environments.

Data Vault structure:
(1) Hubs: contain the business keys of core entities (customer_id, account_id, transaction_id). Append-only — a business key is loaded once and never changes. Hubs are the stable anchor of the model.
(2) Links: record relationships between hubs (which customer owns which account, which transaction is associated with which payment method). Append-only. Relationships are captured as they are observed — late-arriving links are simply appended.
(3) Satellites: store the descriptive attributes (name, address, status, amount) along with the source system, load timestamp, and hash diff. Multiple satellites per hub are common — each source system contributing data gets its own satellite. Full history preserved — never overwrite, only insert new rows.

Advantages for financial services: Complete, unaltered audit trail — every row has source system, load timestamp, and hash of the original record. Agile to source system changes — adding a new source system adds a new satellite; it doesn't break existing queries. Natural parallel loading (hubs, links, satellites load independently). Multi-source integration without loss of provenance.

When Data Vault beats Kimball: Multiple source systems with conflicting or overlapping data. Strong audit and regulatory requirements (PCI-DSS, SOX, BCBS 239 for banking). Frequent source system changes. Large team (Data Vault's modular structure allows parallel development).

When Kimball beats Data Vault: Single source system, simpler business rules. Smaller team / faster time-to-insights. Users query the warehouse directly (Data Vault raw vault is not analyst-friendly — you build a Business Vault / Information Mart on top). When query simplicity matters more than model flexibility.

On GCP: Data Vault raw vault in BigQuery (hubs/links/satellites as separate tables). Business Vault and marts as dbt models. Load scripts automated with dbt or custom Cloud Run jobs. Hash computation in BigQuery SQL (SHA256 or MD5 on concatenated business key + satellite attributes).
DM14How do you design a partitioning strategy for a large financial transactions table in BigQuery, and what are the operational implications?
Partitioning StrategiesSenior
▼
Partitioning is the single most impactful performance and cost optimisation in BigQuery. The goal is to ensure that common queries scan only the partitions they need, not the entire table.

Partition types:
(1) Ingestion-time partitioning (_PARTITIONTIME): automatically partitions by load date. Simple to set up, but queries must filter on _PARTITIONTIME — confusing when rows loaded on one date represent events from a different date.
(2) DATE/TIMESTAMP column partitioning: partition on a column in the data — transaction_date, event_timestamp. Queries filtering on the partition column will prune. Best choice for most time-series data — the filter column aligns with natural query patterns.
(3) INTEGER RANGE partitioning: for account_id ranges, sequential IDs. Use when natural date ranges don't align with access patterns (e.g., partitioning by account shard for customer-specific queries).

Operational implications:
Partition limit: BigQuery supports up to 10,000 partitions per table. Daily partitioning on 30 years of data = 10,950 partitions — hit the limit. Solution: use monthly partitioning for historical data, daily for recent data. Or use partition expiry (expire partitions older than X days, archive to GCS).

Partition pruning requirements: Queries MUST filter on the partition column with a literal value or parameter for pruning to activate. Dynamic filters (WHERE transaction_date = (SELECT MAX(transaction_date) FROM ...)) may not always prune — use WITH clause to materialise the filter value first.

Clustering complements partitioning: After partitioning by transaction_date, cluster by (account_id, transaction_type). Queries filtering on account_id within a date partition scan only the relevant cluster blocks — typically 3-10x additional data reduction beyond partitioning alone.

Cost monitoring: INFORMATION_SCHEMA.PARTITIONS view shows partition size and row count. Identify unexpectedly large partitions (data skew) — may indicate a date conversion bug loading all rows to the same partition.
DM15How do you plan and execute a database migration from an on-premises RDBMS to GCP with minimal downtime for a production financial system?
Database Migration StrategySenior
▼
Database migration in financial services is high-risk — downtime or data loss has direct business and regulatory consequences. The strategy must prioritise safety over speed.

Phase 1 — Assessment (2-4 weeks): Schema audit: unsupported data types, stored procedures, triggers, functions that must be re-implemented in Cloud SQL/AlloyDB. Row count and data volume (determines migration duration). Latency requirements (determines acceptable cutover window). Compliance requirements (data residency, encryption at rest/transit, audit logging).

Phase 2 — Parallel run setup (2-4 weeks): Provision the target Cloud SQL/AlloyDB instance. Use Database Migration Service (DMS) for continuous replication from source → target. DMS uses Change Data Capture (CDC) to keep the target in sync with ongoing source transactions. Run validation queries on both source and target to verify data consistency (row counts, checksums on key tables, sample record comparison).

Phase 3 — Shadow mode (1-2 weeks): Application writes go to both source and target simultaneously (dual-write via application layer or replication). Application reads come from the source — target is a shadow. Compare query results between source and target for consistency. This validates that the target handles production workload without impacting production service.

Phase 4 — Cutover (maintenance window, minutes to hours): Stop writes to source. Verify DMS replication lag is zero. Final consistency check (row count, last transaction ID). Switch application connection string to Cloud SQL target. Verify application functionality. Remove source writes as a fallback.

Phase 5 — Decommission (2-4 weeks after cutover): Keep source running in read-only mode for 2 weeks — emergency rollback path. After no rollback incidents, decommission source system.

Tools on GCP: Database Migration Service (DMS) for MySQL, PostgreSQL, and Oracle migrations. Striim as an alternative for complex heterogeneous migrations. Schema conversion: pgloader (MySQL → PostgreSQL), AWS Schema Conversion Tool (Oracle → PostgreSQL — also works for GCP).

[USE YOUR EXPERIENCE]: Your Azure-to-GCP migration at Scotiabank — describe how you handled the database migration phase, what tools you used, and how you managed the cutover.
